The Universal Acceptance of Qiyas and the Zahiri Rejection
Describes the universal acceptance of qiyas and the isolated opposition of the Zahiris.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the evidence for qiyas as a source of Islamic law and explain why the Zahiri rejection is considered invalid in the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Qiyas is essential to Islamic law, accepted by jurists since the Companions; the Zahiri rejection is an outlier without weight.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to Islamic jurisprudence addresses the foundational role of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Islamic law. It highlights the universal acceptance of qiyas by the majority of scholars throughout Islamic history, and dismisses the Zahiri school's rejection as contrary to the practical necessities of legal development. As a Hanafi student, understanding this consensus reinforces qiyas as a primary source of law alongside Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim of comprehensiveness
Note the opening statement: Islamic law is broad enough to cover all past and future events. This sets the stage for why qiyas is necessary—to derive rulings for new cases not explicitly covered in texts.
This is a classical apologetic for qiyas as a tool for legal extension.
2
Identify the outlier group
The passage mentions the Zahiris, who relied only on literal textual meanings and rejected qiyas. Understand that their position was a minority view.
The Zahiri school, founded by Dawud al-Zahiri, is often contrasted with the four Sunni schools.
3
Analyze the proof of consensus
Al-Muzani's statement is cited as evidence that jurists from the Prophet's time to the present have used qiyas. This is an argument from ijma (consensus).
The phrase 'the equivalent of truth is truth, the equivalent of falsehood is falsehood' encapsulates the logical basis of qiyas.
4
Understand the definition of qiyas
The passage defines qiyas as 'tashbih bi'l-umur wa al-tamthil 'alayha'—comparing things and analogizing them. This is the classical Hanafi understanding.
In Hanafi usul, qiyas requires a clear 'illa (effective cause) linking the original and new case.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi position
The overwhelming acceptance of qiyas, supported by consensus, makes it a binding source for fatwa. The Zahiri view is not considered a valid ikhtilaf in the Hanafi school.
A mufti must rely on qiyas when no explicit text or ijma covers the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني : مصادر الفقه الإسلامي ما لا نهاية. وهذا من سر سَعَةِ الفقه الإسلامي الشاملة لما كان وما يكون من الحوادث. على أن هناك جماعة من فقهاء بعض المذاهب لم يقبلوا طريقة القياس واعتمدوا على ظواهر النصوص فقط، فسموا «الظاهرية»؛ ولم يكن لمذهبهم هذا حياة ووزن لمخالفته ضرورات الحياة التشريعية . وقد لخص المُزَني صاحب الإمام الشافعي فكرة القياس وحجته أبلغ تلخيص، فقال: إن الفقهاء من عصر رسول الله إلى يومنا هذا، استعملوا المقاييس في جميع الأحكام في أمر دينهم. وأجمعوا على أنَّ نظير الحقِّ ،حق، ونظير الباطل باطل. فلا يجوز لأحد إنكار القياس، لأنه التشبيه بالأمور والتمثيل عليها (ر) کتاب «مالك» للشيخ محمد أبي زهرة الأستاذ في كلية الحقوق بالقاهرة، القسم الثاني ف/ ١٦٠).
Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Law – without end. This is part of the secret of the comprehensiveness of Islamic law, encompassing whatever events have occurred and will occur. However, there is a group of jurists from some schools who did not accept the method of qiyas and relied only on the literal meanings of texts, so they were called 'the Zahiris'; and their school had no life or weight because it contradicted the necessities of legislative life. And al-Muzani, the companion of Imam al-Shafi'i, summarized the concept of qiyas and its proof in the most comprehensive summary, saying: The jurists from the time of the Messenger of God to our present day have used analogical reasoning (maqayis) in all rulings concerning their religion. And they have consensus that the equivalent of truth is truth, and the equivalent of falsehood is falsehood. So it is not permissible for anyone to deny qiyas, because it is comparing things and analogizing them. (Ref: Book 'Malik' by Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, professor at the Faculty of Law in Cairo, part two, p. 160).
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's introduction discusses the source of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Islamic jurisprudence. It begins by noting the infinite comprehensiveness of Islamic law, which can address all events past and future. Then it mentions that a small group of scholars, called the Zahiris, rejected qiyas entirely and adhered strictly to the literal texts. The author dismisses their school as having no lasting impact because it contravenes the practical needs of legislation. The passage then quotes al-Muzani, the leading student of al-Shafi'i, to summarize the universal acceptance of qiyas: from the time of the Prophet to the present, jurists have used qiyas in all legal rulings. There is consensus (ijma) that the like of a truth is truth, and the like of a falsehood is falsehood. Therefore, denying qiyas is not permissible, as qiyas is essentially comparing similar things. This passage establishes qiyas as a fundamental source of law, supported by the practice of the Companions and later generations, and refutes the Zahiri position as an outlier. For the Hanafi student, qiyas is one of the four primary sources (after Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma), and this passage reinforces its legitimacy.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogizing things to similar things; comparing a new case to an established ruling due to a common underlying reason ('illa).
الظاهرية (al-Zahiriyya): A school that relied only on the literal (zahir) meanings of texts and rejected qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the status of the Zahiri school?
A) It was the dominant school in the early period.
B) It had no life or weight because it contradicted legislative necessities.
C) It was accepted by all scholars.
D) It based its rulings solely on qiyas.

Key Takeaway
The overwhelming consensus (ijma) of Muslim jurists from the time of the Companions onward affirms the validity of qiyas as a source of law; the Zahiri rejection is a historical outlier without practical impact.

The Hierarchy of Islamic Legal Sources
Summarizing the primary and secondary sources and their interrelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary and secondary sources of Islamic law and explain why reducing all sources to the Quran is impractical for deriving specific rulings.

Core Idea
All sources derive ultimate validity from the Quran, but each source provides direct evidence that cannot be reduced to a single text.

This passage concludes the overview of Islamic legal sources. It emphasizes that every ruling in any madhhab must be traceable to one of the established sources. The author then critiques the theoretical reduction of all sources to the Quran alone, using the inheritance of grandparents as an example to show that specific rulings often rely on ijma or sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about sources
The passage states that every fiqh ruling must be based on one of the recognized sources. Read the first sentences to grasp this foundational rule.
The sources include Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas, and in the Hanafi school, Istihsan and Urf.
2
Recognize the reductionist positions
Some scholars reduce all sources to Quran and Sunnah; others reduce them to the Quran alone. Note that the author acknowledges these views as 'true in themselves' but not useful for practical jurisprudence.
This is a methodological distinction: ultimate validity vs. immediate evidence.
3
Examine the counterexample
The author provides the example of the grandfather's and grandmother's inheritance shares. The grandfather's share is established by ijma, the grandmother's by sunnah, not directly by the Quran.
This shows that reducing all to Quran would incorrectly claim these rulings lack textual basis.
4
Understand the Hanafi stance
The Hanafi school accepts multiple sources with independent authority. The passage implicitly supports a multi-source approach, aligning with Hanafi methodology.
The passage is from a Hanafi introductory text, so this position is the standard.
5
Apply to the fatwa process
In issuing a fatwa, a mufti must identify the specific source that directly supports the ruling, not just appeal to the Quran as an ultimate source.
This lesson underscores the practical necessity of knowing all sources.

6/6 - These are the original sources of Islamic jurisprudence, and the most important sources are the secondary ones; we have presented in this introduction what the student must know about them. So every jurisprudential ruling recorded by jurists in various schools must be based on one of them. This, and among scholars are those who reduce all sources to the Book and the Sunnah, considering that consensus in reality must be based on one of them, and that analogy (qiyas) also goes back to the illah of the text in the analogue, thus entering into the indication of that text. The Sunnah, they refer back. Rather, among them are those who reduce all sources to the Book alone, because it is the matter that is accepted, no, and they are excluded from its foundations. And this view is true in itself but does not serve our purpose, for it does not look at the kind of specific direct evidence relied upon in proving rulings, but rather looks at the source of validity of this evidence; and no doubt that the source of validity for all is the Quran. Nevertheless, it is not correct to say, for example, that the inheritance of the grandfather or grandmother (the share of one-sixth) is established by the text of the Quran; rather, the share of the grandfather in inheritance with the son in the absence of the father is established by consensus, and the share of the grandmother is established by the Sunnah, because the Quran does not contain any of that, as previously mentioned in sections 3/3 and 4/3.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage concludes the section on sources. It states that every ruling from any school must be based on one of the recognized sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas, etc.). It then discusses that some scholars attempt to reduce all sources back to the Quran and Sunnah, or even to the Quran alone. The author comments that while such reduction may be correct in its own theoretical framework, it is not useful for practical jurisprudence because it conflates the specific evidence (dalil) for a ruling. For example, the share of a grandfather in inheritance is not directly from the Quran but from ijma, and the grandmother's share from Sunnah. This highlights that each source has its own direct applicability.

Key Terms
المصادر الأصلية (al-masadir al-asliyyah): Primary sources: Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas; the fundamental bases of Islamic law.
المصادر التبعية (al-masadir al-tabi'iyyah): Secondary sources: those not primary but derived or supplementary, such as Istihsan, Urf, and custom in the Hanafi school.
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the scholars of the community on a legal ruling.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning from an existing ruling to a new case based on a shared illah (effective cause).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which source establishes the inheritance share of a grandmother (one-sixth)?
A) Quran
B) Sunnah
C) Ijma
D) Qiyas

Key Takeaway
Every fiqh ruling must be traced to its specific direct source (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas, etc.), even though the ultimate authority remains the Quran.

Historical Emergence of the Primary Sources
Explains the chronological emergence of the four primary sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the chronological order of the four primary sources of Islamic law and identify the hadith of Mu'adh as evidence for the early practice of qiyas.

Core Idea
Only Quran and Sunnah were operative during the Prophet's lifetime; qiyas was used informally, but ijma emerged only after his death.

This lesson traces the historical development of the four primary sources of Islamic jurisprudence. It reveals that the Quran and Sunnah were the sole sources during the prophetic era, while qiyas was already being practiced by some companions in distant lands. The concept of ijma, however, had no place because the Prophet himself was the final arbiter of all disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two operative sources during the Prophet's era
From the passage, note that all rulings were based on the Book (Quran) and the Sunnah (the Prophet's sayings, actions, and approvals).
These two sources were the ultimate reference for all disagreements.
2
Explain the absence of ijma
Understand that ijma (consensus) could not exist because the Prophet was alive to resolve all disputes authoritatively.
Ijma requires scholars to agree after the Prophet's death.
3
Recognize the early practice of qiyas
Some companions used analogical reasoning when away from the Prophet, calling it 'fahm', 'ra'y', or 'ijtihad'.
This was not yet formalized as 'qiyas' but was the same method.
4
Analyze the hadith of Mu'adh
The hadith shows the Prophet approving Mu'adh's method: first Quran, then Sunnah, then personal reasoning by ijtihad.
This hadith is a primary evidence for the legitimacy of qiyas.
5
Consider the scholarly discussion on the hadith's authenticity
Note that some scholars graded it weak while others authenticated it. Its widespread use in usul renders it reliable for establishing the principle.
Hanafis and Shafi'is generally accept it as evidence for qiyas.

Source Text

الفصل السابع
الترتيب التاريخي والاعتباري لمصادر الفقه الإسلامي
۱/۷ - أ ـ الترتيب التاريخي :
إنَّ تلك المصادر الأربعة الأساسية للفقه الإسلامي لم تنشأ كلها معاً في تاريخ واحد.
فقد كان في عصر الرسالة مرجع الأحكام كلها إلى الكتاب والسنة التي تتجلى في بيان الرسول وأقضيته عليه الصلاة والسلام وكل اختلاف فمرده إليه .
لذلك لم يكن ثمة من مجال لتصور إجماع.
•
أما القياس فقد كان يلجأ إليه بعض الصحابة فيما يحدث لهم وهم بعيدون عن النبي في أسفارهم أو قبائلهم أو أعمالهم، ويسمونه فهماً، أو رأياً، أو اجتهاد (۱) ، كما سماه الإمام الشافعي في رسالة الأصول. ولكنهم
(۱) عندما أرسل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم معاذ بن جبل إلى أهل اليمن سأله فقال : كيف تقضي؟ قال معاذ: أقضي بما في كتاب الله قال فإن لم يكن في كتاب الله؟ قال: فبسنة رسول الله له قال فإن لم يكن في سنة رسول الله؟ قال: أجتهد رأيي. فَضَرب رسول الله له في صدره استحساناً وقال : الحمد لله الذي وفق رسول رسول الله لما يرضي رسول الله . والحديث أخرجه أحمد في المسند ٢٤٢:٥، وأبو داود (٣٥٩٣)، والترمذي (۱۳۲۷ ، ۱۳۲۸) وقال : ليس إسناده عندي بمتصل. وأخرجه البيهقي في الكبرى ١١٤:١٠. وصححه الخطيب البغدادي في الفقيه والمتفقه» ۱ : ۱۸۹، وأشار أبو بكر ابن العربي إلى صحته في أحكام القرآن» ٥٧٥١ ، وفصل الكلام عليه في عارضة الأحوذي ٦ : ٧٢ - ٧٣ . وجود الحافظ ابن كثير هذا الحديث في تفسيره ٦:١، وصححه ابن القسم في إعلام الموقعين ٢٠٢:١.
Chapter Seven: Historical and Hierarchical Order of the Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence. 1/7 - A. Historical Order: Indeed, those four primary sources of Islamic jurisprudence did not all arise together in a single history. It was that in the era of the Message, the reference for all rulings was the Book and the Sunnah, which manifested in the Prophet's explanation and judgments, peace be upon him, and every disagreement was referred back to him. Therefore, there was no room for the concept of ijma'. As for qiyas, some companions resorted to it regarding matters that occurred to them while they were away from the Prophet in their travels, tribes, or works, and they called it 'understanding', 'opinion', or 'ijtihad' (1), as Imam al-Shafi'i called it in his Risala. But they... (1) When the Prophet sent Mu'adh ibn Jabal to Yemen, he asked him: How will you judge? Mu'adh said: I will judge by what is in the Book of Allah. He said: If it is not in the Book of Allah? He said: By the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah. He said: If it is not in the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah? He said: I will exert my opinion (ajtahid ra'yi). So the Messenger of Allah patted his chest approvingly and said: Praise be to Allah who has guided the messenger of the Messenger of Allah to what pleases the Messenger of Allah. The hadith is reported by Ahmad in al-Musnad 5:242, Abu Dawud (3593), al-Tirmidhi (1327, 1328) who said its chain is not continuous in my view, and al-Bayhaqi in al-Kubra 10:114. It was authenticated by al-Khatib al-Baghdadi in al-Faqih wa al-Mutafaqqih 1:189, and Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi indicated its authenticity in Ahkam al-Qur'an 5751, and a full discussion is in A'ridat al-Ahwadhi 6:72-73. Ibn Kathir rated it as good (jayyid) in his Tafsir 6:1, and Ibn al-Qayyim authenticated it in I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in 1:202.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage introduces the historical development of the four primary sources of Islamic jurisprudence. The author explains that during the prophetic era, legal reference was exclusively to the Quran and the Sunnah (the Prophet's explanations and judgments). There was no scope for ijma because the Prophet himself was the final authority on all matters of disagreement. However, qiyas (analogical reasoning) was already practiced by some companions, particularly when they were far from the Prophet, such as in travel or distant communities. They referred to it as 'fahm' (understanding), 'ra'y' (opinion), or 'ijtihad' (independent reasoning). The footnote provides the famous hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal, which serves as a primary evidence for the legitimacy of qiyas. The hadith shows that the Prophet approved of Mu'adh's method: first the Quran, then the Sunnah, then his own reasoned opinion. This demonstrates that the use of ijtihad and qiyas dates back to the time of the Prophet, even if it was not formalized as a distinct source until later. The author notes the hadith's status with various scholars—some graded it weak, others authentic—but its recognition in later Hanafi and Shafi'i usul is well-established.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijma): Consensus of the scholars of a generation on a legal ruling; not applicable during the Prophet's lifetime because his authority was paramount.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical extension of a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a shared effective cause (illa); practiced by some companions during the Prophet's time under the term 'ijtihad' or 'ra'y'.
السنة (al-sunnah): The sayings, actions, and tacit approvals of the Prophet; alongside the Quran, the only source during his lifetime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why was there no concept of ijma during the Prophet's era according to the passage?
A) Because the scholars were not yet qualified
B) Because the Prophet was the ultimate reference for all disputes
C) Because the Quran and Sunnah were already complete
D) Because the companions did not meet together

Key Takeaway
The Quran and Sunnah were the only sources during the Prophet's life; qiyas was already practiced informally, and ijma emerged after his death.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
6 min
The Historical Emergence of Islamic Legal Sources
Historical narrative on the chronological order of the four sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the chronological development of the Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas as sources of Islamic law, and identify which sources were operative during the Prophet's lifetime versus after his death.

Core Idea
Only Quran and Sunnah existed as sources during the Prophet's era; Ijma and Qiyas developed later.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa outlines the historical sequence in which the four main sources of Islamic jurisprudence came into use. Understanding this chronology is essential for grasping how the Shariah was derived in different periods and why later sources like Ijma and Qiyas are considered secondary. The passage emphasizes that during the Prophet's lifetime, the Quran and Sunnah were the only authoritative references, while Ijma and Qiyas emerged as formal methods after his death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key periods
The passage distinguishes between the era of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the period after his death. Note that during the Prophet's time, all disputes were referred directly to him.
2
Recognize the primary sources in the Prophetic era
Only the Quran and Sunnah were sources; there was no need for Ijma because the Prophet was the final authority.
3
Understand the role of early ijtihad and its verification
Some Companions used personal reasoning (which later became Qiyas) when away from the Prophet, but they always presented it to him for approval or correction.
This is not formal Qiyas as a source, but an informal practice.
4
Trace the emergence of Ijma after the Prophet
After the Prophet's death, the Caliphs consulted the Companions, and their agreement on a ruling became a precedent that later scholars formalized as Ijma.
5
See the formalization of Qiyas and Istihsan
When no text or consensus was available, scholars turned to ijtihad, which was systematized into Qiyas and Istihsan as formal sources.
The Hanafi school gives Istihsan a distinct role.

Source Text

الفصل السابع
الترتيب التاريخي والاعتباري لمصادر الفقه الإسلامي
١/٧ - أ ـ الترتيب التاريخي :
إنَّ تلك المصادر الأربعة الأساسية للفقه الإسلامي لم تنشأ كلها معاً في تاريخ واحد.
فقد كان في عصر الرسالة مرجع الأحكام كلها إلى الكتاب والسنة التي تتجلى في بيان الرسول وأقضيته عليه الصلاة والسلام وكل اختلاف فمرده إليه . لذلك لم يكن ثمة من مجال لتصور إجماع.
•
أما القياس فقد كان يلجأ إليه بعض الصحابة فيما يحدث لهم وهم بعيدون عن النبي في أسفارهم أو قبائلهم أو أعمالهم، ويسمونه فهماً، أو رأياً، أو اجتهاد (١) ، كما سماه الإمام الشافعي في رسالة الأصول. ولكنهم لا يلبثون أن يعرضوا أفهامهم واجتهاداتهم على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أول ما يعودون إليه، فيقرهم، أو يخطئهم ويصحح لهم.
أما بعد عصر الرسالة فقد كان أبو بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما يجمعان الصحابة في الحوادث ويشاورانهم. فإن لم يعرف أحد منهم فيها عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نصاً أو قضاء، واتفقوا فيها على حكم أنه هو مقتضى قواعد الشريعة عمل الخليفتان فيها بما اتفقوا عليه .
وإن لم يتفق الصحابة على حكم الحادثة اجتهد الخليفتان بآرائهما الحكم الأصلح والأقرب شبهاً بأقضية الرسول عملاً بدلالة القياس أو المصالح .
ثم بعد ذلك حدث في العصور التالية حاجة إلى تعرف ما اتفق عليه فقهاء الصحابة ليتخذ أساساً في فقه الشريعة.
ثم ألحق به في الاعتبار ما يتفق عليه الفقهاء التابعيون(١) من المجتهدين، لأنهم هم الراسخون في علم الشريعة ومرجع فهمها إليهم.
فأصبح الإجماع بعد عصر الرسالة مصدراً من مصادر الأحكام.
فإن لم يحصل هذا الإجماع، أو لم يثبت نقله وروايته في موضوع ما، لم يكن بد لكل فقيه أن يلجأ إلى الطريق التي كان يلجأ إليها الصحابة أنفسهم عندما يعوزهم النص، وذلك طريق الاجتهاد والرأي الذي سمي بعد قياساً أو استحساناً.
فيتلخص من ذلك أنه لم يكن في عصر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مصدر تشريعي غير الكتاب والسنة. وأما الإجماع والقياس فكانا مصدرين تابعين نشآ بعد عصر الرسالة.
(١) التابعون والتابعيون من لم يدركوا النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وإنما أدركوا بعض صحابته. وتابعو التابعين من لم يدركوا أحداً من الصحابة وإنما أدركوا التابعين.
Chapter Seven: The Historical and Conceptual Order of the Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence. 1/7 - A: The Historical Order: Indeed, those four fundamental sources of Islamic jurisprudence did not all arise together at one time. In the era of the Message, the reference for all rulings was the Book and the Sunnah, which are manifested in the explanation of the Messenger and his judgments, peace be upon him, and every disagreement referred back to him. Therefore, there was no room to conceive of consensus (ijma). As for analogy (qiyas), some Companions resorted to it regarding events that occurred while they were far from the Prophet on their travels, among their tribes, or in their work, and they called it understanding (fahm), opinion (ra'y), or independent reasoning (ijtihad), as Imam al-Shafi'i named it in his Risala. But they would not delay in presenting their understandings and ijtihad to the Prophet, peace be upon him, as soon as they returned to him, and he would either approve them or correct their mistakes. After the era of the Message, Abu Bakr and Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, would gather the Companions regarding events and consult them. If none of them knew a text or a ruling from the Prophet, peace be upon him, about them, and they agreed upon a ruling that it was required by the principles of Shariah, the two Caliphs would act according to what they agreed upon. And if the Companions did not agree on a ruling for an event, the two Caliphs would exert ijtihad with their opinions to find the most appropriate ruling and the one closest in resemblance to the judgments of the Messenger, acting by the indication of analogy or interests (masalih). Then, in subsequent eras, there arose a need to ascertain what the jurists among the Companions had agreed upon, to take it as a foundation in the jurisprudence of Shariah. Then, in consideration, there was appended to this what the jurists among the Tabi'un (the successors) of the mujtahids agreed upon, because they were the firmly grounded in the knowledge of Shariah and the reference for understanding it. Thus, consensus (ijma) became after the era of the Message a source of legal rulings. If this consensus was not obtained, or its transmission and narration was not established regarding a subject, there was no alternative for every faqih but to resort to the method that the Companions themselves used when a text was lacking, and that is the method of ijtihad and opinion, which was later called analogy (qiyas) or juristic preference (istihsan). It is concluded from this that in the era of the Prophet, peace be upon him, there was no legislative source other than the Book and the Sunnah. As for consensus and analogy, they were two subsidiary sources that arose after the era of the Message.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative explaining the chronological emergence of the four main sources of Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by stating that the four fundamental sources—Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas—did not arise simultaneously. During the Prophet's lifetime, all rulings were derived from the Quran and Sunnah, as the Prophet was the final authority. There was no need for Ijma (consensus) because disputes were resolved directly by revelation. Qiyas (analogy) existed in practice among some Companions when they were away from the Prophet, but they would always refer their conclusions back to him for approval or correction. After the Prophet's death, the Caliphs Abu Bakr and Umar continued this practice by consulting the Companions. If they agreed on a ruling consistent with Shariah principles, the Caliphs would adopt it, foreshadowing the concept of Ijma. If no consensus was reached, they would use personal reasoning (ijtihad) guided by analogy or public interest. Later, as the Companions passed away, scholars needed to preserve the rulings agreed upon by them and later by the Tabi'un, leading to the recognition of Ijma as a formal source. Finally, when neither text nor consensus was available, scholars turned to ijtihad, which became systematized as Qiyas and Istihsan. Thus, the passage clarifies that only the Quran and Sunnah are primary sources from the Prophet's era, while Ijma and Qiyas are secondary sources that developed later.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the mujtahid scholars of a generation on a legal ruling from the Shariah after the era of the Prophet.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning that extends a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') based on a shared effective cause (illa).
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that departs from a clear qiyas in favor of a stronger consideration (e.g., custom, necessity).
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent reasoning to derive a ruling from the sources when no clear text or consensus exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which sources of Islamic jurisprudence existed during the lifetime of the Prophet (peace be upon him)?
A) Quran and Sunnah only
B) Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma
C) Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas
D) Quran and Ijma only

Key Takeaway
The Quran and Sunnah are the primary sources from the Prophet's time; Ijma and Qiyas are secondary sources that arose later and must be applied in that order.

The Eight Stages of Islamic Fiqh Development
Preview of the eight-stage historical framework of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for studying the eight historical stages of fiqh and identify each stage's place in the development of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Core Idea
Fiqh developed through eight distinct stages; a mufti must know them to properly contextualize rulings.

This lesson introduces the framework of the eight developmental periods of Islamic fiqh. Understanding these stages is essential for a mufti, as each period brought unique methodological approaches and institutional contexts that shaped the rulings we rely on today. The passage previews a detailed study that will help you trace how Hanafi fatwa evolved from the prophetic era to contemporary times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall structure
Recognize that the chapter is divided into a preliminary section and eight independent sections, one for each stage. This structure provides a comprehensive historical map.
The preliminary chapter offers an overview before diving into each period.
2
Note the chronological span
The stages begin with the prophetic era (عصر النبوة) and end with the current eighth stage. Understand that this covers the entire history of Islamic fiqh.
Each stage represents a major shift in methodology or institutions.
3
Reflect on the purpose of such periodization
Consider why the author divided fiqh history into these specific stages. Each stage marks a distinct phase in the development of legal reasoning and madhhab formation.
For a Hanafi mufti, knowing these stages helps situate different levels of authority (zahir al-riwayah, nawadir, etc.).

The Nature of Post-Prophetic Ijtihad: Tashri' vs. Tafri'/Tatbiq
Historical introduction to fiqh periods and nature of ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tashri' (prophetic legislation) and tafri'/tatbiq (derivation/application) in the Hanafi framework, and explain why fiqh development began after the Prophet's death.

Core Idea
Post-Prophetic ijtihad is not legislation but derivation; fiqh expands through application of revealed principles to new cases.

This passage, from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to the periods of Islamic fiqh, sets a crucial foundation: the Shariah was complete in its principles at the Prophet's death. What came after was not new law but careful derivation from those principles. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi view that ijtihad is an act of discovery, not creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about the completion of Shariah
Find the sentence stating that the Prophet left this life only after the Shariah was completed through explicit texts on foundations and general principles, with restrictions, clarifications, and abrogations.
This refutes the idea that the Shariah was incomplete.
2
Distinguish tashri' from tafri'/tatbiq
Locate the direct statement: 'فاجتهاد الصحابة ومن بعدهم لم يكن تشريعاً؛ وإنما هو تفريع وتطبيق.' The author explicitly denies that Companions' ijtihad was legislation; it was derivation and application.
In Hanafi usul, tashri' is exclusive to the Prophet (peace be upon him).
3
Recognize the catalyst for fiqh development
The passage states that the scattered rulings and principles would have sufficed had Islam not expanded beyond Arabia, where new customs and incidents required organization and rule-setting.
This explains why fiqh developed historically – necessity due to new contexts.
4
Understand the expansive nature of Quranic foundations
The last line says: 'وتلك الأسس التي احتواها القرآن كانت أصولاً قابلة لأن تتسع' – the Qur'anic foundations were principles capable of expansion.
This underpins the Hanafi reliance on qiyas and istihsan to extend rulings.

Chapter Three: Periods of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Distinctive Features in Each. This chapter includes an introductory chapter, which is Chapter Nine, in which we give a general overview of the entire chapter, followed by a separate chapter for each of the eight periods of fiqh, starting from the first fiqh period, which is the era of prophethood, and ending with the current eighth fiqh period. Chapter Nine: On the expansion and development of Islamic fiqh and its historical periods. 1/9 - The Prophet did not leave this life until the structure of the Shariah was completed by explicit text on its foundations and general principles, restricting absolute texts, clarifying the ambiguous, specifying the general, abrogating what was intended to be abrogated, etc. So the ijtihad of the Companions and those after them was not legislation; rather it was derivation and application. (Cf. Book: History of Islamic Legislation by Professor al-Subki and his colleagues, p. 7). But the Prophet, peace be upon him, did not leave his Companions a compiled fiqh; rather he left a set of general principles and rules and partial rulings and judgments, scattered in the Qur'an and the Sunnah. And this would have nearly sufficed them had not the dominion of Islam extended beyond the Arabian Peninsula, where they encountered matters, incidents, and customs with which they were unfamiliar, so they needed to organize them, establish rules for them, and place them in the appropriate positions among the rulings and objectives of the Shariah. This is the beginning of the development of Islamic fiqh. And those foundations contained in the Qur'an were principles capable of expanding. 159.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a new chapter on the historical periods of Islamic fiqh. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, introduces the framework: eight periods from the Prophetic era to the present. He then clarifies a crucial point: the Shariah was complete at the Prophet's death in its foundations and general principles. The ijtihad of the Companions was not new legislation (tashri') but derivation and application (tafri' wa tatbiq) from those principles. The Prophet left no codified fiqh, but rather scattered rulings and general rules in the Qur'an and Sunnah. These would have sufficed had Islam not expanded. New encounters required the Companions to derive rules compatible with Shariah objectives. Thus, fiqh development began. The passage emphasizes that the Qur'anic foundations were inherently expansive, allowing for growth.

Key Terms
تشريع (tashri'): Legislation in the sense of establishing new divine laws; the author states that post-Prophetic ijtihad is not tashri' but rather derivation.
تفريع وتطبيق (tafri' wa tatbiq): Derivation and application; the activity of later jurists as they extrapolated rulings from the general principles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It was new legislation (tashri') added to the Shariah.
B) It was derivation and application (tafri' wa tatbiq) from existing principles.
C) It was independent reasoning without reference to revelation.
D) It was limited to matters not mentioned in the Qur'an or Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Post-Prophetic ijtihad is not new legislation; it is the derivation and application of rulings from the complete Shariah, a principle that underpins Hanafi usul and fatwa methodology.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
15 min
The Necessity of Fiqh Development After the Prophet
Explaining the need for fiqh development after the prophetic era

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Islamic fiqh needed to develop after the Prophet and distinguish between legislation (tashri') and derivation (tafri').

Core Idea
The Prophet left principles, not a code; fiqh developed through tafri' to meet new circumstances.

Many assume the Prophet left a complete codified fiqh, but the reality is that he left foundational principles and scattered rulings. When Islam expanded beyond Arabia, Muslims faced unfamiliar customs and situations, requiring them to derive new rulings from those principles. This process of tafri' (derivation) is the engine of fiqh development, not new legislation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the completeness of Sharia at the Prophet's death
The Prophet completed the Sharia by establishing its foundations, general principles, and explicit texts on key matters. However, this does not mean a fully codified fiqh was left behind.
The Sharia was complete in its sources (Quran and Sunnah), but their application to new cases required ijtihad.
2
Understand that the Prophet left principles, not a code
He left a body of 'usul wa qawa'id kulliyyah' (universal principles and rules) and particular rulings, but these were not compiled into a systematic code. The Companions had to engage in tafri' to apply them.
Tafri' is deduction from principles, not independent legislation.
3
Identify the catalyst for development
When Islam spread beyond the Arabian Peninsula, Muslims encountered new cultures, customs, and legal needs. The existing principles had to be interpreted and expanded to cover these new realities.
Examples include regulating transactions with non-Muslims and war rights.
4
Appreciate the flexibility of Islamic principles
The Quranic principles were inherently capable of expansion and adaptation. As intellectual horizons broadened and new challenges arose, understanding of these principles evolved.
This is a key hallmark of Hanafi methodology: the ability to respond to changing circumstances through ijtihad.
5
Distinguish tafri' from tashri'
The ijtihad of the Companions and later scholars was tafri' (derivation and application of existing principles), not tashri' (new legislation). This ensures that fiqh remains rooted in revelation while addressing new situations.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the authority of later jurists.

Chapter Nine: On the expansion of Islamic jurisprudence and its development and its historical phases. 1/9 - The Prophet did not leave this life except after the structure of Sharia was completed by explicit text on the foundations and general principles, restricting unrestricted texts, explaining ambiguous ones, specifying general ones, and abrogating what was intended to be abrogated, etc... So the ijtihad of the Companions and those after them was not legislation; rather it was derivation and application. (See the book 'History of Islamic Legislation' by al-Subki and his colleagues, p.7). But the Prophet, peace be upon him, did not leave his Companions a codified fiqh; rather he left a body of principles and universal rules and of particular rulings and judgments, scattered in the Quran and the Sunnah. And this would have almost sufficed them if the dominion of Islam had not extended beyond the Arabian Peninsula, where they encountered matters, events, and customs they were not familiar with, so they needed to organize them and establish rules for them, and to assign them the appropriate status within the rulings and objectives of Sharia. This is the beginning of the development of Islamic jurisprudence. And those foundations contained in the Quran were principles capable of expanding their concepts, and their understanding develops with the expansion of intellectual circles and at major events where Islam connected with other conditions and cultures. (See the book 'A General Overview of the History of Islamic Jurisprudence', pp. 52-53). Here, Muslim scholars opened the door to thinking about issues, and the practical matters of life began to be codified in light of religion. The development in their systems was a result of the work of the caliphs and their successors, according to what the situation required. And in the conquered lands, following the entry of nations in droves into Islam, a great need arose to teach them what was hidden from them and to regulate and coordinate practical Sharia rulings to organize transactions and know rights. Successive wars also called for organization and derived rulings in the laws of war and combatants, and in the civil and political rights of peoples in conquered lands, in light of Islamic principles. (1) See the advice of Umar ibn al-Khattab to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari regarding the judiciary when he appointed him; its text has been presented in the discussion of qiyas. (2) Examples of that in civil rights: permitting dealing in wine and pigs for dhimmis – i.e., non-Muslims – while dealing in them is invalid for Muslims. Also confirming them in what they practice in their personal status and family rights, such as validating the marriages of their women without a waiting period they observe after the death of their husbands if that was permissible according to them. Examples of war rights: the fatwa of Imam al-Awza'i prohibiting the killing of political hostages if their people betrayed covenants with Muslims and broke treaties, due to the saying of Allah in the Great Quran: 'And no bearer of burdens will bear the burden of another,' while it was customary among nations to kill them. (See the introduction of Amir Shakib Arslan to the treatise 'Mahasin al-Masa'i fi Tarjamat al-Imam al-Awza'i', p. 14, quoting from al-Baladhuri's history.) Political hostages are persons of status among their people who are detained by other people as a guarantee for fulfilling covenants between the two parties. Examples of political rights also include what al-Baladhuri narrated in his book 'Futuh al-Buldan' (p. 428): 'When Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz became caliph, a delegation from the people of Samarkand came to him and complained to him...'
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a historical and philosophical overview of the development of Islamic fiqh. It begins by stating that the Prophet left the Sharia complete in its foundations, but not as a codified fiqh. The Companions' ijtihad was not new legislation but rather derivation and application of those principles. The need for development arose when Islam expanded beyond Arabia, exposing Muslims to new customs and situations. The Quranic principles were flexible enough to accommodate expansion. The author then describes how scholars opened the door to reasoning, and the practical organization of life began in light of Islamic principles, driven by caliphs and their successors. In conquered lands, the influx of new Muslims necessitated teaching and codifying laws. Successive wars also demanded regulation of war rights and civil/political rights. Footnotes provide examples: Umar's advice on judging, civil rights for dhimmis (e.g., allowing trade in wine/pork, upholding their family laws), and al-Awza'i's fatwa against killing political hostages based on the Quranic principle of individual responsibility. This illustrates how fiqh adapted through ijtihad to new circumstances while remaining rooted in Islamic sources.

Key Terms
تفريع (tafri'): Deriving subsidiary rulings from established principles; application of general rules to particular cases.
أصول وقواعد كلية (usul wa qawa'id kulliyyah): Foundational principles and universal rules that form the basis for deriving further rulings.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): The effort of a qualified scholar to derive a ruling from the primary sources (Quran and Sunnah) when the ruling is not explicitly stated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the main catalyst for the development of Islamic fiqh after the Prophet?
A) The Prophet's order to compile fiqh before his death.
B) The expansion of Islam beyond the Arabian Peninsula and encountering new customs.
C) The desire of the Companions to create a new legal system.
D) The need to translate fiqh into other languages.

Key Takeaway
Islamic fiqh developed through tafri' (derivation) from Quranic principles to address new realities encountered after the Prophet's era.

The Eight Historical Periods of Islamic Fiqh
Classifies Islamic fiqh history into eight periods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and describe the eight historical periods of Islamic fiqh development as classified by Mustafa al-Zarqa, and explain the significance of the preparatory and foundational phases.

Core Idea
Islamic fiqh evolved through eight distinct periods, from the Prophet to modern times, each with unique characteristics.

This lesson presents a historical classification of Islamic fiqh into eight successive periods, as outlined by the renowned scholar Mustafa al-Zarqa. Understanding this periodization helps you grasp how fiqh developed from its prophetic roots through its golden age, stagnation, and modern revival. We will walk through each period, note its defining features, and examine a concrete historical example that demonstrates the practical application of fiqh to state affairs.

Guided Path
1
List the Eight Periods
Read the passage and extract the eight periods in order. Write them down with their approximate time frames.
Pay attention to the two main phases: preparatory (first two periods) and foundational (third period).
2
Analyze the Samarkand Example
In the second period, the passage recounts the story of Qutayba ibn Muslim and the judge's ruling. Understand how Caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz enforced Sharia over military action. This illustrates the principle that fiqh governs public life based on reality (haqiqa).
This example shows that even political decisions must comply with fiqh rulings.
3
Identify Characteristics of Each Period
For each period, note the key developments mentioned: the rise of madhhabs in the third, peak of ijtihad in the fourth, takhrij and tarjih in the fifth, decline in the sixth, the Majalla in the seventh, and modern independence in the eighth.
The sixth period is labeled 'decline' but still saw scholarly activity.
4
Understand the Phases
Group the first two periods as the 'preparatory phase' (al-marhalah al-tamhidiyyah) and the third as the 'foundational phase' (al-marhalah al-ta'sisiyyah). Explain why the author divides them this way.
The preparatory phase laid the groundwork; the foundational phase established fiqh as an independent discipline.
5
Reflect on the Eighth Period
Consider the current period starting from the independence of many Islamic countries. What challenges and opportunities does it present for fiqh? The passage is cut off, but you can infer from the trend.
Think about the revival of ijtihad and the role of modern states.

الفصل (۹) الفقه تطوره وأدواره التاريخية

والخلاصة أنه على أثر الحاجات الضرورية التي كانت تظهر تباعاً بصورة تدريجية أخذ الفقه الإسلامي يتطور ويتّسع أفقه، سواء في ذلك ما يخص الحياة الداخلية وما يخص الحياة الخارجية للدولة الإسلامية. وهكذا تطوّر الفقه في الأزمنة المتوالية وأخذت الأجيال المتعاقبة في تنميته، حتى أصبح بناء ضخماً هائلاً منظماً لكل أنواع المعاملات والعلاقات الإنسانية تنظيماً دقيقاً.

هذا وصف إجمالي لنشأة الفقه الإسلامي وتعاظم بنائه ، وتباعد آفاقه وأجوائه . وسنتولى فيما يلي تقسيماً وتفصيلاً للأدوار التاريخية التي تميزت في كل منها للفقه الإسلامي خصائص وأطوار.

أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي:

۲/۹ - إنَّ التتبع التاريخي لحركة الفقه الإسلامي يوحي بتقسيم المراحل التطورية التي مرَّ بها هذا الفقه إلى ثمانية أدوار :

=

الدور الأول: عصر الرسالة أي مدة حياة الرسول .

الدور الثاني: عصر الخلفاء الراشدين فيما بعده إلى منتصف القرن الأول الهجري، حيث استتب الأمر للأمويين ونهج معظمهم بسياستهم الداخلية على وفق أهوائهم في الحكم، لا على وفق الأوامر الشرعية. أن قائد الجيش الإسلامي قتيبة بن مسلم دخل بلدتهم غدراً، فكتب إلى عامله أن ينصب لهم قاضياً، فإن قضى القاضي بإخراج المسلمين أخرجوا، فنصب لهم جميع بن حاضر الباجي فحاكمهم والقائد ، وقضى بإخراج الجيش الإسلامي، وأن ينذرهم القائد وينابذهم على سواء ثم يحاربهم إن أبوا فكره أهل سمرقند الحرب وأقروا بقاء الجيش.

وبذلك كما ترى قرر عمر بن عبد العزيز مبدأ إخضاع الجيش والسياسة للقضاء العادي، أخذاً من قواعد الشرع وكثير من نصوصه لأن فكرة الحلال والحرام الدينية، واعتبار الحق بالنظر إلى الواقع لا إلى الظاهر هي صبغة أصلية في التشريع الإسلامي وفقهه كما سبقت الإشارة إليه وهي معتبرة في نطاق الحقوق الخاصة والعامة على السواء (ر: ف ۱/۲ - ۳) .

وهذان الدوران هما المرحلة التمهيدية للفقه الإسلامي.

الدور الثالث : من منتصف القرن الأول إلى أوائل القرن الثاني، حيث استقل علم الفقه وأصبح اختصاصاً ينصرف إليه، وتكونت المدارس الفقهية، أي الاجتهادات المسماة: المذاهب. وهذا الدور هو المرحلة التأسيسية في الفقه

الدور الرابع من أوائل القرن الثاني إلى منتصف القرن الرابع بلغ الفقه الأوج في الاجتهاد والتدوين والتفريع المذهبي، وتم فيه وضع علم أصول الفقه، وتكامل. وهذا الدور هو دور الكمال في الفقه الإسلامي.

الدور الخامس من منتصف القرن الرابع إلى سقوط بغداد في أيدي التتار في منتصف القرن السابع، وفيه نشطت حركة التحرير والتخريج والترجيح في المذاهب.

الدور السادس من منتصف القرن السابع إلى ظهور مجلة الأحكام العدلية التي تم وضعها على يد لجنة من الفقهاء وصدرت الإرادة السنية السلطانية بالعمل بها في ٢٦ شعبان سنة ١٢٩٣ هـ . وهذا الدور هو دور الانحطاط الفقهي.

الدور السابع من ظهور المجلة إلى بعيد الحرب العالمية الثانية. أي بعيد منتصف القرن الرابع عشر الهجري (نحو منتصف القرن العشرين الميلادي)، حين انحصر تطبيق المجلة في بضعة بلدان فقط .

الدور الثامن: الحالي بدءاً من استقلال كثير من البلدان الإسلامية عن
Chapter (9): Fiqh: Its Development and Historical Stages. Summary: As a result of the necessary needs that emerged successively in a gradual manner, Islamic fiqh began to develop and expand its horizon, both regarding the internal life and the external life of the Islamic state. Thus fiqh developed over successive times, and successive generations undertook its development, until it became a huge, immense structure precisely organizing all types of transactions and human relations. This is a general description of the genesis of Islamic fiqh and the growth of its structure and the spread of its horizons and spheres. We will now undertake a division and detailing of the historical stages that characterized each of them with specific features and phases. Stages of the Development of Islamic Fiqh: 2/9 - Historical tracking of the movement of Islamic fiqh suggests dividing the evolutionary stages that this fiqh passed through into eight stages: = First Stage: The era of the Message, i.e., the lifetime of the Messenger. Second Stage: The era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, from after him to the middle of the first Hijri century, when matters settled for the Umayyads and most of them followed their internal policies according to their whims in governance, not according to the legal commands. That the commander of the Islamic army, Qutayba ibn Muslim, entered their town treacherously, so he wrote to his governor to appoint a judge for them, and if the judge ruled that the Muslims should leave, they would leave. So he appointed for them Jumayy ibn Hadhir al-Baji, and he judged between them and the commander, and ruled that the Islamic army should leave, and that the commander should give them notice and declare war fairly, then fight them if they refused. The people of Samarkand disliked war and agreed to the army's remaining. Thus, as you see, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz established the principle of subjecting the army and politics to the regular judiciary, taking from the rules of Sharia and many of its texts, because the idea of halal and haram in religion, and considering right based on reality not appearance, is an original characteristic of Islamic legislation and its fiqh, as previously indicated, and it is considered in the scope of private and public rights equally (see: 1/2-3). These two stages are the preparatory phase of Islamic fiqh. Third Stage: From the middle of the first century to the early second century, when the science of fiqh became independent and became a specialization devoted to it, and the fiqh schools were formed, i.e., the ijtihads called madhhabs. This stage is the foundational phase of fiqh. Fourth Stage: From the early second century to the middle of the fourth century: fiqh reached its peak in ijtihad, codification, and madhhab-based derivation, and the science of usul al-fiqh was laid down and perfected. This stage is the perfection phase in Islamic fiqh. Fifth Stage: From the middle of the fourth century to the fall of Baghdad at the hands of the Tatars in the middle of the seventh century, during which the activity of editing, extracting, and weighing (takhrij and tarjih) within the madhhabs was active. Sixth Stage: From the middle of the seventh century to the appearance of the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, which was compiled by a committee of jurists and the sultan's decree was issued to implement it on 26 Sha'ban 1293 AH. This stage is the decline phase of fiqh. Seventh Stage: From the appearance of the Majalla to shortly after the Second World War, i.e., shortly after the middle of the fourteenth Hijri century (around the middle of the twentieth century CE), when the application of the Majalla was confined to only a few countries. Eighth Stage: The current stage, beginning with the independence of many Islamic countries from...
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents a historical overview of the development of Islamic fiqh, dividing it into eight successive periods. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by explaining that fiqh evolved gradually in response to emerging needs, covering both internal and external aspects of the Islamic state. He then lists eight stages: 1) the prophetic era, 2) the Rightly Guided Caliphs and early Umayyads (preparatory phase), 3) the formation of independent fiqh schools (foundational phase), 4) the peak of ijtihad and codification (perfection phase), 5) the era of tarjih and takhrij within madhhabs, 6) the period of stagnation until the Ottoman Majalla, 7) the Majalla era up to WWII, and 8) the modern post-independence period. The text includes a notable historical example from the second period: the case of Samarkand, where Caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz ordered that the Islamic army's occupation be subject to a judge's ruling, illustrating the principle that even military and political actions must comply with Sharia. This demonstrates the author's view that Islamic fiqh is not merely theoretical but governs public affairs based on reality (haqiqa) not mere appearances.

Key Terms
المرحلة التمهيدية (al-marhalah al-tamhidiyyah): The preparatory stage of Islamic fiqh, comprising the first two periods (prophetic era and early caliphate up to mid-1st century AH).
المرحلة التأسيسية (al-marhalah al-ta'sisiyyah): The foundational stage of Islamic fiqh, comprising the third period (mid-1st to early 2nd century AH) when fiqh became an independent discipline and schools emerged.
دور الكمال (dawr al-kamal): The perfection phase (fourth period) when fiqh reached its peak in ijtihad, codification, and the science of usul al-fiqh was perfected.
دور الانحطاط (dawr al-inhitat): The decline phase (sixth period) characterized by relative stagnation in ijtihad, though it ended with the compilation of the Majalla.
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya): The Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi jurisprudence, compiled in the 19th century.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Islamic fiqh developed through eight distinct historical periods, each with unique characteristics, and understanding this periodization is essential for contextualizing fiqh rulings and scholarly contributions.

History and Development of Fiqh

Preparatory and Foundational Stages of Fiqh (1st-4th Centuries)
Lists the first four historical periods of fiqh development.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the first four historical stages of fiqh development and identify the distinguishing milestones of each stage, including the emergence of madhhabs and usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Fiqh matured from a preparatory phase into an independent science with the formation of madhhabs and codification of usul al-fiqh by the 4th century.

This lesson covers the first four stages of Islamic fiqh development as identified by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa. Understanding this chronology helps a mufti appreciate how fiqh evolved from being inseparable from daily life to a specialized discipline with formal methodologies. We begin with the early formative centuries, which set the stage for the later golden age.

Guided Path
1
Identify the preparatory phase
Note that the first two centuries (1st-2nd Hijri) are considered preparatory. Governance was not fully aligned with Shariah, and fiqh had not yet become an independent science.
2
Recognize the foundational phase
The third stage (mid-1st to early 2nd century) marks the emergence of independent fiqh scholarship and the formation of madhhabs. This is when ijtihad became systematized.
The madhhabs formed during this period are the proto-Hanafi, Maliki, etc.
3
Understand the perfection phase
The fourth stage (early 2nd to mid-4th century) is the peak of ijtihad, codification, and madhhab-based elaboration. The science of usul al-fiqh was established and perfected.
This is the period of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and the compilation of Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Note the transition
The fourth stage ends around the mid-4th century, after which fiqh activity shifted to editing and weighting within madhhabs.

Chapter Three: Stages of the development of Islamic fiqh and its characteristics. The first Hijri century, when the Umayyads established control and most of them pursued their internal policies according to their whims in governance, not according to Shariah commands. These two stages are the preparatory phase of Islamic fiqh. The third stage: from the middle of the first century to the early second century, when the science of fiqh became independent and became a specialization to which one devoted oneself, and fiqh schools (i.e., the ijtihads called madhhabs) were formed. This stage is the foundational phase in fiqh. The fourth stage: from the early second century to the middle of the fourth century, fiqh reached its peak in ijtihad, codification, and madhhab-based elaboration, and the science of usul al-fiqh was established and perfected. This stage is the stage of perfection in Islamic fiqh. The fifth stage: from the middle of the fourth century to the fall of Baghdad at the hands of the Tatars in the middle of the seventh century, during which the movement of editing, derivation (takhrij), and weighting (tarjih) within the madhhabs was active. The sixth stage: from the middle of the seventh century to the emergence of the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, which was compiled by a committee of jurists and the imperial Sultanic decree issued for its implementation on 26 Sha'ban 1293 AH. This stage is the stage of fiqh decline. The seventh stage: from the emergence of the Majallah to after World War II, i.e., after the middle of the fourteenth century AH (around the middle of the twentieth century CE), when the application of the Majallah was confined to only a few countries. The eighth stage: the current stage, beginning from the independence of many Islamic countries from direct colonialism after World War II until today, when a multifaceted Islamic awakening emerged, especially in the field of fiqh. We will dedicate a detailed chapter to each of these stages later, and we will point out the trends that occurred in the movement of fiqh in each stage and the qualities and features that emerged.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents a periodization of Islamic fiqh history into eight stages as outlined by Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It is a historical overview intended to provide context for the development of fiqh. The first two stages (early Hijri centuries) are lumped together as a preparatory phase where Islamic governance was not fully aligned with Shariah. The third stage marks the emergence of independent fiqh scholarship and the formation of madhhabs. The fourth stage is considered the golden age with peak ijtihad and codification of usul al-fiqh. The fifth stage saw activity in editing and weighting within madhhabs. The sixth stage is labeled as decline, culminating in the Ottoman Majallah in the 13th century. The seventh stage saw limited application of the Majallah. The eighth stage is the contemporary period with an Islamic revival. The author promises detailed chapters for each stage later.

Key Terms
دور (dawr): A historical period or stage in the development of Islamic fiqh
المرحلة التمهيدية (al-marhalah al-tamhidiyyah): The preparatory phase, referring to the first two centuries of Islam before fiqh became an independent science

History and Development of Fiqh

Later Stages of Fiqh: Decline, Codification, and Revival (5th-8th Centuries)
Lists the later four historical periods of fiqh development, from editing to contemporary revival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to characterize the later four stages of fiqh development, explain the reasons for the decline, and describe the modern revival.

Core Idea
After a period of decline following the golden age, fiqh saw a revival through codification (Majallah) and a contemporary awakening.

This lesson continues the historical periodization of fiqh, covering stages five to eight. These stages are marked by internal madhhab activity, a decline, the Ottoman Majallah codification, and the recent Islamic revival. Understanding these stages is crucial for a mufti to weigh the authority of later rulings and to contextualize the current renaissance in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Examine the editing and weighting phase
Stage 5 (mid-4th to mid-7th century AH) is characterized by activity within madhhabs: editing (tahrir), derivation (takhrij), and weighting (tarjih). No new major schools formed.
This is the era of later Hanafi scholars like al-Sarakhsi and al-Marghinani (author of al-Hidayah).
2
Identify the decline phase
Stage 6 (mid-7th to late 13th century AH) is described as a period of decline (inhitat), ending with the compilation of the Ottoman Majallah.
The decline is linked to political fragmentation and stagnation of ijtihad, but the Majallah itself was a product of this period.
3
Understand the Majallah era
Stage 7 (emergence of the Majallah to after WWII) saw limited application of the Majallah in only a few countries.
The Majallah codified Hanafi principles into a civil code for the Ottoman Empire.
4
Recognize the contemporary revival
Stage 8 (post-WWII independence to present) is marked by an Islamic awakening, especially in fiqh, with renewed interest and activity.
This includes efforts to apply Shariah in modern contexts, such as Islamic finance.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
الأول الهجري، حيث استتب الأمر للأمويين ونهج معظمهم بسياستهم الداخلية على وفق أهوائهم في الحكم، لا على وفق الأوامر الشرعية.
وهذان الدوران هما المرحلة التمهيدية للفقه الإسلامي.
الدور الثالث : من منتصف القرن الأول إلى أوائل القرن الثاني، حيث استقل علم الفقه وأصبح اختصاصاً ينصرف إليه، وتكونت المدارس الفقهية، أي الاجتهادات المسماة: المذاهب.
وهذا الدور هو المرحلة التأسيسية في الفقه
الدور الرابع من أوائل القرن الثاني إلى منتصف القرن الرابع بلغ الفقه الأوج في الاجتهاد والتدوين والتفريع المذهبي، وتم فيه وضع علم أصول الفقه، وتكامل.
وهذا الدور هو دور الكمال في الفقه الإسلامي.
الدور الخامس من منتصف القرن الرابع إلى سقوط بغداد في أيدي التتار في منتصف القرن السابع، وفيه نشطت حركة التحرير والتخريج والترجيح في المذاهب.
الدور السادس من منتصف القرن السابع إلى ظهور مجلة الأحكام العدلية التي تم وضعها على يد لجنة من الفقهاء وصدرت الإرادة السنية السلطانية بالعمل بها في ٢٦ شعبان سنة / ١٢٩٣ / هـ .
وهذا الدور هو دور الانحطاط الفقهي.
الدور السابع من ظهور المجلة إلى بعيد الحرب العالمية الثانية. أي بعيد منتصف القرن الرابع عشر الهجري (نحو منتصف القرن العشرين الميلادي)، حين انحصر تطبيق المجلة في بضعة بلدان فقط
·
الدور الثامن: الحالي بدءاً من استقلال كثير من البلدان الإسلامية عن الاستعمار المباشر بعد الحرب العالمية الثانية إلى اليوم، حيث برزت للعيان صحوة إسلامية متعددة الجوانب، وبخاصة في جانب الفقه

Chapter Three: Stages of the development of Islamic fiqh and its characteristics. The first Hijri century, when the Umayyads established control and most of them pursued their internal policies according to their whims in governance, not according to Shariah commands. These two stages are the preparatory phase of Islamic fiqh. The third stage: from the middle of the first century to the early second century, when the science of fiqh became independent and became a specialization to which one devoted oneself, and fiqh schools (i.e., the ijtihads called madhhabs) were formed. This stage is the foundational phase in fiqh. The fourth stage: from the early second century to the middle of the fourth century, fiqh reached its peak in ijtihad, codification, and madhhab-based elaboration, and the science of usul al-fiqh was established and perfected. This stage is the stage of perfection in Islamic fiqh. The fifth stage: from the middle of the fourth century to the fall of Baghdad at the hands of the Tatars in the middle of the seventh century, during which the movement of editing, derivation (takhrij), and weighting (tarjih) within the madhhabs was active. The sixth stage: from the middle of the seventh century to the emergence of the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, which was compiled by a committee of jurists and the imperial Sultanic decree issued for its implementation on 26 Sha'ban 1293 AH. This stage is the stage of fiqh decline. The seventh stage: from the emergence of the Majallah to after World War II, i.e., after the middle of the fourteenth century AH (around the middle of the twentieth century CE), when the application of the Majallah was confined to only a few countries. The eighth stage: the current stage, beginning from the independence of many Islamic countries from direct colonialism after World War II until today, when a multifaceted Islamic awakening emerged, especially in the field of fiqh. We will dedicate a detailed chapter to each of these stages later, and we will point out the trends that occurred in the movement of fiqh in each stage and the qualities and features that emerged.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents a periodization of Islamic fiqh history into eight stages as outlined by Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It is a historical overview intended to provide context for the development of fiqh. The first two stages (early Hijri centuries) are lumped together as a preparatory phase where Islamic governance was not fully aligned with Shariah. The third stage marks the emergence of independent fiqh scholarship and the formation of madhhabs. The fourth stage is considered the golden age with peak ijtihad and codification of usul al-fiqh. The fifth stage saw activity in editing and weighting within madhhabs. The sixth stage is labeled as decline, culminating in the Ottoman Majallah in the 13th century. The seventh stage saw limited application of the Majallah. The eighth stage is the contemporary period with an Islamic revival. The author promises detailed chapters for each stage later.

Key Terms
التحرير والتخريج والترجيح (al-tahrir wa al-takhrij wa al-tarjih): Editing, derivation, and weighting: activities within madhhabs to refine and choose among existing opinions
دور الانحطاط الفقهي (dawr al-inhitat al-fiqhi): The stage of fiqh decline, a period where independent ijtihad stagnated
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah): The Ottoman Majalla, a codification of Hanafi fiqh for civil transactions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which stage is specifically called 'the stage of fiqh decline'?
A) The fifth stage
B) The sixth stage
C) The seventh stage
D) The eighth stage

Key Takeaway
The later stages of fiqh saw internal refinement, a period of decline punctuated by the Majallah codification, and a contemporary revival, each shaping how muftis approach rulings today.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Prophetic Era: The Original Scope of Fiqh
Historical overview of fiqh in the prophetic era

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the broad scope of fiqh in the prophetic era and identify the role of the Prophet ﷺ and the Qurra' in early Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
In the prophetic era, fiqh meant all understanding from Quran and Sunnah — creed, law, and ethics — not just legal rulings.

This lesson explores the first period of Islamic fiqh: the age of the Prophet ﷺ. It clarifies that the term 'fiqh' was far broader than later technical definitions, encompassing faith, practice, and spirituality. It also introduces the companions known as 'Qurra'', who were the first jurists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of authority
Recognize that in the prophetic era, the Prophet ﷺ held sole authority for legislation, adjudication, and fatwa. This sets the foundation for understanding later developments.
This is unique to the prophetic era; after his death, ijtihad emerged.
2
Understand the original meaning of fiqh
Note that fiqh was used for everything understood from the Quran and Sunnah, including creed (aqidah), practical law (ahkam), and etiquette (adab). It was not limited to legal rulings.
This contrasts with later Hanafi usage where fiqh is restricted to practical legal rulings.
3
Differentiate Meccan and Medinan periods
Observe that in Mecca, the emphasis was on creed and combating polytheism; practical legislation came after the hijra to Medina.
This shows the gradual revelation of Sharia.
4
Recognize the Qurra' as early fuqaha
The companions who specialized in fiqh were called Qurra' because they memorized the Quran and its rulings. They learned and acted upon the Quran simultaneously.
The hadith cited emphasizes acting upon knowledge.
5
Reflect on the integration of knowledge and action
The companions learned ten verses at a time and did not move on until they had acted upon them. This shows that fiqh was inseparable from practice.
This methodology influenced later Hanafi emphasis on practice (amal).

Chapter 10: The First Fiqh Era: The Age of Prophecy. 1/10 - The authority of legislation, adjudication, and issuing fatwas in this era was solely in the hands of the Messenger, he is the reference in all of that. In his time, the structure of the Sharia was completed in the fulfillment of its foundational texts as previously stated. The word 'fiqh' was used in the prophetic era for everything understood from the texts of the Book and the Sunnah, whether from matters of creed, practical legislation, or etiquette. Islam in Mecca was directed towards reforming creed and combating polytheism. As for its direction towards practical legislation, it was only in Medina after the Hijra. The words 'fiqh' and 'knowledge' in this era were synonymous in Islamic usage; numerous hadiths indicate that. The jurists or scholars from the Companions of the Messenger (peace be upon him) were called 'Qurra'', plural of Qari, and they were memorizers of the Quran by heart, because the Quran is the primary source for the knowledge of the Sharia, and they used to memorize its verses and their rulings (1). Hence (1) Al-A'mash narrated from Abu Wa'il from Ibn Mas'ud that he said: 'If a man among us learned ten verses, he would not go beyond them until he knew their meanings and acted upon them.' Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami said: 'Those who used to teach us told us that they used to recite to the Prophet, and if they learned ten verses, they would not leave them until they acted upon what was in them of deeds. They said: So we learned the Quran and action together.' Ref: Tafsir Ibn Kathir introduction, 3/1. 165
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an historical overview of the first period of fiqh, the prophetic era. It establishes three key points: (1) The Prophet ﷺ held sole authority for legislation, judgment, and fatwa. (2) The term 'fiqh' was broader than later technical usage, encompassing all understanding from Quran and Sunnah, including creed and ethics, not just practical law. (3) The companions who specialized in this knowledge were called 'Qurra'' because they memorized the Quran and its rulings. The passage includes a hadith from Ibn Mas'ud and a statement from Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami emphasizing that the companions learned the Quran and acted upon it simultaneously. This illustrates that early fiqh was inseparable from both faith and practice.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): In the prophetic era, it means understanding everything derived from the Quran and Sunnah, including creed, practical law, and ethics.
القراء (al-qurra'): Companions who memorized the Quran and were the primary scholars of the Sharia in the prophetic era.
سنة (sunnah): The sayings, actions, and approvals of the Prophet, which together with the Quran form the primary texts of the Sharia.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh in the prophetic era was comprehensive knowledge of the Deen, and the Prophet ﷺ was the sole source of legislation and guidance.

Fiqh in the Prophetic Era: Scope and Nature
Historical description of the Prophetic era's fiqh characteristics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the unique features of fiqh in the Prophetic era that shape subsequent fatwa methodology and describe the role of the Qurra' as the first scholars.

Core Idea
Fiqh was the practical understanding of revelation, not theoretical speculation.

The Prophetic era laid the foundation for all subsequent Islamic legal thought. This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa outlines the key features of that era: the central authority of the Prophet, the broad meaning of 'fiqh', and the practical nature of legal inquiry. Understanding this context is essential for a mufti to appreciate the original flexibility and generality of Sharia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of authority
Locate in the text who held the authority of legislation, judiciary, and fatwa. How does this differ from later eras?
The Prophet was the sole reference.
2
Understand the scope of 'fiqh'
Note that 'fiqh' during this period included creed, ethics, and practical law. It was synonymous with 'knowledge' (al-ilm).
Not yet a specialized legal science.
3
Recognize the practical vs. theoretical nature
Observe that rulings were sought after events occurred, not hypothesized. Fiqh was applied, not abstract.
Contrast with later theoretical fiqh.
4
Examine the educational method of the Companions
Read the footnote about learning ten verses and acting upon them. This shows integrated learning and practice.
The Qurra' were both reciters and practitioners.
5
Spot the seeds of fiqh development
The Prophet left general principles and universal rules, not codified fiqh. This flexibility allowed for future adaptation.
Examples: the two verses on individual responsibility.

Chapter Ten
First Jurisprudential Era: The Era of Prophethood

1/10 - The authority of legislation, judiciary, and fatwa in this era was solely in the hands of the Messenger alone, so he was the reference in all of that.
In his time the construction of Sharia was completed in fulfilling its foundational texts as previously mentioned. And the word 'fiqh' was used in the Prophetic era for everything understood from the texts of the Book and the Sunnah, whether it be matters of creed, practical legislation, or ethics. And Islam in Mecca was directed toward reforming creed and combating polytheism. As for its direction toward practical legislation, that was only in Medina after the Hijrah. And the words 'fiqh' and 'knowledge' in this era were synonymous in Islamic custom; numerous hadiths indicate that.
And the jurists or scholars from among the Companions of the Messenger (peace be upon him) were called: 'al-qurra'' (the reciters), plural of 'qari'', and they were memorizers of the Quran by heart, because the Quran is the original source of the knowledge of Sharia, and they used to memorize its verses and its rulings (1). Thus

And Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami said: 'Those who used to teach us the Quran told us that they used to learn the recitation from the Prophet, and when they learned ten verses they would not leave them until they acted upon what was in them of deeds.'
They said: 'So we learned the Quran and action together.'
