Chapter Three: Stages of the Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics
Scholars of Sharia and its jurists.
They were the qurra', and there was not at that time the idea of specialization by devoting oneself to the issues of knowledge from the Book and the Sunnah; rather efforts in general were directed toward jihad and toward action, and knowledge would come in their path from reciting the Quran, listening to the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and comprehending the incidents in which fatwa was given or judgment was passed. And fiqh in this era was practical, not theoretical; for people would seek the ruling on incidents and ask about them after their occurrence, or they would litigate concerning them, and they would be dealt with by the ruling required by Sharia. Incidents were not hypothesized hypothetically... And we have already mentioned earlier that the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not leave for his Companions a codified fiqh, but rather a set of general principles, universal rules, and particular rulings spread throughout the Quran and the Sunnah, yet they are at the ultimate level imaginable of flexibility, generality, and comprehensiveness for various major concepts supported by sound reason and endorsed by articulate interests. And in them is the capacity for adaptation in particular applications to specific incidents, and transition among them from one sound understanding to another different sound understanding, so that from them can be generated a fiqh of amazing breadth, unbounded and inexhaustible.

2/10 - Examples of this capacity in the texts of the Quran and Prophetic Sunnah.
And if you want clarification with examples, look at the following evidences from Quranic verses and Prophetic hadiths, and at their likes from the texts of the Book and the Sunnah:
a) From Quranic verses: 'And no bearer of burdens will bear the burden of another' (al-Isra 17:15) (1).
'And that there is not for man except that [the result of] his effort' (al-Najm 53:39).
(1) The two numbers after the name of a Quranic surah: the first is the surah number and the second is the verse number in it. If there is a single number after the surah name, it is the verse number.
166
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the first phase in the development of Islamic fiqh: the Prophetic era. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, opens by stating that in this period, all legislative, judicial, and fatwa authority rested solely with the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). He then clarifies that the term 'fiqh' during the Prophet's time was used broadly to mean understanding from the Quran and Sunnah, covering creed, law, and ethics. Fiqh was not yet a specialized discipline; it was synonymous with 'knowledge' (al-ilm). The companions who were scholars were called 'al-qurra'' (reciters) because they memorized the Quran and understood its rulings. The author emphasizes that fiqh was practical, not theoretical: people asked about rulings after events occurred, and rulings were applied based on revelation. The Prophet did not leave a codified fiqh but rather general principles, universal rules, and particular rulings in the Quran and Sunnah. These texts are flexible and comprehensive, capable of generating boundless fiqh. To illustrate this flexibility, al-Zarqa quotes two Quranic verses: 'No bearer of burdens will bear the burden of another' (17:15) and 'And that there is not for man except that [the result of] his effort' (53:39). These verses establish principles of individual responsibility and personal accountability, which later fuqaha' would develop into detailed legal rulings. The passage sets the foundation for understanding subsequent phases of fiqh development.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): In this context, understanding of all matters derived from Quran and Sunnah, including creed, law, and ethics.
القراء (al-qurra'): Companions who memorized the Quran and were the scholars of Sharia in the Prophetic era.
الشريعة (al-shari'ah): The divine law as revealed to the Prophet, encompassing all aspects of life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What were the scholar-companions called during the Prophetic era?

B) القراء (qurra')
C) المحدثون (muhaddithun)

Key Takeaway
Fiqh in the Prophetic era was a comprehensive, practical understanding of revelation, not a specialized theoretical discipline.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Prophetic Era: Fiqh as a Practical, Flexible Discipline
Historical description of early fiqh with textual examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how fiqh in the prophetic era was practical and flexible, and identify the implications of this flexibility for modern ifta.

Core Idea
Shariah texts were designed with inherent flexibility to address real events, not hypotheticals.

The prophetic era laid the foundation for Islamic jurisprudence. Understanding its practical, event-driven nature is essential for the mufti: fiqh is not a set of abstract rules but a dynamic response to human circumstances. This lesson explores the characteristics of that era and the built-in adaptability of the primary texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify key characteristics
Read the passage and list the characteristics of fiqh in the prophetic era: realistic (waqi'i), not theoretical; derived after events occurred; based on general principles and universal rules left by the Prophet.
Focus on the contrast with later theoretical developments.
2
Understand textual flexibility
The passage states that the Quran and Sunnah possess 'qabiliyyat al-takayyuf' (adaptability). Reflect on how this allows for multiple sound understandings and application to new situations.
This concept underpins Hanafi istihsan and consideration of custom.
3
Examine the Quranic examples
Review the cited verses (e.g., on contracts, hardship, justice). How do these verses demonstrate generality and flexibility?
Note that they provide overarching principles, not detailed rules for every case.
4
Connect to the mufti's role
Consider how a mufti today uses these same principles to derive rulings for new issues. The passage implies that fiqh is inexhaustible because the texts are adaptable.
This is directly relevant to usul al-iftaa.

Chapter Three: Stages of the Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics. The scholars of Shariah and its jurists. The readers (qurra') were the jurists, and there was not at that time the idea of specialization in devoting oneself to the sciences of the Book and the Sunnah. Rather, efforts in general were directed to the benefit derived from jihad and to action, and knowledge came in their path from reading the Quran, listening to the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and absorbing the events in which fatwas were given or judgments were made. Fiqh in this era was realistic, not theoretical. People only sought the ruling of events and asked about them after their occurrence, or they litigated concerning them, and they were dealt with by the ruling required by the Shariah. Events were not assumed hypothetically. We have already explained earlier that the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not leave for his Companions a codified fiqh, but rather a set of principles, universal rules, and particular rulings scattered in the Quran and the Sunnah, but they are at the most perfect degree of flexibility, generality, and comprehensiveness imaginable for various major concepts supported by sound reason and confirmed by manifest interests. In them is such adaptability in particular applications to specific events, and movement from one sound understanding to another different sound understanding, from which can be generated a fiqh of amazing breadth, unlimited and inexhaustible. 10/2 - Examples of this adaptability in the texts of the Quran and Prophetic Sunnah. Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' falls under the discipline of usul al-iftaa as it describes the historical foundation of Islamic jurisprudence. The author sets the stage for understanding the development of fiqh by characterizing the prophetic era. Key points: The Companions were not specialized jurists; they were 'qurra'' (reciters) who derived fiqh organically from Quran and Sunnah as needed for real-life situations. Fiqh was 'waqi'i' (realistic) not 'nazari' (theoretical) – rulings were sought after events occurred, not through hypothetical scenarios. The Prophet left principles and general rules, not a codified system, yet these texts possess remarkable flexibility ('muruna') and adaptability ('qabiliyyat al-takayyuf') allowing for multiple valid understandings. The author then provides numerous Quranic examples (verses on contracts, justice, divorce, orphans, testimony, hardship, etc.) demonstrating this flexibility. For a Hanafi mufti, this passage emphasizes that fiqh is inherently responsive to real circumstances, not rigid. The examples illustrate that Shariah texts are comprehensive yet adaptable, supporting the Hanafi methodology of istihsan and consideration of custom. This understanding is crucial for fatwa: rulings must be grounded in the texts' spirit of ease and justice.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): In this passage, fiqh refers to the understanding and derivation of legal rulings from the Quran and Sunnah, as practiced by the Companions in the prophetic era – practical knowledge arising from real events.
قابلية التكيف (qabiliyyat al-takayyuf): The capacity of Shariah texts to be adapted to new situations and to yield multiple correct understandings, enabling fiqh to be expansive and inexhaustible.

Key Takeaway
The prophetic era established fiqh as a flexible, event-driven discipline rooted in principles that allow for adaptation to new circumstances.

History and Development of Fiqh

Transition from Reliance to Ijtihad After the Prophet's Death
Historical analysis of the shift from reliance to ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Companions were compelled to engage in ijtihad after the Prophet's death and identify the key factors that necessitated this shift.

Core Idea
When the living reference vanished, the need for ijtihad became indispensable.

This lesson explores a pivotal moment in Islamic legal history: the transition from passive reliance on the Prophet (peace be upon him) to active legal reasoning (ijtihad) by the Companions. Understanding this shift is essential for grasping the origins of juristic authority and the ongoing necessity of ijtihad in the contemporary world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-Prophetic-death state
Recall the role of the Companions during the Prophet's lifetime: they listened, followed, and sought fatwas directly from him. There was no need for personal exertion (ijtihad) because the ultimate reference was present.
2
Recognize the cause of the shift
The Prophet's death removed the living reference. The passage states that they 'suddenly moved from the phase of dependence to the phase of ijtihad' because the direct source of guidance was no longer available.
3
Understand the new resources
What replaced the Prophet's oral statements? The Qur'an and Sunnah became the 'constitutional legacies' that required interpretation and application to new situations.
4
Appreciate the consequences
With no new revelation, every new incident demanded a ruling. Ijtihad became an unavoidable necessity, as the text says: 'a necessity to ijtihad from which there was no escape.'
5
Reflect on the broader context
The rapid expansion of the Islamic state brought diverse cultures and unprecedented issues, further reinforcing the need for continuous ijtihad across generations.

Chapter Three: Stages of development of Islamic jurisprudence and its major features and the primary reference; rather some jurists considered it the sole source of legal rulings, and other sources are only details of its summary, or explanation of it, or derivation from its principles, or deduced from its coherent and supportive meanings and wisdom as previously mentioned. (See: vol. 2/3 and 6/6 and introduction to the book of ownership and contract theory in Islamic law by the venerable professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, p. 98) Chapter Eleven: The second juristic period: the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs and beyond up to the mid-first century. 1/11 - In this period, the condition of the Companion scholars changed from one phase to another. During the lifetime of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), their role was to receive from him and to comprehend what he imparted to them. They had no need to exert thought in understanding rulings requiring insight and consideration, that exertion called ijtihad, because bearing this intellectual burden with the possibility of being right or wrong had no meaning when there existed the one to whom reference is made in all matters of the Sharia and its rulings. So their role during the Prophet's life was listening, following, and seeking fatwas from him regarding what was difficult for them. In other words, dependence on the Prophet in understanding and guidance in everything. But after his death (peace be upon him), they suddenly moved from the phase of dependence to the phase of ijtihad, because that reference had vanished, and its constitutional legacies - the Qur'an and Sunnah - replaced his verbal statements. Thus, at that time, an unavoidable necessity for ijtihad arose regarding emerging incidents, with no limits or end. After the death of the Prophet (peace be upon him), the Muslims advanced as conquerors into the realm of Chosroes and Caesar to destroy the barriers that kings had erected against the call of Islam and its reformist mission on earth. Thus under Muslim rule came nations with ancient civilizations in Egypt, Persia, Syria, and then North Africa, and they stirred...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a historical analysis of the second stage in the development of Islamic jurisprudence, covering the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs and beyond up to the mid-first century. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains the profound shift in the Companions' role from passive receivers to active interpreters. During the Prophet's lifetime, they had no need for ijtihad because they could directly refer to him. After his death, with the cessation of revelation and direct guidance, they were compelled to engage in ijtihad using the Qur'an and Sunnah as constitutional sources. This necessity arose from rapid conquests and new incidents requiring rulings. The passage highlights that ijtihad became an indispensable tool for addressing unforeseen circumstances, marking the birth of juristic reasoning in Islam. The author's tone is descriptive, noting the inevitability and ongoing nature of this intellectual effort.

Key Terms
اجتهاد (ijtihād): The exertion of mental effort by a qualified scholar to derive legal rulings from the primary sources (Qur'an and Sunnah) when no clear textual ruling exists.
اعتماد (iʿtimād): Complete reliance and dependence on the Prophet (peace be upon him) for understanding and guidance in all matters of religion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Companions not need to perform ijtihad during the lifetime of the Prophet (peace be upon him)?
A) They were not knowledgeable enough.
B) They could directly refer to the Prophet for all rulings.
C) Ijtihad was prohibited at that time.
D) They had no new incidents requiring rulings.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of ijtihad arose from the Prophet's death and the ongoing emergence of new incidents, establishing a permanent need for juristic reasoning in Islam.

History and Development of Fiqh
beginner
6 min
The Companions' Shift from Reliance to Ijtihad
Historical analysis of legal epistemology shift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why ijtihad became necessary after the Prophet's death and describe the Companions' three-step method for deriving rulings when no explicit text exists.

Core Idea
The Prophet's death removed the living reference, compelling the Companions to develop ijtihad from Quran, Sunnah, and ray.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's historical survey marks the turning point in Islamic legal history. Before the Prophet's death, the Companions simply received and followed his guidance. Afterwards, they were forced into a new role: independent reasoning. This shift set the stage for all later fiqh development. Understanding this transition is essential for any mufti who must grapple with new cases that have no direct textual precedent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-death status
Recognize that during the Prophet's lifetime, the Companions needed no ijtihad; they relied on his direct instruction.
The passage emphasizes 'listening, following, and seeking fatwas'.
2
Recognize the sudden break after death
The Prophet's death removed the immediate reference, leaving the Quran and Sunnah as 'constitutional legacies'.
This is the epistemological shift from oral authority to textual authority.
3
Understand the societal pressures
The rapid expansion into Persia, Egypt, and Syria brought new cultures and complex social problems that demanded rulings not previously needed.
The 'inevitable necessity' of ijtihad arose from these practical challenges.
4
Outline the Companions' method
They first looked to the Quran, then to the Sunnah (including asking other Companions), and finally resorted to 'ray' (reasoned opinion) guided by Sharia objectives.
This three-step hierarchy prefigures later usul al-fiqh.
5
Apply the judge analogy
The Companions acted like a judge bound by legal texts: when no explicit text governed a case, they applied what was just and closest to the Lawgiver's intent.
This analogy remains foundational for muftis today.

الفصل الحادي عشر
الدور الفقهي الثاني عصر الخلفاء الراشدين
فما بعده إلى منتصف القرن الأول

١/١١ - قد انتقلت في هذا الدور حال علماء الصحابة من طور إلى
فهم في حياة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم كان شأنهم تلقياً عنه ووعياً لما يلقيه إليهم. فلم تكن بهم حاجة إلى إعمال الفكر في تفهم الأحكام المحتاجة إلى تبصر ونظر ذلك الإعمال المسمى بالاجتهاد، لأنَّ حمل هذا العبء الفكري مع احتمال الصواب والخطأ فيه لا معنى له مع وجود من إليه المرجع في كل شؤون الشريعة وأحكامها.
فشأنهم في حياة الرسول استماع واتباع واستفتاء منه فيما يشكل عليهم، وبتعبير آخر اعتماد على الرسول في الفهم والتوجيه في كل شيء. أما بعد وفاته عليه الصلاة والسلام فقد انتقلوا فجأة من طور الاعتماد إلى طَوْر الاجتهاد، لزوال ذلك المرجع، وحلول مخلفاته الدستورية - القرآن والسنة - محل بياناته الشفهية. فتولدت في ذلك الحين ضرورة إلى الاجتهاد لا مناص منها تجاه طوارىء الحوادث ولا حدود ولا أمد لها. فالمسلمون بعد وفاة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قد انسابوا فاتحين في ملك كسرى وقيصر لتحطيم الحواجز التي أقامها الملوك في وجه دعوة الإسلام ورسالته الإصلاحية في الأرض، فكانت تحت حكم المسلمين أمم ذات حضارات عريقة في مصر وفارس وسورية ثم شمال إفريقية، وماجت المدن الإسلامية الكبرى بأمشاج من الأمم وعناصر مختلفة الأجناس، فكان لا بد إذن من أن يجد في شؤون الاجتماع والسياسة أحداث تستدعي النظر فيما يصلح لها من الحلول الفقهية، وأن يحتاج المسلمون إلى نظم وأحكام من روح الإسلام لم يكن في عهد الرسول ما يضطرهم إليها، ويدفعهم إلى التفكير فيها.
لذلك كان لا بد لصحابة الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام من الاجتهاد في حلول تلك الأمور المعقدة التي تعرض لهم.
وقد كانت طريقتهم في اجتهادهم هذا أن يلجؤوا إلى كتاب الله، فإن وجدوا فيه الحكم فيما يريدون تمسكوا به، وإن لم يجدوه اتجهوا إلى المأثور عن رسول الله، واستثاروا ذاكرات أصحابه فيما يحفظون عنه من حكم في القضية فإن لم يكن بينهم من يحفظ حديثاً عن رسول الله في الموضوع التجؤوا إلى استعمال «الرأي» فاجتهدوا بآرائهم وحكموا أفهامهم فيما يرونه أشبه بالمعروف من مقاصد الشريعة وقواعدها في إقامة العدل واستقامة المصالح التي أوضحت سبلها.
ومثلهم في ذلك مثل القاضي المقيد بنصوص قانون، فإذا لم يجد في النص ما يحكم به في قضية بين يديه طبق ما يراه عدلاً وانصافاً أقرب إلى غرض الشارع.
Chapter Eleven: The Second Fiqh Era: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs and Beyond Until the Mid-First Century

1/11 - In this era, the state of the Companions as scholars transitioned from one stage to another. During the lifetime of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), their role was receiving from him and comprehending what he imparted to them. They had no need to exercise thought in understanding rulings that required insight and deliberation—that exercise called ijtihad—because bearing this intellectual burden, with its possibility of right and wrong, was meaningless given the presence of the one to whom reference is made in all affairs of the Sharia and its rulings.
So their role during the Prophet's life was listening, following, and seeking fatwas from him regarding what was unclear to them. In other words, they relied on the Prophet for understanding and guidance in everything. But after his death (peace be upon him), they suddenly moved from the stage of reliance to the stage of ijtihad, due to the disappearance of that reference, and his constitutional legacies—the Quran and Sunna—took the place of his oral statements. At that time, an unavoidable necessity for ijtihad arose in response to emerging incidents, with no limits or bounds. After the Prophet's death, Muslims spread out as conquerors in the kingdoms of Chosroes and Caesar to break the barriers that kings had erected against the call of Islam and its reformist mission on earth. Thus, nations with ancient civilizations in Egypt, Persia, Syria, and then North Africa came under Muslim rule, and the great Islamic cities teemed with a mixture of nations and elements of different races. Consequently, it was inevitable that events in social and political affairs would arise requiring consideration of appropriate fiqh solutions, and that Muslims would need systems and rulings from the spirit of Islam that had not necessitated them during the Prophet's era and did not compel them to think about them.
Therefore, it was inevitable for the Companions of the Messenger (peace be upon him) to engage in ijtihad to solve those complex issues that confronted them.
Their method in this ijtihad was to resort to the Book of Allah: if they found therein the ruling regarding what they wanted, they adhered to it; if they did not find it, they directed themselves to what was transmitted from the Messenger of Allah, and they stirred the memories of his Companions regarding what they preserved of rulings on the matter. If among them there was no one who remembered a hadith from the Messenger of Allah on the subject, they resorted to using 'al-ray' (opinion). Thus they exercised ijtihad with their opinions and judged with their understandings regarding what they deemed most similar to the well-known objectives of the Sharia and its principles in establishing justice and achieving the interests whose paths had been clarified.
In this, they were like a judge bound by legal texts: if he does not find in the text a ruling for a case before him, he applies what he deems just and fair and closer to the intent of the Lawgiver.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This is a historical analysis of the second era of Islamic fiqh, focusing on the Companions' transition from reliance on the Prophet to independent ijtihad. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that during the Prophet's lifetime, the Companions simply received and followed his instructions; there was no need for ijtihad because the Prophet was the ultimate authority. After his death, the absence of direct revelation forced them to derive rulings from the Quran and Sunna, and when those sources were silent, they used personal reasoning (ray) guided by Sharia objectives. This passage highlights the natural evolution of fiqh in response to new societal challenges, setting the stage for later juristic methodologies.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihād): The exercise of independent reasoning to derive rulings from the primary sources when no explicit text is found.
الرأي (al-ray): Personal opinion after reflection and seeking what is correct, used when no explicit text is available; broader than qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the primary reason the Companions had to resort to ijtihad after the Prophet's death?
A) They had forgotten many hadiths.
B) The Quran was incomplete.
C) New social and political problems required rulings not explicitly found in the texts.
D) They disagreed with the Prophet's teachings.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' shift from reliance to ijtihad established the methodological foundation for all subsequent Islamic legal reasoning, balancing textual authority with reasoned opinion.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Companions' Method of Ijtihad: From Text to Considered Opinion
Explains the Companions' method of ijtihad and definition of ra'y.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Companions' three-step methodology for deriving rulings and define ra'y as a disciplined juristic tool.

Core Idea
The Companions' ijtihad followed a clear hierarchy: Quran, then Sunnah, then ra'y — disciplined opinion grounded in maqasid.

After the Prophet ﷺ, new challenges required new rulings. The Companions developed a systematic approach: first search the Quran, then the Sunnah, and if no text existed, they used ra'y — but not as arbitrary opinion. This lesson unpacks that methodology and its philosophical foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical context
Why did the Companions need to expand their ijtihad after the Prophet's death? Consider the new social and political situations that arose.
The passage mentions 'complex matters' due to mixing of nations.
2
Trace the three-step hierarchy
What were the sequential sources they consulted? List them in order: Quran, Sunnah, ra'y.
3
Analyze the definition of ra'y
According to Ibn Qayyim, what constitutes ra'y? How is it different from casual opinion?
Ra'y is 'what the heart perceives after thought and reflection to know the correct aspect when indications conflict.'
4
Distinguish ra'y from qiyas
How is ra'y broader than analogical reasoning? What other methods does it include?
It includes istihsan and istislah.
5
Relate to Hanafi usul
Why does the Hanafi school accept ra'y as a legitimate source when texts are silent? How does this connect to the principle of istihsan?
Ra'y is the broader category; qiyas is a subset.

وقد أوضح ابن القيم في كتابه إعلام الموقعين» (٧٦/١) معنى.

أكثر مما يتعلق بالفقه نفسه، وهو أمران :

Chapter Three: Stages of the Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics

... by permission of the great Islamic cities with mixtures of nations and elements of different races, so it was inevitable that in social and political affairs events would occur requiring consideration of appropriate juristic solutions, and that Muslims would need systems and rulings from the spirit of Islam for which there was no necessity in the time of the Messenger that would compel them toward them and drive them to think about them.

Therefore, it was inevitable for the Companions of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him, to engage in ijtihad for solutions to those complex matters that confronted them.

Their method in this ijtihad was to resort to the Book of Allah; if they found therein the ruling for what they sought, they adhered to it. If they did not find it, they turned to what was transmitted from the Messenger of Allah, and they stimulated the memories of his Companions regarding what they preserved of rulings on the issue. If there was none among them who remembered a hadith from the Messenger of Allah on the subject, they resorted to using 'ra'y' (considered opinion). Thus they exerted ijtihad with their opinions and judged according to their understandings, based on what they deemed most consistent with the well-known objectives of the Shari'ah and its principles in establishing justice and upholding the interests whose ways have been clarified.

They are in that like a judge bound by legal texts; if he does not find in the text a ruling for a case before him, he applies what he sees as justice and fairness closest to the intent of the Lawgiver.

2/11 - Meaning of Ra'y:
Ibn Qayyim clarified in his book 'I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in' (1/76) the meaning of juristic ra'y as understood by the Companions, stating that ra'y is: what the heart perceives after thought, reflection, and seeking to know the correct aspect when indications are contradictory.

Ra'y in this sense is broader than the specific concept of juristic qiyas, which is attaching a matter not ruled upon in the texts to a matter ruled upon due to a common effective cause, because ra'y in this sense includes the mentioned qiyas and also includes istihsan and istislah, whose explanation has preceded in the discussion of subsidiary sources.

Chapter (11) The Second Era: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs

Ibn Qayyim divided this ra'y into sound, invalid, and ambiguous, distinguished between these divisions, and applied to them accordingly what is reported from the Companions about censuring ra'y at times and acting upon it at other times. However, the jurisprudence in this second era did not change from its predecessor; jurisprudence remained, as it was in the era of the message, realistic and practical, dealing with events by seeking their rulings after their occurrence in accordance with what the texts of the Book and Sunnah guide to and what the Islamic interests that the Lawgiver has cared for suggest.

3/11 - The prominent thing that distinguishes this era concerns the sources of jurisprudence more than jurisprudence itself, and it is two matters:

First: The use of ra'y and qiyas prominently in extracting rulings for unprecedented events due to encountering ijtihad. Ijtihad and ra'y during the life of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him, were practiced by the Companions only rarely and temporarily, as previously explained.

Second: The generation of ijma' (consensus), for the method of the first two caliphs, Abu Bakr and 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, was to gather the Companions for events, consult them, and act upon what they agreed upon, as has preceded.

11/4 - The Outstanding Ijtihad of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab in This Era

This era was also distinguished by outstanding ijtihad of the second caliph 'Umar ibn al-Khattab in specific events, in which the juristic genius of this great caliph became manifest, suitable to be a constitution for the methodology of realistic fiqh. Among that:

1 - He prevented those whose hearts were to be reconciled [mu'allafatu qulubuhum] who existed in his time from receiving the regular stipend from the treasury, even though giving to them is established by the text of the Quran, and these individuals had been receiving this since the time of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him. But 'Umar looked at the underlying reason (illah) of the text, not its literal meaning; indeed the reason for giving them was ...
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm opens the third chapter on the stages of Islamic jurisprudence. It explains how the Companions, after the Prophet's death, faced new social and political challenges requiring juristic solutions. Their methodology followed a clear hierarchy: first the Quran, then the Sunnah, then ra'y (considered opinion). Ra'y is defined by Ibn Qayyim as a reasoned opinion after deep thought when different indications conflict. This ra'y is broader than qiyas (analogy) and includes istihsan and istislah. The passage then focuses on the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, noting two key developments: the prominent use of ra'y and qiyas, and the emergence of ijma' (consensus) as a source. Finally, it highlights Umar ibn al-Khattab's notable ijtihad, such as suspending the share for mu'allafatu qulubuhum (those whose hearts are to be reconciled) based on the underlying reason (illah) of the Quranic text, demonstrating a maqasid-oriented approach.

Key Terms
الرأي (ar-ra'y): What the heart perceives after thought and reflection to determine the correct aspect when indications are contradictory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Qayyim's definition in the passage, what is 'ra'y' in the juristic sense?
A) Any personal opinion without restriction.
B) What the heart perceives after thought and reflection to know the correct aspect when indications conflict.
C) Strict analogical reasoning (qiyas) only.
D) The literal meaning of the Quran and Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' ijtihad was not unguided opinion; it followed a structured hierarchy ending with ra'y, a disciplined juristic opinion rooted in maqasid.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Emergence of Ijma and Umar's Maqasid Ijtihad
Explains the emergence of ijma and Umar's ijtihad on mu'allafatu qulubuhum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how ijma developed as a source in the Rashidun era and analyze Umar's ijtihad on mu'allafatu qulubuhum as an application of illah-based reasoning.

Core Idea
Ijma was born from the practice of consulting all Companions; Umar's ijtihad on mu'allafatu qulubuhum shows that the spirit of the law can override its literal application.

The era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs saw two major developments: the formal emergence of ijma (consensus) and the application of ijtihad based on maqasid, as exemplified by Umar's decisive action regarding the mu'allafatu qulubuhum. This lesson explores both.

Guided Path
1
Describe the method of Abu Bakr and Umar in gathering Companions
How did the Caliphs generate consensus? According to the passage, they would collect the Companions for events, consult them, and act upon what they agreed upon.
This method of consultation is the basis for ijma.
2
Define ijma as a source
How does the passage present its role? It mentions 'the generation of ijma' as a prominent feature of this era.
Ijma becomes a binding source of law after the Prophet's death.
3
Examine Umar's ijtihad on mu'allafatu qulubuhum
What did Umar do, and why? He prevented those whose hearts are to be reconciled from receiving the stipend, even though the Quran prescribes it.
The Quranic text (Surah 9:60) includes mu'allafatu qulubuhum as a category for zakat.
4
Analyze his reasoning: illah vs. literal text
How did Umar look beyond the apparent meaning? The passage says he looked at the 'illah (underlying reason) of the text, not its literal meaning.
The illah for giving them was to reconcile their hearts to Islam; when Islam was strong, the illah ceased, so the ruling changed.
5
Connect to Hanafi usul
How does this reflect Hanafi principles? Hanafis hold that rulings follow their effective causes (al-hukm yaduru ma'a al-illah wujudan wa 'adaman). Umar's action is a classic application.
This is a foundation for istihsan and maqasid-based ijtihad.

وقد أوضح ابن القيم في كتابه إعلام الموقعين» (٧٦/١) معنى.
«الرأي» الفقهي على حد ما كان يفهمه الصحابة، فأفاد أنّ الرأي هو : ما يراه القلب بعد فكر وتأمل وطلب لمعرفة وجه الصواب مما تتعارض فيه الأمارات .
والرأي بهذا المعنى أوسع من مفهوم القياس الفقهي الخاص، وهو إلحاق أمر غير منصوص على حكمه بأمر منصوص على حكمه لعلة مشتركة جامعة بينهما لأن الرأي بهذا المعنى يشمل القياس المذكور : ويشمل أيضاً الاستحسان والاستصلاح اللذين تقدم بيانهما في بحث المصادر
التبعية .
الفصل (۱۱) الدور الثاني : عصر الخلفاء الراشدين
وقد قسم ابن القيم هذا الرأي إلى صحيح وباطل ومشتبه، وميز بين هذه الأقسام، ونَزَّل عليها بالتوزيع ما ورد عن الصحابة من ذم الرأي تارة، والعمل به تارة أخرى. غير أنَّ منهاج الفقه في هذا العصر الثاني لم يتبدل عن سابقه، فقد ظل الفقه، كما كان في عصر الرسالة واقعياً وعملياً تعالج فيه الحوادث بالتماس أحكامها بعد وقوعها على وفق ما ترشد إليه نصوص الكتاب والسنة، وتوحي بفهمه المصالح الإسلامية التي رعاها الشارع. ۳/۱۱ - والشيء البارز الذي يتميز به هذا العصر يتعلق بمصادر الفقه
أكثر مما يتعلق بالفقه نفسه، وهو أمران :
الأول : استعمال الرأي والقياس بصورة بارزة في استخراج الأحكام
لما لم يسبق من الحوادث بسبب مواجهة الاجتهاد. وقد كان الاجتهاد والرأي في حياة الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام لا
يمارسه الصحابة إلا قليلاً وموقتاً كما سبق بيانه .
والثاني : تولد الإجماع، إذ كان منهاج الخليفتين الأولين أبي بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما جمع الصحابة في الحوادث، واستفتاءهم، والعمل بما يتفقون عليه كما تقدم.

وقد تميز هذ العصر أيضاً باجتهادات بارزة للخليفة الثاني عمر بن الخطاب في حوادث معينة تجلت فيها لهذا الخليفة العظيم عبقرية فقهية تصلح أن تكون دستوراً لمنهاج الفقه الواقعي. فمن ذلك :

١٧٥
Chapter Three: Stages of the Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics

... by permission of the great Islamic cities with mixtures of nations and elements of different races, so it was inevitable that in social and political affairs events would occur requiring consideration of appropriate juristic solutions, and that Muslims would need systems and rulings from the spirit of Islam for which there was no necessity in the time of the Messenger that would compel them toward them and drive them to think about them.

Therefore, it was inevitable for the Companions of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him, to engage in ijtihad for solutions to those complex matters that confronted them.

Their method in this ijtihad was to resort to the Book of Allah; if they found therein the ruling for what they sought, they adhered to it. If they did not find it, they turned to what was transmitted from the Messenger of Allah, and they stimulated the memories of his Companions regarding what they preserved of rulings on the issue. If there was none among them who remembered a hadith from the Messenger of Allah on the subject, they resorted to using 'ra'y' (considered opinion). Thus they exerted ijtihad with their opinions and judged according to their understandings, based on what they deemed most consistent with the well-known objectives of the Shari'ah and its principles in establishing justice and upholding the interests whose ways have been clarified.

They are in that like a judge bound by legal texts; if he does not find in the text a ruling for a case before him, he applies what he sees as justice and fairness closest to the intent of the Lawgiver.

2/11 - Meaning of Ra'y:
Ibn Qayyim clarified in his book 'I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in' (1/76) the meaning of juristic ra'y as understood by the Companions, stating that ra'y is: what the heart perceives after thought, reflection, and seeking to know the correct aspect when indications are contradictory.

Ra'y in this sense is broader than the specific concept of juristic qiyas, which is attaching a matter not ruled upon in the texts to a matter ruled upon due to a common effective cause, because ra'y in this sense includes the mentioned qiyas and also includes istihsan and istislah, whose explanation has preceded in the discussion of subsidiary sources.

Chapter (11) The Second Era: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs

Ibn Qayyim divided this ra'y into sound, invalid, and ambiguous, distinguished between these divisions, and applied to them accordingly what is reported from the Companions about censuring ra'y at times and acting upon it at other times. However, the jurisprudence in this second era did not change from its predecessor; jurisprudence remained, as it was in the era of the message, realistic and practical, dealing with events by seeking their rulings after their occurrence in accordance with what the texts of the Book and Sunnah guide to and what the Islamic interests that the Lawgiver has cared for suggest.

3/11 - The prominent thing that distinguishes this era concerns the sources of jurisprudence more than jurisprudence itself, and it is two matters:

First: The use of ra'y and qiyas prominently in extracting rulings for unprecedented events due to encountering ijtihad. Ijtihad and ra'y during the life of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him, were practiced by the Companions only rarely and temporarily, as previously explained.

Second: The generation of ijma' (consensus), for the method of the first two caliphs, Abu Bakr and 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, was to gather the Companions for events, consult them, and act upon what they agreed upon, as has preceded.

11/4 - The Outstanding Ijtihad of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab in This Era

This era was also distinguished by outstanding ijtihad of the second caliph 'Umar ibn al-Khattab in specific events, in which the juristic genius of this great caliph became manifest, suitable to be a constitution for the methodology of realistic fiqh. Among that:

1 - He prevented those whose hearts were to be reconciled [mu'allafatu qulubuhum] who existed in his time from receiving the regular stipend from the treasury, even though giving to them is established by the text of the Quran, and these individuals had been receiving this since the time of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him. But 'Umar looked at the underlying reason (illah) of the text, not its literal meaning; indeed the reason for giving them was ...
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm opens the third chapter on the stages of Islamic jurisprudence. It explains how the Companions, after the Prophet's death, faced new social and political challenges requiring juristic solutions. Their methodology followed a clear hierarchy: first the Quran, then the Sunnah, then ra'y (considered opinion). Ra'y is defined by Ibn Qayyim as a reasoned opinion after deep thought when different indications conflict. This ra'y is broader than qiyas (analogy) and includes istihsan and istislah. The passage then focuses on the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, noting two key developments: the prominent use of ra'y and qiyas, and the emergence of ijma' (consensus) as a source. Finally, it highlights Umar ibn al-Khattab's notable ijtihad, such as suspending the share for mu'allafatu qulubuhum (those whose hearts are to be reconciled) based on the underlying reason (illah) of the Quranic text, demonstrating a maqasid-oriented approach.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijma'): The agreement of the Companions on a ruling after consultation, considered a binding source of law.
العلة (al-illah): The underlying reason or effective cause for a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Umar prevent the mu'allafatu qulubuhum from receiving the stipend?
A) He forgot the Quranic verse.
B) He looked at the underlying reason (illah) of the text.
C) He thought the recipients were wealthy.
D) He wanted to punish them.

Key Takeaway
Umar's ijtihad on mu'allafatu qulubuhum demonstrates that the effective cause (illah) can override literal application, a key principle in Hanafi usul.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
7 min
Umar's Ijtihad on Mu'allafatu Qulubuhum: Considering 'Illa over Zāhir
Explaining Umar's ijtihad based on 'illa and the principle of maslaha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of considering 'illa over zāhir in ijtihad, using the case of mu'allafatu qulubuhum, and construct a fatwa that respects the permanence of the text while adapting to changing circumstances.

Core Idea
The ruling remains in the text; only its application changes when the cause (illa) ceases.

This lesson examines a landmark ijtihad of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab: his decision to stop payments to the category of 'those whose hearts are to be reconciled' (mu'allafatu qulubuhum) despite a clear Qur'anic provision and Prophetic practice. Through this case, we learn a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: the ruling (hukm) is tied to its effective cause ('illa). When the cause disappears, the application may be suspended, but the text remains operative for future needs. This demonstrates how ijtihad can balance textual authority with real-world maslaha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its textual basis
Locate the Qur'anic provision for mu'allafatu qulubuhum and note that the Prophet applied it. Recognize that Umar was faced with a situation where the text commanded giving, but he chose to withhold.
The verse is in Surah al-Tawbah (9:60). The Prophet allocated a share of zakah and state funds to this category.
2
Determine the effective cause ('illa) of the ruling
Analyze the purpose behind giving to mu'allafatu qulubuhum. The 'illa was to protect the nascent Islamic state by winning over influential non-Muslims or neutralizing their hostility.
Umar understood that the cause was the weakness of Islam at the time.
3
Assess whether the 'illa still exists
Evaluate the changed circumstances. By Umar's caliphate, Islam had become strong and established; the need to buy loyalty or prevent harm had diminished.
Umar concluded that the 'illa had ceased for those specific individuals.
4
Apply the principle: if 'illa ceases, the ruling is suspended but not abrogated
Distinguish between suspension of application (ta'til al-hukm) and abrogation (naskh). Umar's action did not cancel the verse; he only stopped payments because the cause was absent.
The ruling remains in the text, ready to be reactivated if the 'illa returns.
5
Draft a fatwa based on the Hanafi method
Using the case, construct a fatwa that acknowledges the permanent ruling but applies it conditionally: give when the cause exists, withhold when it does not. Consider modern equivalents (e.g., political propaganda funds).
The fatwa must cite the text, identify the 'illa, and show the link between cause and application.

تخصصها الدول اليوم في ميزانياتها لأجل الدعاية السياسية. فإذا انقضت حاجة الدعاية في مكان أو في فئة لا يسوغ عندئذ صرف : المال إلى من لا تحتاج الدعاية إليه، بل يوفّر المال على خزينة الدولة ريثما تتجدد الحاجة. وقد يتغير وجه الحاجة بين عصر وعصر، فيستغنى عن إعطاء أشخاص كانوا يعطون لوجاهتهم ونفوذهم كما كان في الماضي، ويحتاج إلى إعطاء أرباب الصحف، أو إنشاء مجلات دعاية أو محطة إذاعة لاسلكية، كما في عصرنا اليوم. قال الإمام أبو عبيد القاسم بن سلام في كتابه «الأموال» بمناسبة ما اشترطه النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لبني ثقيف أهل الطائف عند دخولهم في الإسلام ما نصه: «وفيه أنه شرط لهم شروطاً عند إسلامهم خاصة لهم دون الناس مثل تحريمه واديهم، وأن لا يدخله أحد يغلبهم عليه، وأن لا يؤمر عليهم إلا بعضهم. وهذا مما قلت لك : إنّ الإمام ناظر للإسلام وأهله فإذا خاف من عدو غلبة لا يقدر على دفعهم إلا بعطية يردهم بها فعل كالذي صنع النبي له بالأحزاب يوم الخندق . وكذلك لو أبوا أن يسلموا إلا على شيء يجعله لهم، وكان في إسلامهم عز للإسلام، ولم يأمن معرتهم وبأسهم أعطاهم ذلك ليتألفهم ،به كما فعل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بالمؤلفة قلوبهم إلى أن يرغبوا في الإسلام، وتحسن فيه نيتهم. وإنما يجوز من هذا ما لم يكن فيه نقض للكتاب ولا للسنة. ويبين ذلك أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يجعل لهم فيما أعطاهم تحليل الربا، ألا تراه قد شَرَط عليهم أن لهم رؤوس أموالهم؟). (الأموال، ۱۹۳/۱ - ١٩٤ الطبعة الأولى بمطبعة الحجازي بالقاهرة.
Chapter (11) The Second Era: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. Ibn al-Qayyim divided this opinion into correct, false, and ambiguous, and distinguished between these categories, and applied to them by distribution what was reported from the Companions of condemning opinion at times and acting upon it at other times. However, the methodology of jurisprudence in this second era did not change from its predecessor; jurisprudence remained, as it was in the era of the message, realistic and practical, dealing with incidents by seeking their rulings after their occurrence according to what the texts of the Book and the Sunnah guide to, and what the Islamic interests that the Lawgiver took into account suggest. 3/11 - The prominent thing that distinguishes this era relates to the sources of jurisprudence more than to jurisprudence itself, and it is two matters: First: The prominent use of opinion and analogy in extracting rulings for unprecedented incidents due to facing ijtihad. Ijtihad and opinion during the life of the Messenger, peace be upon him, were practiced by the Companions only rarely and temporarily, as previously explained. Second: The emergence of consensus, for the methodology of the first two caliphs, Abu Bakr and Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, was to gather the Companions regarding incidents, seek their opinions, and act upon what they agreed upon, as previously mentioned. 11/4 - The methodology of the prominent ijtihads of Umar ibn al-Khattab in this era. This era was also distinguished by prominent ijtihads of the second caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab regarding specific incidents in which the juristic genius of this great caliph manifested, worthy of being a constitution for the methodology of realistic jurisprudence. Among that: 1 - His prevention of the people whose hearts are to be reconciled (mu'allafatu qulubuhum) who existed in his time from what they used to receive from the treasury of a regular stipend, even though giving to those whose hearts are to be reconciled is established by the text of the Qur'an, and these people had been taking this stipend since the time of the Messenger, peace be upon him. But Umar looked at the cause of the text, not its literal wording. For the cause of giving them was to reconcile them to avoid their evil when Islam was weak. When the strength of Islam became firm, the reason for giving them ceased. The Qur'an does not obligate giving to specific individuals by their names from this share. (1) (1) This does not mean that Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, abolished or suspended a commanding Qur'anic text, but he saw that those people who were considered among those whose hearts are to be reconciled and the Prophet gave them this share from the treasury due to the need of the Islamic call to win them over, support them, and reconcile their hearts, Islam no longer had a need to buy their support with money after its authority became established, and they became in need of being honored by it. So he only withheld the stipend from those specific individuals. As for its ruling in the Qur'an, it remains, not suspended. If the need arises for the nation at another time or place to reconcile hearts, or to mobilize forces to repel an enemy, or to win over someone whom lofty virtues do not attract as money attracts, then it is given from this share allocated for this need, like the expenditures that states today allocate in their budgets for political propaganda. When the need for propaganda ceases in a place or among a group, it is not permissible then to spend money on those whom propaganda does not need, but rather the money is saved in the state treasury until the need arises again. The aspect of need may change between eras, so it may be unnecessary to give to individuals who were given due to their status and influence in the past, and it may be necessary to give to newspaper owners, or to establish propaganda magazines or a radio station, as in our era today. Imam Abu Ubayd al-Qasim ibn Sallam said in his book 'Al-Amwal' regarding the condition that the Prophet, peace be upon him, stipulated for Banu Thaqif, the people of Ta'if, when they entered Islam: 'In it, he stipulated conditions for them upon their Islam specifically for them, not for others, such as declaring their valley sacred, that no one should enter it to overcome them, and that only one of them should be appointed over them. And this is what I told you: The Imam is the guardian of Islam and its people. If he fears from an enemy a domination that he cannot repel except by a gift that turns them away, he does so, as the Prophet did with the confederates on the day of the trench. Likewise, if they refuse to accept Islam except upon something that he gives them, and in their Islam there is strength for Islam, and he does not feel safe from their harm and violence, he gives them that to reconcile them, as the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, did with those whose hearts are to be reconciled until they desire Islam and their intention in it becomes good. And this is only permissible as long as it does not involve a violation of the Book or the Sunnah. This is shown by the fact that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, did not make permissible for them in what he gave them the analysis of usury; do you not see that he stipulated to them that they have their principal amounts? (Al-Amwal, 1/193-194, first edition, Hijazi Press, Cairo.)
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' discusses the second era of Islamic jurisprudence—the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. The author notes that the methodology remained realist and practical, addressing events as they occurred. Two prominent features distinguish this era: the prominent use of legal opinion (ra'y) and analogy (qiyas) for novel cases, and the emergence of consensus (ijma) through the practice of the first two caliphs consulting the companions. The author then focuses on the remarkable ijtihad of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab. One example is his decision to stop the regular stipend to the category of 'those whose hearts are to be reconciled' (mu'allafatu qulubuhum), despite the Qur'anic provision and the practice of the Prophet. The key is that Umar looked at the underlying cause (illa) of the ruling, not its literal wording. The cause was to protect the nascent Islamic state from harm by buying loyalty. Once Islam became strong, the cause ceased, so the application stopped. However, the Qur'anic ruling remains; if the need arises again—for example, to counter a powerful enemy or to win over influential people in a modern context—the state may resume payments to suitable recipients. Imam Abu Ubayd's comment reinforces that such expenditures are permissible if they serve the interests of Islam and do not contradict the Book and Sunnah. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle of considering 'illa and maslaha in ijtihad, without abrogating the text.

Key Terms
المؤلفة قلوبهم (al-mu'allafatu qulubuhum): Those whose hearts are to be reconciled—a category eligible for zakah or state funds to win them over to Islam or to avert their harm.
علة (illa): The effective cause or rationale behind a legal ruling, which determines its application.
مصلحة (maslaha): Public interest or benefit that the Lawgiver intends to realize, guiding ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Caliph Umar stop giving the stipend to the mu'allafatu qulubuhum?
A) He considered the verse abrogated.
B) He considered the verse inapplicable because the cause (weakness of Islam) no longer existed.
C) He disliked giving money to non-Muslims.
D) He wanted to save money for other purposes.

Key Takeaway
Umar's ijtihad exemplifies that the ruling in the text is permanent, but its application depends on the existence of its effective cause ('illa).

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
10 min
Umar's Ijtihad on Conquered Lands: Fay' vs Ghanima
Historical case study of Umar's ijtihad on disposition of conquered lands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ghanima and fay' in Hanafi fiqh, and evaluate Umar's ijtihad as a model of maslaha-based reasoning in public finance.

Core Idea
Conquered lands are fay' for all Muslims, not ghanima for the few — a permanent endowment for the community.

The conquest of Sawad al-Iraq presented a critical legal question: should the fertile lands be divided among the victorious army as war booty, or preserved for the benefit of the entire Muslim community, present and future? Umar ibn al-Khattab's ijtihad, supported by a minority of leading Companions, ultimately shaped Hanafi law on land tenure. This lesson explores the Quranic basis for the distinction between ghanima and fay', the arguments presented by Umar and his opponents, and the enduring principle that conquered lands become a public trust administered for the common good.

Guided Path
1
Define ghanima and fay'
Identify the key differences: ghanima is taken by force of arms in battle; fay' is obtained without combat, such as through surrender or tribute. Note that land conquered by force (like Sawad) can be treated as fay' according to Umar's ijtihad, based on the Quranic verse in Surah al-Hashr.
The Prophet's treatment of Khaybar land is a precedent, but Umar distinguished Sawad based on the broader principle.
2
Analyze Umar's consultation with the Companions
Understand that most Companions initially favored division as ghanima, citing Khaybar. Umar, along with a minority (Ali, Uthman, Talha, Mu'adh), argued for retention as fay'. Mu'adh's warning about concentrating wealth in a few hands highlights the maslaha (public interest) reasoning.
The principle 'so that it does not become a circulating currency among the rich' (Quran 59:7) is directly invoked.
3
Identify Umar's Quranic proof
Umar found his decisive argument in the last verse of Surah al-Hashr (59:10) which includes 'those who came after them' as beneficiaries of fay'. This shows that future generations have a claim on these lands, preventing exclusive ownership by the conquerors.
This is a powerful example of istinbat (deduction) from the Quranic text.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: dividing the land would make it private property; keeping it as fay' imposes kharaj (land tax) and the land remains an endowment for the public treasury. The Hanafi school adopts Umar's position, making such lands waqf for all Muslims.
This ruling has shaped Islamic land policy for centuries.
5
Extract the governing qawida
The case illustrates the maxim: 'تصرف الإمام على الرعية منوط بالمصلحة' (The Imam's actions concerning the subjects are dependent on public interest). Umar's decision was based on what he saw as the greater good for the entire Muslim community, balancing the rights of the conquerors with those of future generations.
This qawida is central to Hanafi public law and ijtihad.

ملحق بالفصل الحادي عشر
اجتهاد عمر في شأن
الأراضي المفتوحة
١١/ ٥ - بينا في الفصل / ۱۱ / أنَّ اجتهاد عمر رضي الله عنه كان عدم تقسيم أراضي سواد العراق وأراضي مصر على الفاتحين كما تقسم الغنائم الحربية. وفيما يلي بيان ذلك :
لقد وردت آية الغنائم في سورة الأنفال تقول: ﴿وَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّمَا غَنِمْتُم مِّن شَيْءٍ فَأَنَّ لِلَّهِ مُمْسَهُ وَلِلرَّسُولِ وَلِذِي الْقُرْبَى وَالْيَتَمَى وَالْمَسَكِينِ وَابْنِ السَّبِيلِ﴾ (٤١/٨)
فعينت الآية مصرف الخمس من الغنائم الحربية لجهات عامة، وبينت السنة بفعل النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام كيفية توزيع الباقي بين الغانمين.
ثم جاءت آية الفيء في سورة الحشر تقول:
﴿مَا أَفَاءَ اللهُ عَلَى رَسُولِهِ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْقُرَى فَلِلَّهِ وَلِلرَّسُولِ وَلِذِي الْقُرْبَى وَالْيَتَمَى وَالْمَسَكِينِ وَابْنِ السَّبِيلِ كَي لَا يَكُونَ دُولَةٌ بَيْنَ الْأَغْنِيَاءِ مِنكُمْ وَمَا الَكُمُ الرَّسُولُ فَخُذُوهُ وَمَا نَهَنكُمْ عَنْهُ فَانتَهُوا وَاتَّقُوا اللهَ إِنَّ اللَّهَ شَدِيدُ الْعِقَابِ * لِلْفُقَرَاءِ الْمُهَاجِرِينَ الَّذِينَ أُخْرِجُوا مِن دِيَارِهِمْ وَالَّذِينَ تَبَوَّهُ وَ الدَّارَ وَالْإِيمَنَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ ... وَالَّذِينَ جَاءُو مِنْ بَعْدِهِمْ﴾
فأفادت هذه الآية أنَّ الفيء يفيد منه جميع المسلمين حاضرهم وآتيهم ولا يختص بالمجاهدين الفاتحين بل يوضع في بيت المال.
والفرق بين الغنيمة والفيء في الاصطلاح الشرعي أن الغنيمة ما استخلصت من العدو المحارب بقوة السلاح، والفيء ما تركه الأعداء عن رعب، أو أدوه عن قهر وغلبة ولم يؤخذ منهم بإيجاف الخيل وصولة الجيش في المعارك الحربية فيشمل الفيء الضرائب التي تفرض عليهم كالجزية على الأشخاص، والخراج على الأراضي.
وقد ثبت في السنة أنه عندما فتح النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام خيبر وقد فتحت عنوة لا صلحاً، ونزل أهلها المحاربون على حكم الجلاء - عُدَّت أراضيها من الفيء وعزل النبي نصفها (وقيل خمسها)، فتركه للنوائب والنوازل، وقسم الباقي بين الفاتحين.
فلما فتح سواد العراق في خلافة عمر طالب الفاتحون بتقسيم أراضيه بينهم كالغنائم، وكان رأي عمر عدم جواز تقسيمها، فجمع الناس واستشارهم. فكان رأي عامتهم تقسيمها إلا علياً وعثمان وطلحة ومعاذ بن جبل، فقد كان رأيهم كرأي عمر. وقال له معاذ :
إنك إن قسمتها صار الريع العظيم في أيدي هؤلاء القوم. ثم يبيدون فيصير ذلك إلى الرجل الواحد أو المرأة ثم يأتي من بعدهم قوم يسدون من الإسلام مسدّاً ـ أي يكون لهم في الإسلام بلاء حسن ونفع . وهم لا يجدون شيئاً، فانظر أمراً يسع أولهم وآخرهم».
فالح الفاتحون في طلب تقسيم الأرضين عليهم بعد تخميسها، بحجة ما فعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بخيبر، وامتنع عمر عن تقسيمها فرأى أن البلاد المحيطة قد تم فتحها، وأن الأراضي فيء فلا يستحق الفاتحون ملك أعيانها، بل للإمام النظر فيها بما هو أصلح لإحيائها وأدوم نفعاً لجماعة المسلمين، وإنما قسم النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام ما قسمه من أرض خيبر إذ رأى المصلحة في ذلك لحاجتهم حينذاك إلى الأرض.
فمكثوا يتناقشون أياماً حتى قال عمر وجدت الحجة عليهم بآخر آية الحشر إذ قالت في بيان من يستحقون من الفيء: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ جَاءُو مِنْ بَعْدِهِمْ﴾
Appendix to Chapter Eleven
Umar's Ijtihad Regarding Conquered Lands
11/5 - We explained in Chapter 11 that the ijtihad of Umar (RA) was not to divide the lands of Sawad al-Iraq and the lands of Egypt among the conquerors as war booty is divided. The following is an exposition of that:
The verse of booty in Surah al-Anfal says: "Know that whatever you take as booty, for Allah is one-fifth, and for the Messenger, and for the near relative, and the orphans, the needy, and the wayfarer" (8:41). So the verse specified the expenditure of the one-fifth of war booty for public purposes, and the Sunnah by the practice of the Prophet (SAW) clarified how to distribute the remainder among the conquerors.
Then came the verse of fay' in Surah al-Hashr saying: "What Allah has bestowed on His Messenger from the people of the towns — it is for Allah, for the Messenger, for the near relative, the orphans, the needy, and the wayfarer, so that it does not become a circulating currency among the rich among you. And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it; and whatever he forbids you, desist. And fear Allah; indeed Allah is severe in punishment. For the poor emigrants who were expelled from their homes... and those who settled in the city and believed before them... and those who came after them." So this verse indicated that fay' benefits all Muslims, present and future, and is not specific to the conquerors; rather it is placed in the treasury.
The difference between ghanima and fay' in legal terminology is that ghanima is what is taken from the fighting enemy by force of arms, while fay' is what the enemy abandons out of fear, or pays under coercion and domination, and is not taken by galloping horses and the army's assault in battles. Thus fay' includes taxes imposed on them, such as jizya on persons and kharaj on lands.
It is established in the Sunnah that when the Prophet (SAW) conquered Khaybar — which was conquered by force, not by treaty, and its fighting inhabitants agreed to evacuation — its lands were considered fay'. The Prophet set aside half (or one-fifth, it is said) and left it for emergencies and calamities, and distributed the rest among the conquerors.
When Sawad al-Iraq was conquered during the caliphate of Umar, the conquerors demanded the division of its lands among them like booty. Umar's opinion was that it was not permissible to divide them. So he gathered the people and consulted them. The opinion of the majority was to divide it, except for Ali, Uthman, Talha, and Mu'adh ibn Jabal, whose opinion was like Umar's. Mu'adh said to him: "If you divide it, great revenue will come into the hands of these people. Then they will perish, and it will go to one man or one woman. Then later there will come people who have a great role in Islam (i.e., good deeds and benefit) but they will find nothing. So consider a matter that encompasses both the first and the last."
The conquerors persisted in demanding the division of the lands after taking out one-fifth, arguing by what the Prophet (SAW) did at Khaybar. Umar refused to divide them, holding that the surrounding lands had been conquered and that the lands are fay', so the conquerors are not entitled to ownership of the lands themselves. Rather, the Imam has discretion to consider what is most beneficial for their cultivation and most enduring in benefit for the community of Muslims. The Prophet (SAW) divided what he divided of Khaybar because he saw the benefit in that, due to their need at that time for land.
They remained debating for days until Umar said: "I have found the proof against them in the last part of Surah al-Hashr, where it says regarding those who are entitled to fay': 'And those who came after them.'"
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a historical case study of Umar ibn al-Khattab's ijtihad regarding the disposition of conquered lands, which became a foundational ruling in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by presenting the Quranic verses on ghanima (booty) from Surah al-Anfal and fay' (spoils obtained without combat) from Surah al-Hashr. He defines the two terms: ghanima is taken by force of arms; fay' is acquired without direct combat, such as by surrender or tribute payments like jizya and kharaj. He then cites the precedent of Khaybar, where the Prophet considered the land as fay' and divided only part of it among the conquerors, keeping part for public needs. When Umar faced the conquest of Sawad al-Iraq, the soldiers demanded division of the land as ghanima, citing Khaybar. Umar consulted the Companions; the majority agreed with the soldiers, but a minority including Ali, Uthman, Talha, and Mu'adh agreed with Umar. Mu'adh's argument was that dividing the land would concentrate wealth in a few hands, leaving later Muslims, who would serve Islam greatly, with nothing. Umar ultimately refrained from dividing, reasoning that the lands are fay' and thus belong to the entire Muslim community, past and future. He found his proof in the verse of al-Hashr that includes "those who come after them," indicating future generations have a share in fay'. This ruling became the basis for the Hanafi position that conquered lands are not distributed as private property but remain as an endowment (waqf) for the public treasury, with the produce used for the benefit of all Muslims.

Key Terms
الفيء (al-fay'): Wealth obtained from non-Muslims without actual combat, such as through surrender, tribute, or taxes like jizya and kharaj
الغنيمة (al-ghanima): Booty taken from the enemy in battle by force of arms
الخراج (al-kharaj): Land tax imposed on conquered agricultural lands, considered part of fay'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the key Quranic proof Umar used to justify treating conquered lands as fay' rather than dividing them as ghanima?
A) The verse on ghanima in Surah al-Anfal (8:41)
B) The verse on fay' in Surah al-Hashr (59:10) that includes 'those who came after them'
C) The verse on consultation in Surah al-Shura
D) The Prophet's division of Khaybar land as a precedent

Key Takeaway
Umar's ijtihad established that conquered lands are fay' for the entire Muslim community, forming the basis of the Hanafi ruling that such lands become a public endowment administered for the common good.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
Umar's Ijtihad on Conquered Lands: Three Madhhabs and Siyasa Shar'iyya
Describes Umar's ijtihad and three madhhabs on conquered lands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three major madhhab positions on conquered lands and apply the usul principle of siyasa shar'iyya to distinguish between prophetic legislation and administrative policy.

Core Idea
Umar's ijtihad on land was Quranic, and the Hanafi imam retains discretion via siyasa shar'iyya.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa presents a pivotal historical moment: Caliph Umar's decision to treat conquered lands differently from movable booty. This episode demonstrates how ijtihad operates within the framework of Shariah and gave rise to three distinct positions among the major schools. More importantly, the passage introduces a key usul al-fiqh distinction between the Prophet's legislative rulings and his administrative policies, known as siyasa shar'iyya. Understanding this distinction is critical for any mufti dealing with state affairs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical incident and Umar's ruling
Understand the context: after the conquest of Sawad (Iraq), Umar decided not to distribute the land as movable booty but to leave it with its owners and impose kharaj. He based this on a Quranic verse about provision for future generations.
Umar's opponents wanted division like movable booty; he argued the verse required preserving land for later Muslims.
2
Compare the three madhhab positions
The Shafi'i school holds that conquered lands are divided after khums like movable booty. The Maliki school holds they become a permanent waqf for all Muslims. The Hanafi school holds that the imam has discretion: he may divide or impose kharaj as circumstances require.
Each madhhab derives its view from different textual evidence and reasoning.
3
Analyze the usul distinction between legislative and administrative actions
The author explains that the distribution of four-fifths of booty is established by the Sunnah, but this Sunnah belongs to 'siyasa shar'iyya' (administrative policy) done by the Prophet as head of state, not as a legislator. Hence it is not an immutable ruling.
Contrast with the Prophet's ruling on grandmother's inheritance (fixed share) which is binding legislation.
4
Apply the principle to modern scenarios
If conditions change (e.g., a salaried army), the imam may adopt a different system for distributing war spoils, such as all spoils going to the state.
This shows the flexibility of Shariah in state policy while maintaining adherence to texts.

Source Text

آخر الناس ما فتحت قرية إلا قسمتها كما قسم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم خيبر
أن تبقى الأراضي والأنهار لأهلها وعمالها، وأن يفرض فقرر عمر
عليها الخراج.
ثم أمر أن يفعل كذلك بأراضي مصر لما وقع فيها نظير هذا الخلاف بين الزبير بن العوام وعمرو بن العاص أمير مصر : ر : كتاب الأموال للإمام أبي عبيد القاسم ابن سلام، الفقرات / ١٤١ - ١٥٣ / ، وكتاب الخراج لأبي يوسف، وفتح الباري، ۱۳٨/٦ ، وأحكام القرآن لأبي بكر الجصاص في سورة الحشر).
ومن الواضح أن هذا الاتجاه الحكيم من عمر في التمييز بين الغنائم الحربية المنقولة وبين الأراضي إنما كان تمسكاً بدلائل النصوص، وجمعاً بينها وإعمالاً لكل منها بتنزيله على منزله الذي يرشد إليه النظر الجامع السديد لا كما يوهمه كلام بعض المتوهمين اليوم من أنَّ خالف عمر في هذه القضية نصوص الشريعة ويعدون هذه المخالفة بزعمهم عبقرية اجتهادية ! فقد رأيت بما نقلناه أن عمر كانت حجته النص القرآني نفسه الذي أفحم به مخالفيه وأثبت رجحان فهمه فيه، وكان على رأيه كبار فقهاء الله الصحابة كعلي ومعاذ رضي ومن هنا اختلفت الاجتهادات في حكم الأراضي التي يفتحها الجيش الإسلامي عنوة على ثلاثة مذاهب :
عنهم .
مذهب يرى أنها تقسم بعد الخمس على الجيش الفاتح كالغنائم المنقولة، وإلى هذا ذهب الاجتهاد الشافعي .
- ومذهب يرى أنها تكون وقفاً حبيساً على جميع المسلمين، فتوضع ثمرتها في بيت المال وتصرف إلى مصالحهم وحاجاتهم العامة. وإلى هذا ذهب الاجتهاد المالكي
;
- ومذهب يرى أنَّ أمرها يعود إلى نظر الإمام وتقديره بحسب ما يرى من الحاجة والمصلحة فإن شاء عَزَل منها الخمس أو أكثر منه البيت المال، وقسم الباقي على الفاتحين، كما فعل النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بأرض خيبر، وإن شاء ترك الأرضين لأهلها وطرح عليها ضريبة الخراج، كما فعل عمر بسواد العراق ومصر. وإلى هذا ذهب الاجتهاد الحنفي (ر: الأموال لأبي عبيد القاسم بن سلام الفقرة / ١٤٩ و ١٥٣، وبداية المجتهد
لابن رشد ٣٢٤١ ورد المحتار ج ۳ باب المغنم وقسمته). ،
تنبيه هام:
هذا، ولا يخفى أن القرآن لم ينص على وجوب توزيع الغنائم الحربية حتى المنقولة منها بين المجاهدين الفاتحين وإنما نصت آية الأنفال على مصارف معينة لخمس الغنائم .
وتوزيع الأخماس الأربعة الباقية على الغانمين إنما جاءت به السنة وهذا النوع من السنة يعد - بحسب قواعد الشريعة - من السياسة الشرعية والتدابير المصلحية التي يفعلها النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بصفة ولايته العامة في الحكم والإدارة لا بصفة النبوة والتشريع، فلا تفيد حقاً تشريعياً ثابتاً لا يتبدل كحكم النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام للجدة بفريضة السدس في الميراث، بل إن لكل من يخلف النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في هذه الولاية العامة أن يلجأ إلى تدبير آخر عند الحاجة كما كان ذلك ممكناً للنبي
نفسه .
فإذا تبدلت الظروف واقتضت الحاجة نظاماً آخر للجندية تقوم فيه حقوق الجيش المجاهد لا على اقتسام الغنيمة بل على أساس إعاشة ووظائف مالية مرتبة للجند، وتكون الغنائم الحربية كلها للدولة ولا حق فيها
Supplement to Chapter (11): Ijtihad of Umar concerning conquered lands. ... Then Umar said: 'I do not see this verse except that it includes all creation until ...' Then he said to them: 'You want that later people come and have nothing? What for those after you? If not for the later people, no village would be conquered except I would divide it as the Messenger of God divided Khaybar.' Then Umar decided that the lands and rivers remain for their people and workers, and that he imposes kharaj (land tax) on them. Then he ordered that the same be done with the lands of Egypt when a similar dispute occurred between al-Zubayr ibn al-Awwam and Amr ibn al-As, the governor of Egypt. (Ref: ...) It is clear that this wise direction of Umar in distinguishing between movable war booty and lands was adherence to textual proofs, combining them and applying each according to its proper place, as guided by comprehensive sound consideration - not as the talk of some deluded people today suggests that Umar contradicted the texts of Shariah and consider this contradiction, as they claim, a genius of ijtihad! Indeed, we have seen from what we have transmitted that Umar's proof was the very Quranic text with which he silenced his opponents and established the preponderance of his understanding. And with his opinion were great jurists among the Companions like Ali and Muadh. Hence, ijtihads differed regarding the ruling of lands conquered by force by the Islamic army into three schools: A school that sees they are distributed after the khums to the conquering army like movable booty - this is the Shafi'i ijtihad. A school that sees they become a permanent endowment for all Muslims, their revenue placed in the public treasury and spent on their public interests and needs - this is the Maliki ijtihad. A school that sees that their matter is referred to the imam's discretion and assessment according to what he sees of necessity and interest: if he wishes, he sets aside the khums or more for the public treasury and divides the rest among the conquerors, as the Prophet did with the land of Khaybar; and if he wishes, he leaves the lands for their owners and imposes upon them the kharaj tax, as Umar did with the Sawad of Iraq and Egypt. This is the Hanafi ijtihad. Important note: It is not hidden that the Quran did not textually stipulate the obligation of distributing war booty, even movable, among the conquering fighters. Rather, the verse of al-Anfal specifies only the expenditures for the khums. The distribution of the remaining four-fifths to the warriors is only established by the Sunnah. This type of Sunnah is considered - according to the rules of Shariah - from the Shariah policy and expedient measures that the Prophet did in his capacity as general ruler in governance and administration, not in his capacity as prophet and legislation. Thus, it does not confer a fixed legislative right that cannot change, like the Prophet's ruling for the grandmother's share of one-sixth in inheritance. Rather, every successor of the Prophet in this general rulership may resort to another measure when needed, as that was possible for the Prophet himself. So if conditions change and necessity requires another system for the military in which the rights of the fighting army are based not on division of spoils but on a basis of maintenance and regular financial salaries for soldiers, and the war booty all goes to the state with no right for them.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a historical and legal controversy: the treatment of conquered lands after military conquest. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by recounting the famous incident where Umar ibn al-Khattab, after the conquest of the Sawad (Iraq), decided not to distribute the land as movable booty but to leave it with its original owners and impose a land tax (kharaj). This decision was opposed by some Companions who wanted division, but Umar justified it by reciting Quranic verses about provision for later generations. The author emphasises that Umar's ijtihad was firmly based on Quranic evidence, contrary to modern claims that he contravened texts. The passage then outlines the three main legal schools' positions on conquered lands: (1) Shafi'i: division after khums (since land is like movable booty); (2) Maliki: permanent endowment (waqf) for all Muslims, with usufruct for public benefit; (3) Hanafi: the matter is left to the imam's discretion, who may either divide (like Khaybar) or leave with kharaj (like Umar). The 'important note' clarifies a key usul al-fiqh principle: the Quran only mandates the distribution of the fifth (khums) of booty; the distribution of the remaining four-fifths is established by prophetic practice (Sunnah). However, this prophetic action is not legislation (tashri') but rather administrative policy (siyasa shar'iyya) done in the Prophet's capacity as head of state. Hence, it is not immutable like a fixed inheritance share. Later rulers may adopt alternative systems if circumstances change, such as a salaried army instead of division of spoils. This principle is critical for understanding the flexibility of Shariah in state policy.

Key Terms
الغنائم الحربية المنقولة (al-ghanā’im al-ḥarbiyya al-manqūla): Movable war booty, such as weapons, goods, and captives, as opposed to immovable property like land.
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyāsa al-sharʿiyya): State policy that is in accordance with Shariah, not directly legislated but permissible within its framework.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the Prophet's ruling on grandmother's inheritance (one-sixth) from his distribution of Khaybar land (four-fifths to fighters)?
A) One is Quranic, the other is not
B) One is binding legislation, the other is administrative policy (siyasa shar'iyya)
C) One applies to Muslims only, the other to non-Muslims
D) Both are the same in legal status

Key Takeaway
Umar's ijtihad on land demonstrates how siyasa shar'iyya allows flexibility in state policy while remaining grounded in Shariah.
