The Dawn of Regional Schools: The Third Formative Period
Historical overview of school formation after the Sahaba dispersal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the dispersal of the Companions after Caliph Umar's death led to the formation of regional fiqh schools and identify the transmission chain that grounds the Hanafi school in the Kufan tradition of Ibn Mas'ud.

Core Idea
The dispersal of the Sahaba created distinct regional schools; the Hanafi school traces its lineage to the companions of Ibn Mas'ud in Kufa.

This passage takes us to the third period of Islamic jurisprudence, a foundational era when the Companions spread from Medina to the far corners of the Islamic world after the death of Caliph Umar. Understanding this historical shift is essential for a mufti: it explains why different schools emerged, each with its own methodology, and how the Hanafi school specifically inherits the jurisprudential legacy of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud in Kufa. This historical awareness helps the mufti appreciate the chain of transmission and the authority of various riwayat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time period and key event
Locate the mid-first century AH, specifically the beginning of Caliph Uthman's reign when Umar's restriction on leaving Medina was lifted. This is the trigger for the dispersal.
Umar's policy kept scholars in Medina for consultation; after his death, they spread.
2
List the major regions and their founding Sahaba
From the passage, note Hijaz, Yemen, Iraq, Egypt, and Syria. Identify which major Companions influenced each region (e.g., Ibn Mas'ud in Iraq, Zayd ibn Thabit in Medina, Ibn Abbas in Mecca).
Ibn al-Qayyim's quote gives the three key centers.
3
Trace the transmission to the Tabi'un
Understand that the Sahaba taught the Tabi'un (Followers), who became the imams of regional schools. For example, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib in Medina studied under Zayd ibn Thabit and others.
This chain is critical for the authenticity (thubut) of rulings.
4
Connect to the Hanafi school
Recognize that the Hanafi school originates from the Kufan tradition. Abu Hanifa studied under Hammad ibn Abi Sulayman, who was a student of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, who was a student of Alqamah and Aswad, who were students of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.
This makes the Kufan school particularly authoritative for Hanafis.
5
Define 'madrasa' in this context
Use the definition from the footnote: a group of scholars sharing a single theory or methodology. For the mufti, this explains why different schools may have different rulings on the same issue.
The cohesion (shart) of a school is coherence in principles.

فقد رأينا أن علماء الصحابة في أوائل خلافة الخليفة الثالث عثمان بن عفان رضي الله عنه تفرَّقوا في الأقطار، واستوطنوا مختلف الأمصار، بعد

لا يمكنهم أن يبرحوا المدينة لكي يستشيرهم في النوازل.

Chapter Twelve: The Third Jurisprudential Period – From the mid-first century to the early second century. 1/12 – This period was the opening of a foundational era for Islamic jurisprudence. We have seen that the scholars among the Companions, at the beginning of the caliphate of the third Caliph Uthman ibn Affan (may Allah be pleased with him), dispersed across the regions and settled in various cities, after Umar had prevented them from leaving Medina so that he could consult them on new incidents. These Companions carried to their new homelands in Hijaz, Yemen, Iraq, Egypt, and Syria the hadith of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and the rulings of sharia, and under their tutelage the generation of the Followers (Tabi'un) graduated in various countries. Ibn al-Qayyim said: 'Fiqh spread among the ummah through the companions of Ibn Mas'ud in Iraq, the companions of Zayd ibn Thabit and Ibn Umar in Medina, and the companions of Ibn Abbas in Mecca' (I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in by Ibn al-Qayyim, 1/23). The major Companions such as Umar, Uthman, Ibn Mas'ud, Ali, and Mu'adh (may Allah be pleased with them) had a directive influence, and accordingly, schools (1) of jurists were formed in various regions, based on their personalities and ijtihad, through their students among the Followers, who became leaders and imams of those schools, such as Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib in Medina. (1) In contemporary terminology, 'school' here means the group of people who follow a single theory or doctrine, scientific or philosophical; it may also refer to the doctrine itself.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the third period of Islamic jurisprudence, which the author identifies as a foundational era. It explains how the dispersal of the Companions from Medina after the death of Caliph Umar and the onset of Caliph Uthman's rule led to the spread of Islamic knowledge and the formation of distinct regional schools of fiqh. The author notes that Umar had previously restricted the Companions from leaving Medina to ensure they were available for consultation. After Umar, they spread to various regions including Hijaz, Yemen, Iraq, Egypt, and Syria, carrying with them the teachings of the Prophet. This led to the emergence of the Tabi'un (Followers) who studied under these Companions. Ibn al-Qayyim is quoted to show that fiqh spread through specific groups: the students of Ibn Mas'ud in Iraq, the students of Zayd ibn Thabit and Ibn Umar in Medina, and the students of Ibn Abbas in Mecca. The passage emphasizes that the major Companions had a formative influence, and their personal methodologies gave rise to different schools of thought. A footnote clarifies the modern usage of 'school' (madrasa) as a group adhering to a single theory or doctrine, or the doctrine itself. This historical context is crucial for understanding the development of the Hanafi school, which traces its roots to the Kufan tradition through Ibn Mas'ud.

Key Terms
الدور الفقهي الثالث (al-dawr al-fiqhi al-thalith): The third jurisprudential period, from mid-first century to early second century AH, characterized by the dispersal of Sahaba and formation of regional schools.
المدرسة (al-madrasa): In this text, 'school' refers to a group of scholars following a single doctrine or theoretical framework in jurisprudence.

Key Takeaway
The third period marks the beginning of regional fiqh schools through the dispersal of the Companions; the Hanafi school's roots lie in the Kufan tradition of Ibn Mas'ud.

History and Development of Fiqh
beginner

Emergence of Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y
Historical description of fiqh's spread and methodological schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two major interpretive tendencies that emerged among early Muslims and explain their foundational differences in approaching legal texts.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school traces its roots to Ahl al-Ra'y, which seeks the purposes (maqasid) behind texts rather than literal adherence.

This lesson traces the historical context of the third juristic period, when fiqh spread from Medina to major centers and two distinct interpretive approaches crystallized. Understanding these tendencies is essential for grasping the methodological foundations of the Hanafi school, which emerged from the Ahl al-Ra'y tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical context
Note the timing: from mid-1st century AH (after Uthman's caliphate) to early 2nd century. Key event: Companions dispersed from Medina to other regions, carrying the Prophet's hadith and rulings.
This dispersal was a turning point because it allowed fiqh to develop regionally.
2
Trace the spread of fiqh to major centers
Identify the main centers mentioned: Medina, Mecca, Kufa, Basra, Syria, and Yemen. Note the leading Tabi'un in each, e.g., Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib in Medina, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i in Kufa.
The Hanafi school would later develop from the Kufan school, especially through Abu Hanifa's teachers.
3
Distinguish the two interpretive tendencies
Define Ahl al-Hadith: literal adherence to the text without seeking underlying causes. Define Ahl al-Ra'y: employing reasoned opinion (ra'y) to discover the 'illa (effective cause) and purpose (maqsad) of texts, applying them in light of Shariah's general objectives.
The passage emphasizes that Ahl al-Ra'y is not arbitrary opinion; it is disciplined, based on ijtihad and the principle that rulings (except pure worship) are motivated by public interests.
4
Connect to the later Hanafi school
Recognize that the Hanafi school is the primary representative of Ahl al-Ra'y. Abu Hanifa studied under Hammad, who inherited the method of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. The Hanafi reliance on qiyas, istihsan, and maqasid stems from this tradition.
This background helps explain why Hanafi usul differs from Shafi'i usul on issues like the hierarchy of sources and the role of istihsan.

Chapter Twelve: The Third Juristic Period, from the mid-first century to the early second century. 1/12 - This period was the beginning of a foundational era for Islamic fiqh. We have seen that the scholars among the Companions, at the beginning of the caliphate of the third Caliph Uthman ibn Affan (may Allah be pleased with him), dispersed across the regions and settled in various provinces, after Umar had prevented them from leaving Medina so that he could consult them on new issues. These Companions carried to their new homes in the Hijaz, Yemen, Iraq, Egypt, and Syria the hadith of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and the rulings of the Shariah, and the generation of the Tabi'un graduated under them in different countries. Ibn al-Qayyim said: Fiqh spread in the Ummah from the companions of Ibn Mas'ud in Iraq, the companions of Zayd ibn Thabit and Ibn Umar in Medina, and the companions of Ibn Abbas in Mecca (I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in by Ibn al-Qayyim, 1/23). The senior Companions such as Umar, Uthman, Ibn Mas'ud, Ali, and Mu'adh (may Allah be pleased with them) had a guiding influence, and accordingly, depending on their personalities and ijtihads, different schools of jurists formed in various lands at the hands of their students from the Tabi'un, who became leaders and imams of those schools, such as Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib in Medina, 'Ata' ibn Abi Rabah in Mecca, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i in Kufa, al-Hasan al-Basri in Basra, Makhul in Syria, and Tawus in Yemen. Through these and their likes, and those who followed them from the following generation, the wheel of fiqh turned its school-based cycle. Each of them was influenced by the fiqh of the Companion he accompanied and his ijtihad methodology. 2/12 - The emergence of the two schools: Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y. The most important of those schools were the two schools of Medina and Kufa, i.e., the Hijaz and Iraq schools. One who follows the fiqh movement since the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs would see that the first seed from which the fiqh schools grew and then the legal schools formed was the emergence of two tendencies among the Companions in understanding legislative texts. These two tendencies are: A) Stopping, in understanding the text, its interpretation, and its application, at its specified boundaries stated therein, without considering its 'illa (effective cause), motives, the circumstances in which it came, and knowing the purpose of the Lawgiver in it, especially the texts of the Prophet's hadith that dealt with specific matters individually and were not limited to generalities, while the texts of the Qur'an are mostly general constitutional principles. B) Or employing ra'y (opinion) and searching for the 'illa of the text, its wisdom, and the purpose of the Lawgiver in it, to apply it in the manner that achieves that purpose, in light of the general objectives of the Shariah, considering that the Shariah came only to establish the interests of human life in the most complete way and to avoid its corruptions, as the Noble Qur'an indicates in His saying: 'And Allah knows the corrupter from the rectifier' [Quran 2:220]. So its rulings – except for pure acts of worship – from commands and prohibitions, even in detailed particulars, are motivated by interests and corruptions. As for purely devotional matters, such as being
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This is a historical passage from the introduction to the third juristic period. It explains how fiqh spread after the Companions dispersed following the caliphate of Uthman. Key centers emerged: Medina, Mecca, Kufa, Basra, Syria, and Yemen. The author quotes Ibn al-Qayyim to show the main transmission chains. Then he describes the two interpretive tendencies that gave rise to the two major schools: Ahl al-Hadith (people of hadith) who stuck to the literal text without seeking underlying causes, and Ahl al-Ra'y (people of opinion) who employed reasoning to find the 'illa of texts and apply them according to maqasid. This dichotomy is foundational for understanding later Hanafi fiqh, as the Hanafi school is rooted in the Ahl al-Ra'y tradition, especially through the Kufan school of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and his student Hammad, who taught Abu Hanifa. The passage emphasizes that the Shariah's rulings (except pure worship) are motivated by interests and harms, which is a key principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
مدرسة (madrasah): A group of scholars who follow a particular theoretical or methodological approach in fiqh, or the approach itself.
أهل الحديث (ahl al-hadith): Those who adhere to the literal text of hadith without considering underlying causes or purposes, focusing on the text's apparent meaning.
أهل الرأي (ahl al-ra'y): Those who employ opinion and reasoning to seek the 'illa and purpose of texts, applying them in light of public interests and maqasid al-shariah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's method of disciplined reasoning (ra'y) rooted in maqasid distinguishes it from literalist approaches and provides a flexible framework for addressing new issues.

The Two Tendencies Among the Companions
Historical description of two exegetical approaches among early Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two methodological tendencies that emerged among the Companions and distinguish their approaches to deriving rulings.

Core Idea
Strict textualism vs. reasoned opinion: the early Companions' approaches shaped later madhhabs.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa traces the first seeds of madhhab formation to two distinct tendencies among the Companions in understanding legislative texts. One group adhered strictly to the apparent meaning without exploring underlying causes, while the other employed reasoned opinion (ra'y) to search for the wisdom and purpose behind rulings. This foundational divergence eventually crystallized into the schools of Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two tendencies
Read the passage carefully and list the two approaches described: one that stops at the text's limits and one that seeks the cause and purpose.
2
Explain the first tendency
Describe the approach of those who limit interpretation to the explicit wording, especially of hadith, without considering underlying causes or circumstances.
This is the seed of Ahl al-Hadith.
3
Explain the second tendency
Describe the approach that uses ra'y to find the illa (cause) and hikmah (wisdom) behind the text, aiming to apply it in line with the Shariah's general objectives.
This is the seed of Ahl al-Ra'y.
4
Note the Quranic support
Identify the Quranic verse cited (2:220) and explain how it supports the second tendency by indicating that Shariah rulings are based on benefit and harm.
The verse: 'And Allah knows the corrupter from the reformer.'
5
Recognize the exception
Understand that the second tendency applies to non-devotional rulings; pure acts of worship (ta'abbudiyat) are not subject to causal investigation.
Example: the number of rak'ahs in prayer.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
وعطاء بن أبي رباح في مكة وإبراهيم النَّخَعي في الكوفة، والحسن البصري في البصرة ومكحول في الشام، وطاووس في اليمن.
فبهؤلاء وأمثالهم ومن تلاهم من تابعي التابعين دارت عجلة الفقه دورتها المذهبية. وكان كل منهم متأثراً بفقه من لازمه من الصحابة وطريقته الاجتهادية.

۲/۱۲ - نشوء مدرستي أهل الحديث وأهل الرأي:
وكان أهم تلك المدارس : مدرستا المدينة والكوفة أي مدرستا الحجاز والعراق.
إنَّ المتتبع للحركة الفقهية منذ عهد الخلفاء الراشدين يبدو له أنَّ البذرة الأولى التي نَبَتَت منها المدارس الفقهية ثم تكونت المذاهب كانت ظهور اتجاهين بين الصحابة في فهم النصوص التشريعية. هذان الاتجاهان هما :
أ - الوقوف في فهم النص وتفسيره وتطبيقه عند حدوده المبينة فيه، دون نظر في علته وبواعثه والظروف التي وَرَدَ فيها ومعرفة غرض الشارع فيها، ولا سيما نصوص الحديث النبوي التي تناولت أموراً معينة بخصوصها ولم تقتصر على العموميات، بينما نصوص القرآن تغلب فيها العموميات الدستورية .
ب - أو إعمال الرأي والبحث عن علة النص وحكمته وغرض الشارع منه لتطبيقه بالصورة التي يتحقق فيها ذلك الغرض، في ظل المقاصد العامة للشريعة باعتبار أن الشريعة إنما جاءت لإقامة مصالح الحياة البشرية على أكمل وجه واجتناب مفاسدها كما نبه إليه القرآن الكريم بقوله :تعالى: وَاللَّهُ يَعْلَمُ الْمُفْسِدَ مِنَ الْمُصْلِحِ ﴾ [البقرة ٢٢٠/٢]. فأحكامها - فيما سوى العبادات المحضة - من أوامر ونواه حتى في الجزئيات التفصيلية، معللة بالمصالح والمفاسد . أما التعبديات المحضة مثل كون فريضة صلاة الصبح ركعتين، وصلاة الظهر أربع ركعات فلا مجال للبحث عن عللها .
فبعض الصحابة ظهر منهم الميل إلى الاتجاه الأول تحرجاً من أن يخطئوا تطبيق أوامر الرسول الله ونواهيه. وبعض آخر - ولا سيما الذين عهد إليهم بحمل المسؤوليات - ظهر منهم الميل إلى الاتجاه الثاني.

۳/۱۲ - قال العلامة الشيخ محمد الخضري بك في كتابه القيم تاريخ التشريع الإسلامي:
إن كبار الصحابة كانوا في العصر الأول يستندون في فتاواهم إلى الكتاب ثم إلى السنة فإن أعجزهم ذلك أفتوا بالرأي وهو القياس بأوسع معانيه، ولم يكونوا يميلون إلى التوسع في الأخذ بالرأي. لذلك أُثِر عنهم ذم الرأي. وقد بينا فيما مضى ما الرأي المعمول به، وما الرأي المذموم؟ ولما جاء هذا الخلف من بعدهم وُجِدَ منهم من يقف في الفتوى على الحديث ولا يتعداه فيفتي في كل مسألة بما يجده من ذلك (في الحديث النبوي)، وليست هناك روابط تربط المسائل بعضها ببعض.
وَوُجِد فريق آخر يرى أن الشريعة معقولة المعاني ولها أصول يرجع إليها، فكانوا لا يخالفون الأولين في العمل بالكتاب والسنة ما وجدوا إلى ذلك سبيلاً ولكنهم لاقتناعهم بمعقولية الشريعة وابتنائها على أصول محكمة مفهومة من الكتاب والسنة كانوا لا يحجمون عن الفتوى بما يرونه أقرب إلى مقاصد الشريعة فيما لم يجدوا فيه نصاً، وفوق ذلك كانوا يحبون معرفة العلل والغايات التي من أجلها شرعت الأحكام. وربما ردوا بعض أحاديث لمخالفتها أصول الشريعة، ولا سيما إذا عارضتها أحاديث نبوية أخرى.
وقد كان أكثر ظهور هذا المبدأ في أهل العراق، فكان أكثر فقهاء العراق أهل رأي وكان أكثر أهل الحجاز أهل حديث (تاريخ التشريع الإسلامي، ص ۱۰۷ - ۱۰۸ ، ببعض اختصار).
Chapter Three: Stages of the Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. And Ata' ibn Abi Rabah in Mecca, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i in Kufa, al-Hasan al-Basri in Basra, Makhul in Sham, and Tawus in Yemen. Through these and their like, and those who followed them from the successors of the successors, the wheel of jurisprudence turned in its school-based cycle. Each was influenced by the fiqh of the Companion he accompanied and his ijtihadi method. 2/12 - Emergence of the Two Schools: Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y. The most important of those schools were the schools of Medina and Kufa, i.e., the schools of Hijaz and Iraq. The one who follows the jurisprudential movement since the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs sees that the first seed from which the jurisprudential schools grew and then the madhhabs were formed was the appearance of two tendencies among the Companions in understanding legislative texts. These two tendencies are: A) Stopping in understanding the text, its interpretation, and its application at the limits stated therein, without consideration of its cause, motives, circumstances in which it was revealed, and knowledge of the Lawgiver's purpose therein, especially the texts of prophetic hadith that dealt with specific matters in particular and were not limited to generalities, while the texts of the Qur'an are mostly constitutional generalities. B) Or exercising opinion and searching for the cause of the text, its wisdom, and the Lawgiver's purpose to apply it in the manner that achieves that purpose, in light of the general objectives of the Shariah, considering that the Shariah came only to establish the interests of human life in the best way and to avoid its corruptions, as the Noble Qur'an alerted by His saying: 'And Allah knows the corrupter from the reformer' [Al-Baqarah 2:220]. So its rulings - apart from pure acts of worship - of commands and prohibitions, even in detailed particulars, are reasoned by interests and corruptions. As for pure devotional acts, such as the obligatory prayer of Fajr being two rak'ahs and Zuhr being four rak'ahs, there is no room for research into their causes. Some Companions showed inclination towards the first tendency out of caution lest they err in applying the commands and prohibitions of the Messenger of Allah. Others - especially those entrusted with responsibilities - showed inclination towards the second tendency. 3/12 - The scholar Sheikh Muhammad al-Khudari Bek said in his valuable book 'History of Islamic Legislation': The senior Companions in the first era relied in their fatwas on the Book, then the Sunnah, and if that failed them, they gave fatwa by ra'y, which is qiyas in its broadest sense, and they were not inclined to expand in adopting ra'y. Therefore, blame of ra'y is transmitted from them. We have already explained what acceptable ra'y is and what blameworthy ra'y is. When this later generation came after them, there were found among them those who stopped in fatwa at the hadith and did not go beyond it, giving fatwa on every issue according to what they found of that (in the prophetic hadith), and there were no links connecting issues to one another. And another group was found who saw that the Shariah has understandable meanings and has principles to refer to, so they did not oppose the former in acting upon the Book and Sunnah as long as they found a way to do so, but due to their conviction of the rationality of the Shariah and its foundation on solid principles understood from the Book and Sunnah, they did not refrain from giving fatwa according to what they saw as closer to the objectives of the Shariah in matters where they found no text, and above that, they loved to know the causes and ends for which rulings were legislated. And perhaps they rejected some hadiths because they contradicted the principles of the Shariah, especially if other prophetic hadiths opposed them. This principle was most prevalent among the people of Iraq, so most of the jurists of Iraq were people of opinion, and most of the people of Hijaz were people of hadith. (History of Islamic Legislation, pp. 107-108, with some abbreviation.)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the development of Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on the emergence of two distinct methodological tendencies that eventually crystallized into the schools of Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by naming prominent early jurists from different regions, showing the geographical spread of fiqh learning. He then states that the first seed of madhhab formation was the appearance of two tendencies among the Companions in understanding legislative texts. The first tendency is strictly textualist: it limits interpretation and application to the explicit wording of the text, without searching for causes, circumstances, or the Lawgiver's purpose. This was particularly applied to hadiths that dealt with specific matters. The second tendency employs reasoned opinion (ra'y) to discover the underlying cause (illa) and wisdom (hikma) of the text, to apply it in a way that achieves the Shariah's general objectives, which are to secure human interests and prevent harm. The author cites Quran 2:220 to support that Shariah rulings are based on considerations of benefit and harm, except for purely devotional acts (ta'abbudiyat) whose causes are not investigated. He notes that some Companions inclined to the first approach due to caution, while others, especially those in leadership roles, adopted the second. Then he quotes Muhammad al-Khudari, who confirms that the senior Companions used both text and reasoning but were cautious with ra'y. Later generations split: one group stuck only to hadith, giving isolated rulings without linking issues; another group saw the Shariah as rational and based on well-grounded principles, so they felt free to issue fatwas based on objectives when no text was found, and they even rejected hadiths that contradicted those principles, especially when other hadiths conflicted. This latter approach was characteristic of the Iraqi school (Ahl al-Ra'y), while the Hijazi school (Ahl al-Hadith) was more textualist. This distinction is foundational for understanding why Hanafi fiqh, which originated in Kufa, relies heavily on ra'y and istihsan, while other madhhabs like the Hanbali are more textualist.

Key Terms
أهل الحديث (Ahl al-Hadith): Those who limit fatwa to what is found in prophetic hadith and do not go beyond it, without linking rulings through underlying principles.
أهل الرأي (Ahl al-Ra'y): Those who use reasoned opinion and analogy (qiyas) to derive rulings, seeking the causes and objectives behind texts.
الرأي المذموم (al-Ra'y al-Madhmum): Blameworthy opinion that is based on mere personal whim or speculation without textual foundation or valid reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for rulings in non-devotional matters?
A) They are purely based on the ruler's discretion.
B) They are reasoned by interests (masalih) and corruptions (mafasid).
C) They are solely derived from prophetic hadith.
D) They cannot be changed once established.

Key Takeaway
The early divergence between strict textualism and reasoned opinion among the Companions laid the groundwork for the eventual formation of the Hadith and Ra'y schools.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
7 min
Development into Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y
Historical analysis of the split into textualists and rationalists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the two tendencies developed into distinct schools and evaluate the role of ra'y in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Hadith relied solely on texts; Ahl al-Ra'y derived principles and objectives to expand fiqh.

This passage from Muhammad al-Khudari's 'History of Islamic Legislation' describes how the senior Companions used both text and ra'y cautiously, but later generations split into two distinct groups: one that stuck only to hadith (Ahl al-Hadith) and another that saw the Shariah as rational with underlying principles (Ahl al-Ra'y). This latter approach became characteristic of Iraqi jurists, including the founders of the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Summarize the senior Companions' methodology
According to al-Khudari, how did the senior Companions proceed in fatwa? They relied on Quran, then Sunnah, then ra'y (qiyas) cautiously.
2
Identify the later generation's split
When the later generation came, two groups emerged: one that stopped at hadith alone, and another that recognized the Shariah's rationality and principles.
3
Describe the Ahl al-Hadith approach
This group gave fatwa only from prophetic hadith, treating each issue in isolation without connecting principles.
They did not look for underlying causes or objectives.
4
Describe the Ahl al-Ra'y approach
This group believed the Shariah has understandable meanings and firm principles; they did not refrain from fatwa based on what they saw as closer to Shariah objectives when no text was found, and they loved to know causes and ends.
They might reject a hadith if it contradicted those principles, especially if other hadiths opposed it.
5
Identify the regional association
Al-Khudari concludes that most Iraqi jurists were people of opinion (Ahl al-Ra'y), while most Hijazi jurists were people of hadith (Ahl al-Hadith).
This geographical split influenced the formation of madhhabs.

Source Text

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
وعطاء بن أبي رباح في مكة وإبراهيم النَّخَعي في الكوفة، والحسن البصري في البصرة ومكحول في الشام، وطاووس في اليمن.
فبهؤلاء وأمثالهم ومن تلاهم من تابعي التابعين دارت عجلة الفقه دورتها المذهبية. وكان كل منهم متأثراً بفقه من لازمه من الصحابة وطريقته الاجتهادية.

۲/۱۲ - نشوء مدرستي أهل الحديث وأهل الرأي:
وكان أهم تلك المدارس : مدرستا المدينة والكوفة أي مدرستا الحجاز والعراق.
إنَّ المتتبع للحركة الفقهية منذ عهد الخلفاء الراشدين يبدو له أنَّ البذرة الأولى التي نَبَتَت منها المدارس الفقهية ثم تكونت المذاهب كانت ظهور اتجاهين بين الصحابة في فهم النصوص التشريعية. هذان الاتجاهان هما :

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the development of Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on the emergence of two distinct methodological tendencies that eventually crystallized into the schools of Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by naming prominent early jurists from different regions, showing the geographical spread of fiqh learning. Key Terms
أهل الحديث (Ahl al-Hadith): Those who limit fatwa to what is found in prophetic hadith and do not go beyond it, without linking rulings through underlying principles.
أهل الرأي (Ahl al-Ra'y): Those who use reasoned opinion and analogy (qiyas) to derive rulings, seeking the causes and objectives behind texts.
المقاصد العامة للشريعة (al-Maqasid al-'Ammah li al-Shari'ah): The general objectives of the Lawgiver, such as establishing benefits and preventing harms, which guide ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Khudari, what was the attitude of the senior Companions towards using ra'y?
A) They completely avoided it.
B) They used it frequently without hesitation.
C) They used it only when necessary and were cautious about expanding it.
D) They preferred ra'y over textual evidence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exemplifies Ahl al-Ra'y, using reasoned opinion and maqasid to address new issues while maintaining textual foundations.

History and Development of Fiqh
beginner
6 min
The Birth of Fiqh as a Specialized Discipline
Historical narrative of scholarly withdrawal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical factors that led to fiqh becoming an independent specialization and identify the role of early Hanafi scholars in this process.

Core Idea
Political turmoil under the Umayyads drove pious scholars to withdraw and specialize in fiqh.

This lesson traces the pivotal shift in early Islamic history when fiqh separated from general hadith transmission to become a distinct, systematic discipline. You will learn how the political and social crises of the late first century AH motivated sincere scholars to devote themselves exclusively to fiqh, laying the foundation for the great madhhabs, including our Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Pre-Separation State
Understand that before the second half of the first century AH, fiqh and hadith were intertwined. A faqih was also a muhaddith, and vice versa. Recognize this fusion as the starting point.
2
Analyze the Catalytic Events
Identify the deviation of Umayyad caliphs from the practices of the rightly-guided caliphs and the resulting internal strife (fitan). These events drove pious scholars to withdraw from public life.
This withdrawal was a conscious choice for religious integrity, not an official decree.
3
Trace the Withdrawal and Focus
See how these scholars turned exclusively to fiqh—studying, teaching, and occasionally criticizing rulers. They formed circles of dedicated students, marking the beginning of fiqh as an independent specialization.
Key cities included Kufa, Basra, Medina, and Mecca.
4
Connect to the Hanafi School
Reflect that Imam Abu Hanifa (d. 150 AH) was part of this movement. His systematic approach to fiqh and his students (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) exemplified this new specialization.
The Hanafi school is a direct product of this historical shift.

بدء استقلال علم الفقه وصيرورته اختصاصاً : ٥/١٢ - في النصف الثاني من القرن الأول الهجري ظهرت بادرة جديدة هي اعتزال بعض الأتقياء من أهل العلم والفتوى الحياة العامة، وانصرافهم إلى الفقه تعلماً وتعليماً في بعض الأمصار. قبل ذلك كان الفقه ورواية الحديث النبوي متداخلين متلازمين، فالفقيه حامل حديث، وكثرة علمه وفقهه تأتي من كثرة ما يرويه من أحاديث الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وحامل الحديث النبوي متفقه يفتي الناس في مسائلهم بما عنده من الحديث الذي يرويه. لكن انحراف الخلفاء الأمويين عن سيرة الخلفاء الراشدين الأربعة الأولين في التزام المثل العليا الدينية والعملية في النصف الثاني من القرن الأول، وما ظهر فيه من فتن وثورات داخلية دفع فريقاً من أهل العلم والفتوى ذوي الإخلاص والتقوى إلى اعتزال الحياة العامة وصخبها السياسي والاجتماعي، فانصرفوا في عدد من المدن والأمصار إلى الفقه دراسة وتعليماً ينقدون الحكام أحياناً، وينشرون العلم بين تلاميذهم الذين لازموهم. وكان هذا بداية استقلال علم الفقه ونعني باستقلاله أنه صار اختصاصاً ينصرف إليه راغبوه العاكفون عليه تعلماً وتعليماً. وينضم إلى كل واحد من أقطاب الفقه جماعة يتلقون عنه علمه ومنهجه، وينشرونه بين الناس . وفي النصف الثاني من القرن الأول كان التابعون الذين تلقوا عن الصحابة حين انتشروا في الأمصار كثيرين. وفي أوائل القرن الثاني كان تابعو التابعين كثيرين أيضاً. وكثير من هؤلاء وأولئك كان لكل منهم فتاواه وآراؤه الاجتهادية في المسائل والقضايا، نقلت عنهم وسُجِّلت بعدهم فيما نقله وسجله المؤلفون في اختلاف الفقهاء. ولكن الذين دُوِّنت لهم مذاهب كاملة أي آراء وفتاوى في جميع أبواب الفقه وأقسامه، هم قلة، وهم الذين رزقوا تلاميذ نجباء فهماء نشيطين نقلوا عنهم آراءهم وسجلوها في مختلف القضايا والمسائل، فتكون لكل منهم.
Beginning of the independence of fiqh and its becoming a specialization: 5/12 - In the second half of the first century AH, a new phenomenon appeared: the withdrawal of some pious scholars of knowledge and fatwa from public life, and their turning to fiqh, learning and teaching, in some cities. Before that, fiqh and the transmission of prophetic hadith were intertwined and inseparable. The faqih was a carrier of hadith, and his abundance of knowledge and fiqh came from the abundance of what he narrated from the hadiths of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him); and the carrier of prophetic hadith was a faqih who gave fatwas to people in their issues based on the hadith he narrated. But the deviation of the Umayyad caliphs from the way of the first four rightly-guided caliphs in adhering to religious and practical ideals in the second half of the first century, and the internal strife and revolutions that appeared, drove a group of scholars of knowledge and fatwa who had sincerity and piety to withdraw from public life and its political and social turmoil. So they turned in a number of cities and towns to fiqh, studying and teaching, sometimes criticizing the rulers, and spreading knowledge among their students who accompanied them. This was the beginning of the independence of the science of fiqh. By its independence we mean that it became a specialization to which its desirous ones, who devoted themselves to learning and teaching, turned. And to each one of the leading figures of fiqh, a group would join, receiving from him his knowledge and methodology, and spreading it among the people. In the second half of the first century, the Tabi'un who had received from the Companions when they spread out in the cities were many. And in the early second century, the Tabi' al-Tabi'in were also many. Many of these and those had their own fatwas and ijtihad opinions on issues and cases, transmitted from them and recorded after them in what authors transmitted and recorded in Ikhtilaf al-Fuqaha'. But those for whom complete madhhabs were recorded—that is, opinions and fatwas in all chapters and sections of fiqh—were few, and they were those who were blessed with brilliant, learned, and active students who transmitted their opinions and recorded them in various issues and cases, so that each of them had (a madhhab).
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage describes the historical process by which the study of fiqh became distinct from hadith transmission. Initially, fiqh and hadith were integrated; a scholar was both a muhaddith and a faqih. However, political turmoil under the Umayyads caused a group of pious scholars to withdraw from public life and dedicate themselves exclusively to fiqh. This shift led to the emergence of fiqh as an independent specialization, where scholars developed systematic methodologies and attracted dedicated students. The passage notes that while many early scholars left fatwas, only a few had their rulings compiled as complete madhhabs covering all areas of fiqh.

Key Terms
استقلال علم الفقه (istiqlal 'ilm al-fiqh): The process by which fiqh became an independent discipline separated from general hadith transmission, with dedicated specialists.
التابعون (al-Tabi'un): The generation after the Companions who learned from them and transmitted Islamic knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the primary factor that led scholars to withdraw from public life and specialize in fiqh?
A) The death of the Prophet (peace be upon him).
B) The deviation of the Umayyad caliphs and internal strife.
C) A command from the caliphs to isolate themselves.
D) The compilation of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh became an independent discipline when pious scholars withdrew from political turmoil and devoted themselves to systematic study, leading to the emergence of complete madhhabs like the Hanafi school.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
The Historical Emergence of Fiqh as an Independent Discipline
Historical account of fiqh's independence and madhhab formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the social and political factors that led to the specialization of fiqh as an independent science and the role of students in preserving madhhabs.

Core Idea
Fiqh became independent when pious scholars withdrew from corrupt politics and dedicated themselves to teaching.

In the early Islamic period, fiqh and hadith were inseparable; a faqih was a muhaddith. However, the political deviations and internal strife under the Umayyads drove many pious scholars to withdraw from public life. They turned exclusively to teaching and studying fiqh, laying the foundation for fiqh as a distinct discipline. Their students recorded their opinions and methodologies, giving birth to complete legal schools (madhhabs). This lesson explores that transformation and its significance for usul al-iftaa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial integration of fiqh and hadith
Recognize that in the early period, fuqaha were also carriers of hadith; their knowledge was based on narrations. There was no specialization.
This explains why early works combined fiqh and hadith.
2
Analyze the political factor: Umayyad deviation
The text states that the Umayyad caliphs deviated from the ways of the Rashidun, leading to internal trials. This caused pious scholars to eschew public life.
This was a key catalyst for the independence of fiqh.
3
Understand the withdrawal and specialization
Scholars turned to fiqh study and teaching, often criticizing rulers. They gathered a circle of students, and fiqh became a distinct specialization.
This marked the beginning of fiqh as an independent science.
4
Recognize the role of students in recording madhhabs
A few imams had exceptional students who recorded their opinions on all chapters of fiqh, creating comprehensive madhhabs. These records became the references for later generations.
Without these students, the madhhabs would not have survived.

الفصل (۱۲) الدور الثالث : جديدة هي اعتزال بعض الأتقياء من أهل العلم والفتوى الحياة العامة، وانصرافهم إلى الفقه تعلماً وتعليماً في بعض الأمصار. قبل ذلك كان الفقه ورواية الحديث النبوي متداخلين متلازمين، فالفقيه حامل حديث، وكثرة علمه وفقهه تأتي من كثرة ما يرويه من أحاديث الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وحامل الحديث النبوي متفقه يفتي الناس في مسائلهم بما عنده من الحديث الذي يرويه. لكن انحراف الخلفاء الأمويين عن سيرة الخلفاء الراشدين الأربعة الأولين في التزام المثل العليا الدينية والعملية في النصف الثاني من القرن الأول، وما ظهر فيه من فتن وثورات داخلية دفع فريقاً من أهل العلم والفتوى ذوي الإخلاص والتقوى إلى اعتزال الحياة العامة وصخبها السياسي والاجتماعي، فانصرفوا في عدد من المدن والأمصار إلى الفقه دراسة وتعليماً ينقدون الحكام أحياناً، وينشرون العلم، بين تلاميذهم الذين لازموهم. وكان هذا بداية استقلال علم الفقه ونعني باستقلاله أنه صار اختصاصاً ينصرف إليه راغبوه العاكفون عليه تعلماً وتعليماً. وينضم إلى كل واحد من أقطاب الفقه جماعة يتلقون عنه علمه ومنهجه، وينشرونه بين الناس. وفي النصف الثاني من القرن الأول كان التابعون الذين تلقوا عن الصحابة حين انتشروا في الأمصار كثيرين. وفي أوائل القرن الثاني كان تابعو التابعين كثيرين أيضاً. وكثير من هؤلاء وأولئك كان لكل منهم فتاواه وآراؤه الاجتهادية في المسائل والقضايا، نقلت عنهم وسُجِّلت بعدهم فيما نقله وسجله المؤلفون في اختلاف الفقهاء. ولكن الذين دُوِّنت لهم مذاهب كاملة أي آراء وفتاوى في جميع أبواب الفقه وأقسامه، هم قلة، وهم الذين رزقوا تلاميذ نجباء فهماء نشيطين نقلوا عنهم آراءهم وسجلوها في مختلف القضايا والمسائل، فتكون لكل منهم مذهب فقهي كامل شامل لمختلف فصول الأحكام من العبادات إلى المعاملات، فالجنايات فشؤون الأسرة الأحوال الشخصية). فكان ما دونه أولئك التلاميذ الأوائل عن كل من أولئك الأئمة هو المرجع في معرفة مذهبه الذي نُسِبَ إليه، ومنهجه الفقهي وقواعده الأصولية التي اعتمدها ثم جاء تلاميذ طبقات لاحقة من فقهاء كل مذهب فنقحوا وتوسعوا في التفريع والتخريج والتأليف في مذاهب أئمتهم حتى بلغت المؤلفات الفقهية في كل مذهب ما بين شروح وفتاوى العشرات أو المئات ما بين مختصرات ومطولات قد يبلغ حجم أحدها ثلاثين مجلداً أو أكثر. المذاهب : ٦/١٢ - فالمذاهب التي خالف أئمتها الحظ السعيد، فكان لهم تلاميذ نجباء نشيطون سجلوا آراءهم فخلدت مذاهبهم و خدمت بالشرح والتخريج والتفريع على أصول كل مذهب منها خلال العصور، هي ثلاث فئات: الفئة الأولى : هي المذاهب الأربعة التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة، منها ما يغلب على فقهه طابع أهل الرأي، ومنها ما يغلب عليه طابع أهل الحديث. ومذاهبهم يتبعها إلى اليوم معظم المسلمين في العالم الإسلامي. وكل قطر أو بضعة أقطار من العالم الإسلامي يسود فيه أحد هذا المذاهب الأربعة. التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة وهي بحسب الترتيب التاريخي لأئمتها المذهب الحنفي، ثم المذهب المالكي، ثم المذهب الشافعي، ثم المذهب الحنبلي. الفئة الثانية: هي المذهب الظاهري وهذا يمكن تصنيفه مع مذاهب أهل السنة الأربعة لأنه يعتمد مصادرهم نفسها ولكنه يختلف في شدة تمسك إمامه وأصحابه بحرفية النص إلى درجة تخرج عن المعقول في بعض الأمور، وينبو عنها المنطق التشريعي ولا يقبلها أهل الحديث أنفسهم فضلاً عن أهل الرأي ولذلك مات مذهبهم فلم تكن له سيادة في قطر ما، وإنما تُعرض بعض آرائهم في دراسة الفقه المقارن اليوم.
Chapter (12) The Third Stage: A new development was the withdrawal of some pious scholars of knowledge and fatwa from public life, and their turning to fiqh, learning and teaching, in some cities. Before that, fiqh and the narration of prophetic hadith were intertwined and inseparable; the faqih was a carrier of hadith, and his abundance of knowledge and fiqh came from the abundance of hadith he narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and the carrier of prophetic hadith was a faqih who gave fatwa to people in their issues based on the hadith he narrated. But the deviation of the Umayyad caliphs from the way of the first four Rightly Guided Caliphs in adhering to religious and practical ideals in the second half of the first century, and the internal trials and revolutions that appeared therein, drove a group of sincere and pious scholars of knowledge and fatwa to withdraw from public life and its political and social clamor. So they turned in a number of cities and regions to fiqh, studying and teaching, sometimes criticizing the rulers, and spreading knowledge among their students who accompanied them. This was the beginning of the independence of the science of fiqh. By its independence we mean that it became a specialization to which its aspirants devoted themselves, learning and teaching. And attached to each of the major scholars of fiqh was a group that received his knowledge and methodology from him and spread it among the people. In the second half of the first century, the Followers (Tabi'un) who had received from the Companions when they dispersed in the regions were many. And at the beginning of the second century, the Followers of the Followers (Tabi' al-Tabi'in) were also many. Many of these and those had their own fatwas and ijtihadi opinions on issues and cases, which were transmitted from them and recorded after them in what the authors recorded in works on the differences of jurists. But those whose complete madhhabs were recorded—i.e., opinions and fatwas in all chapters and sections of fiqh—were few, and they were those who were blessed with intelligent, knowledgeable, and active students who transmitted their opinions from them and recorded them in various cases and issues. Thus there came into being for each of them a complete, comprehensive fiqh madhhab covering the various chapters of rulings, from acts of worship to transactions, crimes, family matters (personal status). So what those early students recorded about each of those imams became the reference for knowing his madhhab—attributed to him—his fiqh methodology, and the usul principles he relied upon. Then came later layers of students from each madhhab who refined, expanded derivation (takhrij), and wrote extensively in the madhhabs of their imams, until the fiqh works in each madhhab, whether commentaries or fatwa collections, reached dozens or hundreds, ranging from abridged to voluminous works, some of which might reach thirty volumes or more. The schools: 6/12 - The madhhabs whose imams were fortunate, having intelligent, active students who recorded their opinions and thus immortalized their madhhabs, which were served with commentary, derivation, and branch rulings upon the principles of each madhhab throughout the ages, are three categories: The first category: the four schools that represent Sunni fiqh. Some have a character predominantly of the People of Opinion (Ahl al-Ra'y), and some have a character predominantly of the People of Hadith (Ahl al-Hadith). Their schools are followed today by most Muslims in the Islamic world. In every region or several regions of the Islamic world, one of these four schools dominates. They represent Sunni fiqh and are, in historical order of their imams: the Hanafi school, then the Maliki school, then the Shafi'i school, then the Hanbali school. The second category: the Zahiri school. This can be classified with the four Sunni schools because it relies on the same sources, but it differs in the extreme adherence of its imam and followers to the literal text to a degree that goes beyond reason in some matters, and from which legislative logic recoils, and which even the People of Hadith themselves do not accept, let alone the People of Opinion. Therefore their school died out and never had dominance in any region. Rather, some of their opinions are discussed today in the study of comparative fiqh.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a historical account of the development of Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh) as an independent discipline and the formation of the major legal schools (madhhabs). The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that in the early period, fiqh and hadith narration were inseparable; a faqih was also a muhaddith. However, due to political deviations and internal strife under the Umayyads, many pious scholars withdrew from public life and dedicated themselves exclusively to teaching and studying fiqh. This specialization marked the beginning of fiqh as a distinct science. These scholars attracted students who recorded their opinions and methodologies, thus forming complete madhhabs. The passage then categorizes the surviving schools into two categories: (1) the four Sunni schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) and (2) the Zahiri school, which, though based on the same sources, was extremly literalistic and eventually died out. The author notes that the Zahiri school is only studied today in comparative fiqh. This is not a ruling or a maxim but a historical and philosophical overview. Students should understand the social and political factors that led to the crystallization of madhhabs and the role of dedicated students in preserving them.

Key Terms
المذهب الفقهي (al-madhhab al-fiqhi): A complete set of legal opinions (fatwas) and ijtihad of an imam, recorded by his students, covering all chapters of fiqh.
أهل الرأي (ahl al-ra'y): Scholars who prioritized qiyas and istihsan over literal hadith reading.
أهل الحديث (ahl al-hadith): Scholars who gave primacy to hadith literalism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, what was the primary catalyst for the independence of fiqh as a specialized discipline?
A) The death of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and the need to preserve his teachings.
B) The deviation of the Umayyad caliphs and internal strife, which caused pious scholars to withdraw from public life.
C) The compilation of the Quran into a single volume.
D) The rise of hadith forgery.

Key Takeaway
The independence of fiqh as a discipline and the formation of lasting madhhabs were triggered by political and social turmoil that pushed pious scholars to withdraw from public life and dedicate themselves to teaching, with their students recording their complete legal opinions.

History and Development of Fiqh

Categorization of Surviving Madhhabs: The Four Sunni Schools and the Zahiri School
Categorization of surviving and extinct madhhabs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the major madhhabs into those that survived and explain the reasons for the survival of the four Sunni schools and the decline of the Zahiri school.

Core Idea
Only four Sunni madhhabs survived because they had active students who recorded and propagated their imams' teachings.

After explaining how madhhabs formed, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa categorizes the surviving schools. He identifies two categories: the four Sunni schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) which continue to be followed by most Muslims, and the Zahiri school, which, despite relying on the same sources, was extremely literal and eventually died out. This lesson examines the classification and the reasons for survival, emphasizing the role of students and the methodological balance between ra'y and hadith.

Guided Path
1
List the three categories of madhhabs mentioned in the passage
The passage categorizes madhhabs into three: (1) the four Sunni schools that survived, (2) the Zahiri school that died out, and (3) presumably other schools that also died out but are not named.
Only the first category dominates today.
2
Analyze the characteristics of the four Sunni schools
These schools represent Sunni fiqh. Some are predominantly ahl al-ra'y (Hanafi) and others ahl al-hadith (Hanbali). They are followed by most Muslims and dominate specific regions.
The historical order: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali.
3
Examine the Zahiri school and its reasons for decline
The Zahiri school adhered literally to the text to an extreme degree, which the author calls 'beyond reason' and rejected even by ahl al-hadith. Therefore it never gained dominance and only survives in comparative fiqh studies.
This shows that an overly literal approach can be untenable.
4
Understand why the four schools survived
They survived because their imams had active, knowledgeable students who recorded their opinions on all fiqh chapters, and later generations continued to develop and comment.
This reinforces the theme from the first lesson.

الفصل (۱۲) الدور الثالث : جديدة هي اعتزال بعض الأتقياء من أهل العلم والفتوى الحياة العامة، وانصرافهم إلى الفقه تعلماً وتعليماً في بعض الأمصار. قبل ذلك كان الفقه ورواية الحديث النبوي متداخلين متلازمين، فالفقيه حامل حديث، وكثرة علمه وفقهه تأتي من كثرة ما يرويه من أحاديث الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وحامل الحديث النبوي متفقه يفتي الناس في مسائلهم بما عنده من الحديث الذي يرويه. لكن انحراف الخلفاء الأمويين عن سيرة الخلفاء الراشدين الأربعة الأولين في التزام المثل العليا الدينية والعملية في النصف الثاني من القرن الأول، وما ظهر فيه من فتن وثورات داخلية دفع فريقاً من أهل العلم والفتوى ذوي الإخلاص والتقوى إلى اعتزال الحياة العامة وصخبها السياسي والاجتماعي، فانصرفوا في عدد من المدن والأمصار إلى الفقه دراسة وتعليماً ينقدون الحكام أحياناً، وينشرون العلم، بين تلاميذهم الذين لازموهم. وكان هذا بداية استقلال علم الفقه ونعني باستقلاله أنه صار اختصاصاً ينصرف إليه راغبوه العاكفون عليه تعلماً وتعليماً. وينضم إلى كل واحد من أقطاب الفقه جماعة يتلقون عنه علمه ومنهجه، وينشرونه بين الناس. وفي النصف الثاني من القرن الأول كان التابعون الذين تلقوا عن الصحابة حين انتشروا في الأمصار كثيرين. وفي أوائل القرن الثاني كان تابعو التابعين كثيرين أيضاً. وكثير من هؤلاء وأولئك كان لكل منهم فتاواه وآراؤه الاجتهادية في المسائل والقضايا، نقلت عنهم وسُجِّلت بعدهم فيما نقله وسجله المؤلفون في اختلاف الفقهاء. ولكن الذين دُوِّنت لهم مذاهب كاملة أي آراء وفتاوى في جميع أبواب الفقه وأقسامه، هم قلة، وهم الذين رزقوا تلاميذ نجباء فهماء نشيطين نقلوا عنهم آراءهم وسجلوها في مختلف القضايا والمسائل، فتكون لكل منهم مذهب فقهي كامل شامل لمختلف فصول الأحكام من العبادات إلى المعاملات، فالجنايات فشؤون الأسرة الأحوال الشخصية). فكان ما دونه أولئك التلاميذ الأوائل عن كل من أولئك الأئمة هو المرجع في معرفة مذهبه الذي نُسِبَ إليه، ومنهجه الفقهي وقواعده الأصولية التي اعتمدها ثم جاء تلاميذ طبقات لاحقة من فقهاء كل مذهب فنقحوا وتوسعوا في التفريع والتخريج والتأليف في مذاهب أئمتهم حتى بلغت المؤلفات الفقهية في كل مذهب ما بين شروح وفتاوى العشرات أو المئات ما بين مختصرات ومطولات قد يبلغ حجم أحدها ثلاثين مجلداً أو أكثر. المذاهب : ٦/١٢ - فالمذاهب التي خالف أئمتها الحظ السعيد، فكان لهم تلاميذ نجباء نشيطون سجلوا آراءهم فخلدت مذاهبهم و خدمت بالشرح والتخريج والتفريع على أصول كل مذهب منها خلال العصور، هي ثلاث فئات: الفئة الأولى : هي المذاهب الأربعة التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة، منها ما يغلب على فقهه طابع أهل الرأي، ومنها ما يغلب عليه طابع أهل الحديث. ومذاهبهم يتبعها إلى اليوم معظم المسلمين في العالم الإسلامي. وكل قطر أو بضعة أقطار من العالم الإسلامي يسود فيه أحد هذا المذاهب الأربعة. التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة وهي بحسب الترتيب التاريخي لأئمتها المذهب الحنفي، ثم المذهب المالكي، ثم المذهب الشافعي، ثم المذهب الحنبلي. الفئة الثانية: هي المذهب الظاهري وهذا يمكن تصنيفه مع مذاهب أهل السنة الأربعة لأنه يعتمد مصادرهم نفسها ولكنه يختلف في شدة تمسك إمامه وأصحابه بحرفية النص إلى درجة تخرج عن المعقول في بعض الأمور، وينبو عنها المنطق التشريعي ولا يقبلها أهل الحديث أنفسهم فضلاً عن أهل الرأي ولذلك مات مذهبهم فلم تكن له سيادة في قطر ما، وإنما تُعرض بعض آرائهم في دراسة الفقه المقارن اليوم.
Chapter (12) The Third Stage: A new development was the withdrawal of some pious scholars of knowledge and fatwa from public life, and their turning to fiqh, learning and teaching, in some cities. Before that, fiqh and the narration of prophetic hadith were intertwined and inseparable; the faqih was a carrier of hadith, and his abundance of knowledge and fiqh came from the abundance of hadith he narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and the carrier of prophetic hadith was a faqih who gave fatwa to people in their issues based on the hadith he narrated. But the deviation of the Umayyad caliphs from the way of the first four Rightly Guided Caliphs in adhering to religious and practical ideals in the second half of the first century, and the internal trials and revolutions that appeared therein, drove a group of sincere and pious scholars of knowledge and fatwa to withdraw from public life and its political and social clamor. So they turned in a number of cities and regions to fiqh, studying and teaching, sometimes criticizing the rulers, and spreading knowledge among their students who accompanied them. This was the beginning of the independence of the science of fiqh. By its independence we mean that it became a specialization to which its aspirants devoted themselves, learning and teaching. And attached to each of the major scholars of fiqh was a group that received his knowledge and methodology from him and spread it among the people. In the second half of the first century, the Followers (Tabi'un) who had received from the Companions when they dispersed in the regions were many. And at the beginning of the second century, the Followers of the Followers (Tabi' al-Tabi'in) were also many. Many of these and those had their own fatwas and ijtihadi opinions on issues and cases, which were transmitted from them and recorded after them in what the authors recorded in works on the differences of jurists. But those whose complete madhhabs were recorded—i.e., opinions and fatwas in all chapters and sections of fiqh—were few, and they were those who were blessed with intelligent, knowledgeable, and active students who transmitted their opinions from them and recorded them in various cases and issues. Thus there came into being for each of them a complete, comprehensive fiqh madhhab covering the various chapters of rulings, from acts of worship to transactions, crimes, family matters (personal status). So what those early students recorded about each of those imams became the reference for knowing his madhhab—attributed to him—his fiqh methodology, and the usul principles he relied upon. Then came later layers of students from each madhhab who refined, expanded derivation (takhrij), and wrote extensively in the madhhabs of their imams, until the fiqh works in each madhhab, whether commentaries or fatwa collections, reached dozens or hundreds, ranging from abridged to voluminous works, some of which might reach thirty volumes or more. The schools: 6/12 - The madhhabs whose imams were fortunate, having intelligent, active students who recorded their opinions and thus immortalized their madhhabs, which were served with commentary, derivation, and branch rulings upon the principles of each madhhab throughout the ages, are three categories: The first category: the four schools that represent Sunni fiqh. Some have a character predominantly of the People of Opinion (Ahl al-Ra'y), and some have a character predominantly of the People of Hadith (Ahl al-Hadith). Their schools are followed today by most Muslims in the Islamic world. In every region or several regions of the Islamic world, one of these four schools dominates. They represent Sunni fiqh and are, in historical order of their imams: the Hanafi school, then the Maliki school, then the Shafi'i school, then the Hanbali school. The second category: the Zahiri school. This can be classified with the four Sunni schools because it relies on the same sources, but it differs in the extreme adherence of its imam and followers to the literal text to a degree that goes beyond reason in some matters, and from which legislative logic recoils, and which even the People of Hadith themselves do not accept, let alone the People of Opinion. Therefore their school died out and never had dominance in any region. Rather, some of their opinions are discussed today in the study of comparative fiqh.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a historical account of the development of Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh) as an independent discipline and the formation of the major legal schools (madhhabs). The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that in the early period, fiqh and hadith narration were inseparable; a faqih was also a muhaddith. However, due to political deviations and internal strife under the Umayyads, many pious scholars withdrew from public life and dedicated themselves exclusively to teaching and studying fiqh. This specialization marked the beginning of fiqh as a distinct science. These scholars attracted students who recorded their opinions and methodologies, thus forming complete madhhabs. The passage then categorizes the surviving schools into two categories: (1) the four Sunni schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) and (2) the Zahiri school, which, though based on the same sources, was extremly literalistic and eventually died out. The author notes that the Zahiri school is only studied today in comparative fiqh. This is not a ruling or a maxim but a historical and philosophical overview. Students should understand the social and political factors that led to the crystallization of madhhabs and the role of dedicated students in preserving them.

Key Terms
المذهب الظاهري (al-madhhab al-zahiri): A school founded by Dawud al-Zahiri, known for extreme literalism in interpreting texts, rejecting analogy and juristic preference.
أهل الرأي (ahl al-ra'y): Scholars who prioritized qiyas and istihsan over literal hadith reading.
أهل الحديث (ahl al-hadith): Scholars who gave primacy to hadith literalism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, what is the primary reason the Zahiri school did not survive as a living madhhab?
A) Its imam died without leaving any written works.
B) Its extreme adherence to the literal text was considered beyond reason and rejected even by the People of Hadith.
C) It was politically suppressed by the Abbasid caliphs.
D) It adopted many opinions from the Shafi'i school.

Key Takeaway
The survival of a madhhab depends not only on dedicated students but also on a methodology that balances textual commitment with rational acceptability; the four Sunni schools survived because they avoided the extreme literalism of the Zahiri school.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
Definition and Formation of a Complete Madhhab
Defining a complete madhhab and its formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define what constitutes a complete madhhab in Hanafi tradition and explain the role of students in documenting and developing the school's teachings.

Core Idea
A madhhab is the recorded and developed corpus of an imam's teachings, not just his personal opinions.

In this lesson, we explore how a legal school (madhhab) is formed. The author describes the process by which early students recorded their imam's views, and later generations expanded and refined them into a comprehensive fiqh corpus. Understanding this process is essential for a mufti to properly attribute rulings to the correct school and to know which sources are authoritative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of a complete madhhab
Look for what scope of rulings a madhhab covers: from acts of worship (ibadat) to transactions (muamalat), criminal law (jinaayat), and family matters (ahwal shakhsiyyah).
2
Note the role of early students
Recognize that it was the early students (al-talamidh al-awail) who recorded the imam's views, becoming the primary reference for the madhhab.
3
Recognize later development layers
Observe that later scholars (tabaqat lahiqa) revised, expanded, and wrote commentaries (shuruh) and fatwas, producing works of tens or hundreds of volumes.
4
Apply the concept to the Hanafi school
The Hanafi madhhab follows this pattern: Abu Hanifa is the founder, his students Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani recorded his views, and later scholars like al-Marghinani (al-Hidayah) and Ibn Abidin (Radd al-Muhtar) continued the development.
The six books of Zahir al-Riwayah are the foundational works.

الباب الثالث : أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
مذهب فقهي كامل شامل لمختلف فصول الأحكام من العبادات إلى المعاملات، فالجنايات فشؤون الأسرة الأحوال الشخصية). فكان ما دونه أولئك التلاميذ الأوائل عن كل من أولئك الأئمة هو المرجع في معرفة مذهبه الذي نُسِبَ إليه، ومنهجه الفقهي وقواعده الأصولية التي اعتمدها ثم جاء تلاميذ طبقات لاحقة من فقهاء كل مذهب فنقحوا وتوسعوا في التفريع والتخريج والتأليف في مذاهب أئمتهم حتى بلغت المؤلفات الفقهية في كل مذهب ما بين شروح وفتاوى العشرات أو المئات ما بين مختصرات ومطولات قد يبلغ حجم أحدها ثلاثين مجلداً أو أكثر. المذاهب :
٦/١٢ - فالمذاهب التي خالف أئمتها الحظ السعيد، فكان لهم تلاميذ نجباء نشيطون سجلوا آراءهم فخلدت مذاهبهم وخدمت بالشرح والتخريج والتفريع على أصول كل مذهب منها خلال العصور، هي ثلاث فئات:
الفئة الأولى : هي المذاهب الأربعة التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة، منها ما يغلب على فقهه طابع أهل الرأي، ومنها ما يغلب عليه طابع أهل الحديث. ومذاهبهم يتبعها إلى اليوم معظم المسلمين في العالم الإسلامي. وكل قطر أو بضعة أقطار من العالم الإسلامي يسود فيه أحد هذا المذاهب الأربعة. التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة وهي بحسب الترتيب التاريخي لأئمتها المذهب الحنفي، ثم المذهب المالكي، ثم المذهب الشافعي، ثم المذهب الحنبلي.
الفئة الثانية: هي المذهب الظاهري وهذا يمكن تصنيفه مع مذاهب أهل السنة الأربعة لأنه يعتمد مصادرهم نفسها ولكنه يختلف في شدة تمسك إمامه وأصحابه بحرفية النص إلى درجة تخرج عن المعقول في بعض الأمور، وينبو عنها المنطق التشريعي ولا يقبلها أهل الحديث أنفسهم فضلاً عن أهل الرأي ولذلك مات مذهبهم فلم تكن له سيادة في قطر ما، وإنما تُعرض بعض آرائهم في دراسة الفقه المقارن اليوم. ولذلك رأينا أن نعتبره وحده فئة ثانية من المذاهب الفقهية .
الفئة الثالثة : مذاهب تمثل فقه فِرق يختلف عن فقه الفئتين الأولى والثانية بأنه يرتبط ببعض أصول اعتقادية مخالفة لما عليه أهل السنة. وهذه المذاهب الأربعة الأخرى التالية :
١ - المذهب الزيدي نسبة إلى زيد بن علي زين العابدين من بيت الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم (ت ١٢٢ هـ ) وكان إماماً ذا شخصية علمية متعددة النواحي. وقد قيل : إن أبا حنيفة كان من تلاميذه. ويسود هذا المذهب في اليمن.
٢ - المذهب الإباضي نسبة إلى إمامه عبد الله بن إباض (ت٨٦ هـ) الذي كان يرى رأي الخوارج، ولكنه في الواقع لم يَجْر غُلاتهم. ويسود في سلطنة عُمان من بلاد الخليج العربي في شبه الجزيرة العربية، وله أتباع كثيرون موزعون في زنجبار وفي مناطق تونس من الشمال الأفريقي وفي جنوب الجزائر، وفيه مدوّنات فقهية واسعة.
ومذهبا الزيدية والإباضية أقرب من سواهما إلى فقه أهل السنة، وهما يعتمدان مصادر السنة النبوية التي يعتمدها أهل السنة .
٣ - المذهب الشيعي الإمامي، وهو فقه الشيعة الإمامية الاثني عشرية. وهم ينسبون أصوله إلى الإمام جعفر الصادق رضي الله عنه (ت ١٤٨ هـ) وهو من آل بيت الرسول له البارزين المتميزين بعلمهم ومكانتهم. وكان معاصراً لأبي حنيفة إمام المذهب الحنفي. وهذا المذهب يسود في إيران وفي مراكز الشيعة الإمامية من العراق وحيث يوجدون في أي قطر، وفيه مؤلفات ضافية .
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. A complete comprehensive juristic school covering all sections of rulings from acts of worship to transactions, criminal law, family matters (personal status). So what those early students recorded about each of those imams was the reference for knowing his school attributed to him, his juristic methodology, and the fundamental principles he adopted. Then came later layers of students from the jurists of each school who revised, expanded in branching, derivation, and authorship in the schools of their imams until juristic works in each school reached—between commentaries and fatwas—tens or hundreds, between summaries and lengthy works, some of which may reach thirty volumes or more. The schools: 6/12 - The schools whose imams had good fortune, so they had distinguished, active students who recorded their views and thus their schools were immortalized and served with commentary, derivation, and branching on the principles of each school throughout the ages, are three categories: The first category: the four schools that represent the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah, some of which are dominated by the character of Ahl al-Ra'y, and some by the character of Ahl al-Hadith. Their schools are followed today by most Muslims in the Islamic world. Each region or several regions of the Islamic world is dominated by one of these four schools, which represent the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah, and they are, according to the historical order of their imams: the Hanafi school, then the Maliki school, then the Shafi'i school, then the Hanbali school. The second category: the Zahiri school, which can be classified with the four Sunni schools because it relies on the same sources, but it differs in the extreme adherence of its imam and followers to the literal text to a degree that goes beyond reason in some matters, and legislative logic recoils from it, and even Ahl al-Hadith themselves do not accept it, let alone Ahl al-Ra'y. Therefore their school died out, it did not have sovereignty in any region, but some of their views are presented today in the study of comparative fiqh. Therefore we considered it alone as a second category of juristic schools. The third category: schools that represent the fiqh of sects different from the fiqh of the first and second categories because it is linked to some doctrinal principles contrary to that of Ahl al-Sunnah. These are the following four schools: 1 - The Zaydi school, attributed to Zayd ibn Ali Zayn al-Abidin from the household of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) (d. 122 AH), and he was an imam with a multifaceted scholarly personality. It is said that Abu Hanifa was among his students. This school prevails in Yemen. 2 - The Ibadi school, attributed to its imam Abdullah ibn Ibad (d. 86 AH), who held the view of the Khawarij, but in reality he did not follow their extremists. It prevails in the Sultanate of Oman in the Arabian Gulf region, and has many followers distributed in Zanzibar, areas of Tunisia in North Africa, and in southern Algeria. It has extensive juristic compilations. The Zaydi and Ibadi schools are closer than others to the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah, and they rely on the sources of the Prophetic Sunnah that Ahl al-Sunnah rely on. 3 - The Imami Shi'i school, which is the fiqh of the Twelver Imami Shi'ah. They trace its origins to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq (may Allah be pleased with him) (d. 148 AH), who is from the household of the Messenger of Allah, distinguished by their knowledge and status. He was a contemporary of Abu Hanifa, the imam of the Hanafi school. This school prevails in Iran and in the centres of the Imami Shi'ah in Iraq and wherever they exist in any region, and it has extensive works.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the author's classification of Islamic legal schools (madhhabs) during the third stage of fiqh development. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, categorizes surviving madhhabs into three categories: (1) The four Sunni schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) which have widespread following and are further characterized by either the predominance of rational opinion (ra'y) or tradition (hadith). (2) The Zahiri school, which, despite relying on the same sources as the Sunni schools, differed by literalism to an extreme degree, leading to its extinction; it is treated as a separate category for study but not as a living madhhab. (3) The schools of other sects (Zaydi, Ibadi, Imami Shi'i) that are linked to doctrinal principles different from Sunni Islam. Within this category, the Zaydi and Ibadi schools are noted as being closer to Sunni fiqh. The classification reflects historical reality and the level of development each madhhab achieved. For the Hanafi student, this contextualizes why the Hanafi school is one of the main surviving schools—thanks to dedicated students like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani—and also situates other schools for comparative understanding.

Key Terms
المذهب (al-madhhab): A complete comprehensive juristic school covering all chapters of rulings from worship to transactions, attributed to an imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the primary role of the early students in forming a madhhab?
A) They independently created new rulings without reference to the imam.
B) They recorded the imam's views and became the reference for the madhhab.
C) They only wrote commentaries on later works.
D) They ignored the imam's methodology.

Key Takeaway
A complete madhhab is a comprehensive school formed by the recording and development of an imam's teachings across generations.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
The Four Sunni Schools: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali
Listing and characterizing the four Sunni schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four Sunni schools in historical order and describe their characteristic reliance on ra'y or hadith.

Core Idea
The four Sunni schools—Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali—represent the living fiqh tradition of Ahl al-Sunnah, varying in emphasis on reason versus tradition.

This lesson examines the first category of madhhabs: the four schools that represent the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah. The author notes that these schools dominate most Muslim regions today and that some are characterized by ra'y and others by hadith. As Hanafi students, understanding this landscape helps situate our school within the broader Sunni tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four schools and their historical order
The passage lists them in order: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali. Memorize this sequence.
2
Note the characterization of each school
Some schools are predominated by Ahl al-Ra'y (rational opinion) and others by Ahl al-Hadith (tradition). The Hanafi school is famously of the Ahl al-Ra'y type.
3
Recognize their widespread following today
These schools are followed by the majority of Muslims worldwide, and each region is dominated by one of them.
4
Reflect on regional dominance
For example, the Hanafi school dominates Turkey, Central Asia, the Indian subcontinent, and parts of the Arab world.
