الباب الثالث : أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
مذهب فقهي كامل شامل لمختلف فصول الأحكام من العبادات إلى المعاملات، فالجنايات فشؤون الأسرة الأحوال الشخصية). فكان ما دونه أولئك التلاميذ الأوائل عن كل من أولئك الأئمة هو المرجع في معرفة مذهبه الذي نُسِبَ إليه، ومنهجه الفقهي وقواعده الأصولية التي اعتمدها ثم جاء تلاميذ طبقات لاحقة من فقهاء كل مذهب فنقحوا وتوسعوا في التفريع والتخريج والتأليف في مذاهب أئمتهم حتى بلغت المؤلفات الفقهية في كل مذهب ما بين شروح وفتاوى العشرات أو المئات ما بين مختصرات ومطولات قد يبلغ حجم أحدها ثلاثين مجلداً أو أكثر. المذاهب :
٦/١٢ - فالمذاهب التي خالف أئمتها الحظ السعيد، فكان لهم تلاميذ نجباء نشيطون سجلوا آراءهم فخلدت مذاهبهم وخدمت بالشرح والتخريج والتفريع على أصول كل مذهب منها خلال العصور، هي ثلاث فئات:
الفئة الأولى : هي المذاهب الأربعة التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة، منها ما يغلب على فقهه طابع أهل الرأي، ومنها ما يغلب عليه طابع أهل الحديث. ومذاهبهم يتبعها إلى اليوم معظم المسلمين في العالم الإسلامي. وكل قطر أو بضعة أقطار من العالم الإسلامي يسود فيه أحد هذا المذاهب الأربعة. التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة وهي بحسب الترتيب التاريخي لأئمتها المذهب الحنفي، ثم المذهب المالكي، ثم المذهب الشافعي، ثم المذهب الحنبلي.
الفئة الثانية: هي المذهب الظاهري وهذا يمكن تصنيفه مع مذاهب أهل السنة الأربعة لأنه يعتمد مصادرهم نفسها ولكنه يختلف في شدة تمسك إمامه وأصحابه بحرفية النص إلى درجة تخرج عن المعقول في بعض الأمور، وينبو عنها المنطق التشريعي ولا يقبلها أهل الحديث أنفسهم فضلاً عن أهل الرأي ولذلك مات مذهبهم فلم تكن له سيادة في قطر ما، وإنما تُعرض بعض آرائهم في دراسة الفقه المقارن اليوم. ولذلك رأينا أن نعتبره وحده فئة ثانية من المذاهب الفقهية .
الفئة الثالثة : مذاهب تمثل فقه فِرق يختلف عن فقه الفئتين الأولى والثانية بأنه يرتبط ببعض أصول اعتقادية مخالفة لما عليه أهل السنة. وهذه المذاهب الأربعة الأخرى التالية :
١ - المذهب الزيدي نسبة إلى زيد بن علي زين العابدين من بيت الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم (ت ١٢٢ هـ ) وكان إماماً ذا شخصية علمية متعددة النواحي. وقد قيل : إن أبا حنيفة كان من تلاميذه. ويسود هذا المذهب في اليمن.
٢ - المذهب الإباضي نسبة إلى إمامه عبد الله بن إباض (ت٨٦ هـ) الذي كان يرى رأي الخوارج، ولكنه في الواقع لم يَجْر غُلاتهم. ويسود في سلطنة عُمان من بلاد الخليج العربي في شبه الجزيرة العربية، وله أتباع كثيرون موزعون في زنجبار وفي مناطق تونس من الشمال الأفريقي وفي جنوب الجزائر، وفيه مدوّنات فقهية واسعة.
ومذهبا الزيدية والإباضية أقرب من سواهما إلى فقه أهل السنة، وهما يعتمدان مصادر السنة النبوية التي يعتمدها أهل السنة .
٣ - المذهب الشيعي الإمامي، وهو فقه الشيعة الإمامية الاثني عشرية. وهم ينسبون أصوله إلى الإمام جعفر الصادق رضي الله عنه (ت ١٤٨ هـ) وهو من آل بيت الرسول له البارزين المتميزين بعلمهم ومكانتهم. وكان معاصراً لأبي حنيفة إمام المذهب الحنفي. وهذا المذهب يسود في إيران وفي مراكز الشيعة الإمامية من العراق وحيث يوجدون في أي قطر، وفيه مؤلفات ضافية .
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. A complete comprehensive juristic school covering all sections of rulings from acts of worship to transactions, criminal law, family matters (personal status). So what those early students recorded about each of those imams was the reference for knowing his school attributed to him, his juristic methodology, and the fundamental principles he adopted. Then came later layers of students from the jurists of each school who revised, expanded in branching, derivation, and authorship in the schools of their imams until juristic works in each school reached—between commentaries and fatwas—tens or hundreds, between summaries and lengthy works, some of which may reach thirty volumes or more. The schools: 6/12 - The schools whose imams had good fortune, so they had distinguished, active students who recorded their views and thus their schools were immortalized and served with commentary, derivation, and branching on the principles of each school throughout the ages, are three categories: The first category: the four schools that represent the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah, some of which are dominated by the character of Ahl al-Ra'y, and some by the character of Ahl al-Hadith. Their schools are followed today by most Muslims in the Islamic world. Each region or several regions of the Islamic world is dominated by one of these four schools, which represent the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah, and they are, according to the historical order of their imams: the Hanafi school, then the Maliki school, then the Shafi'i school, then the Hanbali school. The second category: the Zahiri school, which can be classified with the four Sunni schools because it relies on the same sources, but it differs in the extreme adherence of its imam and followers to the literal text to a degree that goes beyond reason in some matters, and legislative logic recoils from it, and even Ahl al-Hadith themselves do not accept it, let alone Ahl al-Ra'y. Therefore their school died out, it did not have sovereignty in any region, but some of their views are presented today in the study of comparative fiqh. Therefore we considered it alone as a second category of juristic schools. The third category: schools that represent the fiqh of sects different from the fiqh of the first and second categories because it is linked to some doctrinal principles contrary to that of Ahl al-Sunnah. These are the following four schools: 1 - The Zaydi school, attributed to Zayd ibn Ali Zayn al-Abidin from the household of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) (d. 122 AH), and he was an imam with a multifaceted scholarly personality. It is said that Abu Hanifa was among his students. This school prevails in Yemen. 2 - The Ibadi school, attributed to its imam Abdullah ibn Ibad (d. 86 AH), who held the view of the Khawarij, but in reality he did not follow their extremists. It prevails in the Sultanate of Oman in the Arabian Gulf region, and has many followers distributed in Zanzibar, areas of Tunisia in North Africa, and in southern Algeria. It has extensive juristic compilations. The Zaydi and Ibadi schools are closer than others to the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah, and they rely on the sources of the Prophetic Sunnah that Ahl al-Sunnah rely on. 3 - The Imami Shi'i school, which is the fiqh of the Twelver Imami Shi'ah. They trace its origins to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq (may Allah be pleased with him) (d. 148 AH), who is from the household of the Messenger of Allah, distinguished by their knowledge and status. He was a contemporary of Abu Hanifa, the imam of the Hanafi school. This school prevails in Iran and in the centres of the Imami Shi'ah in Iraq and wherever they exist in any region, and it has extensive works.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the author's classification of Islamic legal schools (madhhabs) during the third stage of fiqh development. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, categorizes surviving madhhabs into three categories: (1) The four Sunni schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) which have widespread following and are further characterized by either the predominance of rational opinion (ra'y) or tradition (hadith). (2) The Zahiri school, which, despite relying on the same sources as the Sunni schools, differed by literalism to an extreme degree, leading to its extinction; it is treated as a separate category for study but not as a living madhhab. (3) The schools of other sects (Zaydi, Ibadi, Imami Shi'i) that are linked to doctrinal principles different from Sunni Islam. Within this category, the Zaydi and Ibadi schools are noted as being closer to Sunni fiqh. The classification reflects historical reality and the level of development each madhhab achieved. For the Hanafi student, this contextualizes why the Hanafi school is one of the main surviving schools—thanks to dedicated students like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani—and also situates other schools for comparative understanding.

Key Terms
أهل السنة (Ahl al-Sunnah): The mainstream Muslim community following the Sunni creed and the four schools of fiqh.
أهل الرأي (Ahl al-Ra'y): Those jurists who rely heavily on reasoned opinion and analogical deduction (qiyas) in deriving rulings.
أهل الحديث (Ahl al-Hadith): Those jurists who prioritize textual evidence (hadith) and are cautious with qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In what order does the author list the four Sunni schools?
A) Maliki, Hanafi, Shafi'i, Hanbali
B) Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali
C) Shafi'i, Hanafi, Maliki, Hanbali
D) Hanbali, Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanafi

Key Takeaway
The four Sunni schools differ in methodological emphasis but collectively represent the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah and dominate the Islamic world.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
The Zahiri School: A Separate Category
Explaining the Zahiri school's separate status and decline

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author places the Zahiri school in a separate category and evaluate the reasons for its decline.

Core Idea
The Zahiri school's extreme literalism, which even Ahl al-Hadith rejected, led to its extinction; it is studied only for comparative fiqh.

This lesson focuses on the second category: the Zahiri school. Though it shares sources with Sunni schools, its rigid adherence to the literal text (حرفية النص) went beyond accepted reason, causing its abandonment. The author's classification highlights the importance of balanced methodology for a school's survival.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Zahiri school's sources
It relies on the same sources as the Sunni schools (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas? Actually, Zahiris reject qiyas). But the author says it uses the same sources.
Note the contradiction: They reject qiyas but the author still says they use 'their same sources'.
2
Note the extreme literalism
The passage emphasizes 'شدة تمسك إمامه وأصحابه بحرفية النص' (extreme adherence to the literal text) to an unreasonable degree.
3
Recognize the rejection by other scholars
Even Ahl al-Hadith did not accept this literalism, let alone Ahl al-Ra'y.
4
Understand the consequence: extinction
The school died out (مات مذهبهم) and never dominated any region.
5
Apply the insight for today
Zahiri opinions appear only in comparative fiqh studies, not as operative law.

Commentary
This passage presents the author's classification of Islamic legal schools (madhhabs) during the third stage of fiqh development. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, categorizes surviving madhhabs into three categories: (1) The four Sunni schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) which have widespread following and are further characterized by either the predominance of rational opinion (ra'y) or tradition (hadith). (2) The Zahiri school, which, despite relying on the same sources as the Sunni schools, differed by literalism to an extreme degree, leading to its extinction; it is treated as a separate category for study but not as a living madhhab. (3) The schools of other sects (Zaydi, Ibadi, Imami Shi'i) that are linked to doctrinal principles different from Sunni Islam. Within this category, the Zaydi and Ibadi schools are noted as being closer to Sunni fiqh. The classification reflects historical reality and the level of development each madhhab achieved. For the Hanafi student, this contextualizes why the Hanafi school is one of the main surviving schools—thanks to dedicated students like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani—and also situates other schools for comparative understanding.

Key Terms
المذهب الظاهري (al-madhhab al-Zahiri): A juristic school founded by Dawud al-Zahiri, characterized by strict adherence to the literal text (zahir) and rejection of qiyas and ra'y.
حرفية النص (harfiyyat al-nass): Literal adherence to the exact wording of a text, without allowing interpretation or analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author cite as the primary reason for the Zahiri school's decline?
A) It did not have any students.
B) Its extreme literalism was rejected by scholars.
C) It opposed the use of the Quran.
D) It was politically suppressed.

Key Takeaway
The Zahiri school's extreme literalism led to its extinction; it is not an operative school, unlike the four living Sunni schools.

Non-Sunni Madhhabs: Zaydi, Ibadi, and Imami Shi'i
Introducing the third category: schools based on different creeds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three non-Sunni madhhabs listed in the third category and describe their doctrinal differences from Ahl al-Sunnah.

Core Idea
The Zaydi, Ibadi, and Imami Shi'i schools differ from Sunni fiqh because they are tied to distinct creeds, though the first two are closer to Sunni sources.

This lesson covers the third category of madhhabs: those of sects with different foundational principles. The Zaydi and Ibadi schools are noted to be closer to Ahl al-Sunnah in relying on the Prophetic sources. The Imami Shi'i school traces its fiqh to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq, a contemporary of Abu Hanifa. Understanding these schools is crucial for comparative fiqh study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three schools
List them: Zaydi, Ibadi, Imami Shi'i. Note their founders: Zayd ibn Ali, Abdullah ibn Ibad, and Ja'far al-Sadiq (as source).
The author mentions only three schools despite saying 'four other schools'.
2
Note the general difference from Sunni fiqh
They are linked to doctrinal principles (أصول اعتقادية) that differ from Ahl al-Sunnah.
3
Recognize that Zaydi and Ibadi are closer to Sunni fiqh
They rely on the same Prophetic Sunnah sources as Ahl al-Sunnah.
4
Note their geographical prevalence
Zaydi in Yemen, Ibadi in Oman and parts of Africa, Imami in Iran and Iraq.
5
Relate to Hanafi school
Abu Hanifa was a contemporary of Ja'far al-Sadiq, and it is said Abu Hanifa studied under Zayd ibn Ali.

الباب الثالث : أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
مذهب فقهي كامل شامل لمختلف فصول الأحكام من العبادات إلى المعاملات، فالجنايات فشؤون الأسرة الأحوال الشخصية). فكان ما دونه أولئك التلاميذ الأوائل عن كل من أولئك الأئمة هو المرجع في معرفة مذهبه الذي نُسِبَ إليه، ومنهجه الفقهي وقواعده الأصولية التي اعتمدها ثم جاء تلاميذ طبقات لاحقة من فقهاء كل مذهب فنقحوا وتوسعوا في التفريع والتخريج والتأليف في مذاهب أئمتهم حتى بلغت المؤلفات الفقهية في كل مذهب ما بين شروح وفتاوى العشرات أو المئات ما بين مختصرات ومطولات قد يبلغ حجم أحدها ثلاثين مجلداً أو أكثر. المذاهب :
٦/١٢ - فالمذاهب التي خالف أئمتها الحظ السعيد، فكان لهم تلاميذ نجباء نشيطون سجلوا آراءهم فخلدت مذاهبهم وخدمت بالشرح والتخريج والتفريع على أصول كل مذهب منها خلال العصور، هي ثلاث فئات:
الفئة الأولى : هي المذاهب الأربعة التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة، منها ما يغلب على فقهه طابع أهل الرأي، ومنها ما يغلب عليه طابع أهل الحديث. ومذاهبهم يتبعها إلى اليوم معظم المسلمين في العالم الإسلامي. وكل قطر أو بضعة أقطار من العالم الإسلامي يسود فيه أحد هذا المذاهب الأربعة. التي تمثل فقه أهل السنة وهي بحسب الترتيب التاريخي لأئمتها المذهب الحنفي، ثم المذهب المالكي، ثم المذهب الشافعي، ثم المذهب الحنبلي.
الفئة الثانية: هي المذهب الظاهري وهذا يمكن تصنيفه مع مذاهب أهل السنة الأربعة لأنه يعتمد مصادرهم نفسها ولكنه يختلف في شدة تمسك إمامه وأصحابه بحرفية النص إلى درجة تخرج عن المعقول في بعض الأمور، وينبو عنها المنطق التشريعي ولا يقبلها أهل الحديث أنفسهم فضلاً عن أهل الرأي ولذلك مات مذهبهم فلم تكن له سيادة في قطر ما، وإنما تُعرض بعض آرائهم في دراسة الفقه المقارن اليوم. ولذلك رأينا أن نعتبره وحده فئة ثانية من المذاهب الفقهية .
الفئة الثالثة : مذاهب تمثل فقه فِرق يختلف عن فقه الفئتين الأولى والثانية بأنه يرتبط ببعض أصول اعتقادية مخالفة لما عليه أهل السنة. وهذه المذاهب الأربعة الأخرى التالية :
١ - المذهب الزيدي نسبة إلى زيد بن علي زين العابدين من بيت الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم (ت ١٢٢ هـ ) وكان إماماً ذا شخصية علمية متعددة النواحي. وقد قيل : إن أبا حنيفة كان من تلاميذه. ويسود هذا المذهب في اليمن.
٢ - المذهب الإباضي نسبة إلى إمامه عبد الله بن إباض (ت٨٦ هـ) الذي كان يرى رأي الخوارج، ولكنه في الواقع لم يَجْر غُلاتهم. ويسود في سلطنة عُمان من بلاد الخليج العربي في شبه الجزيرة العربية، وله أتباع كثيرون موزعون في زنجبار وفي مناطق تونس من الشمال الأفريقي وفي جنوب الجزائر، وفيه مدوّنات فقهية واسعة.
ومذهبا الزيدية والإباضية أقرب من سواهما إلى فقه أهل السنة، وهما يعتمدان مصادر السنة النبوية التي يعتمدها أهل السنة .
٣ - المذهب الشيعي الإمامي، وهو فقه الشيعة الإمامية الاثني عشرية. وهم ينسبون أصوله إلى الإمام جعفر الصادق رضي الله عنه (ت ١٤٨ هـ) وهو من آل بيت الرسول له البارزين المتميزين بعلمهم ومكانتهم. وكان معاصراً لأبي حنيفة إمام المذهب الحنفي. وهذا المذهب يسود في إيران وفي مراكز الشيعة الإمامية من العراق وحيث يوجدون في أي قطر، وفيه مؤلفات ضافية .
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. A complete comprehensive juristic school covering all sections of rulings from acts of worship to transactions, criminal law, family matters (personal status). So what those early students recorded about each of those imams was the reference for knowing his school attributed to him, his juristic methodology, and the fundamental principles he adopted. Then came later layers of students from the jurists of each school who revised, expanded in branching, derivation, and authorship in the schools of their imams until juristic works in each school reached—between commentaries and fatwas—tens or hundreds, between summaries and lengthy works, some of which may reach thirty volumes or more. The schools: 6/12 - The schools whose imams had good fortune, so they had distinguished, active students who recorded their views and thus their schools were immortalized and served with commentary, derivation, and branching on the principles of each school throughout the ages, are three categories: The first category: the four schools that represent the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah, some of which are dominated by the character of Ahl al-Ra'y, and some by the character of Ahl al-Hadith. Their schools are followed today by most Muslims in the Islamic world. Each region or several regions of the Islamic world is dominated by one of these four schools, which represent the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah, and they are, according to the historical order of their imams: the Hanafi school, then the Maliki school, then the Shafi'i school, then the Hanbali school. The second category: the Zahiri school, which can be classified with the four Sunni schools because it relies on the same sources, but it differs in the extreme adherence of its imam and followers to the literal text to a degree that goes beyond reason in some matters, and legislative logic recoils from it, and even Ahl al-Hadith themselves do not accept it, let alone Ahl al-Ra'y. Therefore their school died out, it did not have sovereignty in any region, but some of their views are presented today in the study of comparative fiqh. Therefore we considered it alone as a second category of juristic schools. The third category: schools that represent the fiqh of sects different from the fiqh of the first and second categories because it is linked to some doctrinal principles contrary to that of Ahl al-Sunnah. These are the following four schools: 1 - The Zaydi school, attributed to Zayd ibn Ali Zayn al-Abidin from the household of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) (d. 122 AH), and he was an imam with a multifaceted scholarly personality. It is said that Abu Hanifa was among his students. This school prevails in Yemen. 2 - The Ibadi school, attributed to its imam Abdullah ibn Ibad (d. 86 AH), who held the view of the Khawarij, but in reality he did not follow their extremists. It prevails in the Sultanate of Oman in the Arabian Gulf region, and has many followers distributed in Zanzibar, areas of Tunisia in North Africa, and in southern Algeria. It has extensive juristic compilations. The Zaydi and Ibadi schools are closer than others to the fiqh of Ahl al-Sunnah, and they rely on the sources of the Prophetic Sunnah that Ahl al-Sunnah rely on. 3 - The Imami Shi'i school, which is the fiqh of the Twelver Imami Shi'ah. They trace its origins to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq (may Allah be pleased with him) (d. 148 AH), who is from the household of the Messenger of Allah, distinguished by their knowledge and status. He was a contemporary of Abu Hanifa, the imam of the Hanafi school. This school prevails in Iran and in the centres of the Imami Shi'ah in Iraq and wherever they exist in any region, and it has extensive works.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the author's classification of Islamic legal schools (madhhabs) during the third stage of fiqh development. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, categorizes surviving madhhabs into three categories: (1) The four Sunni schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) which have widespread following and are further characterized by either the predominance of rational opinion (ra'y) or tradition (hadith). (2) The Zahiri school, which, despite relying on the same sources as the Sunni schools, differed by literalism to an extreme degree, leading to its extinction; it is treated as a separate category for study but not as a living madhhab. (3) The schools of other sects (Zaydi, Ibadi, Imami Shi'i) that are linked to doctrinal principles different from Sunni Islam. Within this category, the Zaydi and Ibadi schools are noted as being closer to Sunni fiqh. The classification reflects historical reality and the level of development each madhhab achieved. For the Hanafi student, this contextualizes why the Hanafi school is one of the main surviving schools—thanks to dedicated students like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani—and also situates other schools for comparative understanding.

Key Terms
المذهب الزيدي (al-madhhab al-Zaydi): A juristic school attributed to Zayd ibn Ali Zayn al-Abidin, followed in Yemen. It is close to Ahl al-Sunnah in relying on Prophetic Sunnah.
المذهب الإباضي (al-madhhab al-Ibadi): A juristic school attributed to Abdullah ibn Ibad, associated with a moderate branch of Khawarij. Followed in Oman, Zanzibar, and parts of Africa.
المذهب الشيعي الإمامي (al-madhhab al-Shi'i al-Imami): The Twelver Imami Shi'i school, tracing its fiqh to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq. Followed in Iran and Iraq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which school is noted to have a connection to Abu Hanifa?
A) Ibadi
B) Zaydi
C) Imami Shi'i
D) Zahiri

Key Takeaway
The third category includes madhhabs with distinct doctrinal foundations; the Zaydi and Ibadi are closer to Sunni fiqh than the Imami Shi'i.

Third Category Madhhabs: Zaydi, Ibadi, and Imami Schools
Listing non-Sunni Islamic legal schools and their characteristics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three non-Sunni madhhabs in the third category, state their founders, and explain how they relate to Sunni fiqh.

Core Idea
Zaydi and Ibadi schools are closer to Sunni fiqh than Imami Shi'i, sharing most sources.

This passage introduces the third category of Islamic legal schools, which differ from the four Sunni schools in doctrinal foundations. As a future mufti, understanding these schools helps in inter-madhhab relations and recognizing the broader fiqh landscape. The author highlights that Zaydi and Ibadi schools are relatively close to Ahl al-Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Schools
List the three madhhabs mentioned: Zaydi, Ibadi, and Imami.
2
Note Founders and Origins
Associate each with its founder: Zaydi → Zayd ibn Ali; Ibadi → Abdullah ibn Ibad; Imami → Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq.
3
Assess Doctrinal Distance
According to the author, Zaydi and Ibadi are closer to Sunni fiqh and use similar sources; Imami is distinct.
4
Geographic Spread
Note where each predominates: Zaydi in Yemen, Ibadi in Oman and East Africa, Imami in Iran and Iraq.

The third category: schools that represent the fiqh of groups that differ from the fiqh of the first two categories in that it is connected to some doctrinal principles contrary to those of Ahl al-Sunnah. These are the following four other schools: 1. The Zaydi school, attributed to Zayd ibn Ali Zayn al-Abidin from the household of the Prophet (d. 122 AH). Commentary
This passage presents the third category of Islamic legal schools, which are distinct from the Sunni schools due to their doctrinal foundations. The author lists three schools: the Zaydi (prominent in Yemen), Ibadi (prominent in Oman and parts of Africa), and Imami Shi'i (predominant in Iran). He notes that the Zaydi and Ibadi schools are closer to Sunni fiqh and rely on similar Prophetic sources, while the Imami school is attributed to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq. This classification helps students understand the broader landscape of Islamic jurisprudence beyond the four Sunni madhhabs.

Key Terms
المذهب الزيدي (al-madhhab al-Zaydi): A school of thought attributed to Zayd ibn Ali Zayn al-Abidin, predominant in Yemen.
المذهب الإباضي (al-madhhab al-Ibadi): A school originating from Abdullah ibn Ibad, predominant in Oman and parts of East Africa and North Africa.
المذهب الشيعي الإمامي (al-madhhab al-Shi'i al-Imami): The Twelver Imami Shi'i school attributed to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq, predominant in Iran.

The Musarrah Case: Ahl al-Hadith vs Ahl al-Ra'y
Analyzing the musarrah dispute as a paradigm of methodological divergence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the musarrah case and evaluate the methodological difference between Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school interprets the hadith on musarrah as a specific judgment, not a general rule, applying compensation by value.

The dispute over the tied-udder sheep is a classic example of how Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y diverge in their approach to textual evidence. Understanding this case illuminates the Hanafi methodology of purposive interpretation and its commitment to general principles of justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its apparent ruling
State the content of the musarrah hadith: the buyer may return the sheep with a sa' of dates. Note that Ahl al-Hadith take this literally.
The hadith is from Abu Hurairah.
2
State the Ahl al-Hadith position
Ahl al-Hadith adhere to the literal meaning: the buyer owes a sa' of dates as compensation for the milk.
3
State the Ahl al-Ra'y position and their dalil
Ahl al-Ra'y argue that the general principle of compensation (damān) requires the value (qīma) of the milk, not a fixed measure. They point out that a sa' of dates may not be equivalent.
The Hanafi school represents this view.
4
Explain the Hanafi interpretation (takhsis)
The Hanafi response: The hadith is a specific judgment (qadiyyat 'ayn) for that time when dates were readily available. It does not establish a general rule. The general principle of value governs.
This is based on usul al-fiqh: a general text may be specified by context.
5
Determine the mufta bihi ruling
The operative ruling for fatwa is that the buyer returns the sheep with the value of the milk, not a sa' of dates.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
أخطأ، بل أقرّ كلاً منهما على ما فعل(١). ٩/١٢ - المثال الثاني : مسألة الشاة المصراة:
والغنم
روى أبو هريرة رضي الله عنه أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن تصرية الإبل
(٢)
وقال :
لا تُصَروا الإبل والغنم فمن ابتاعها بعد فهو بخير النظرين بعد أن يحلبها : إن شاء ،أمسك، وإن شاء رَدَّها وصاعاً من تمر» (٣). فأهل الحديث يقولون يردها المشتري، وصاعاً من تمر
أن
اللبن الذي احتلبه منها، كما جاء في هذا الحديث النبوي. وأهل الرأي يقولون : إنَّ قانون ضمان المتلفات في الشريعة الإسلامية إنما هو يرد المثل في إتلاف المثليات أو القيمة في القيميات وهذا الحديث بظاهره يجعل اللبن الذي استهلكه المشتري مقدراً بغير مثله ولا قيمته. واختلف رأيهم في النتيجة وأحسن آرائهم أنه يردها مع قيمة اللبن الذي احتلبه منها وليس مع صاع من تمر لأنَّ هذا التمر قد يكون أكثر قيمة من اللبن المحلوب أو أقل.
ويؤولون الحديث المذكور بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قدر أن اللبن المحلوب في تلك الحال يكافؤه صاع من تمر والتمر هو المال الميسور لديهم جميعاً إذ ذاك فقضى به. ولكن الأصل الواجب شرعاً في التعادل هو القيمة، فلا يفيد
الحديث الوارد في الموضوع أنه حكم عام، وإنما هو لحادثة بعينها. أما الحكم العام فيرجع فيه إلى الأصل.
هذان مثلان نموذجيان لاختلاف أهل مدرسة الحديث ومدرسة الرأي، وهناك أمثلة فيها غلو صارخ من أهل الحديث لا يقبله الذوق الفقهي البصير بمقاصد الشريعة، كما أنه لم يخل أهل الرأي في بعض مسائلهم من غلو في تأويل بعض الأحاديث بما يخرج عن المعتاد في فهم الخطاب، ويظهر فيه التكلف أو التمحل.
معنى «الرأي» :
١٠/١٢ ـ وهنا يجدر التنبيه إلى ناحية مهمة، وهي أن المقصود بالرأي عندما نقول: (أهل الرأي وأهل الحديث ليس هو تقرير الأحكام بالتشهي والهوى أو ما يختاره المفتي والقاضي بعقله المجرد دون تقيد بشيء من نصوص الشريعة ومقاصدها العامة التي سبق بيانها في القسم الأول من هذا البحث كما قد يتوهمه من لا علم له. وإنما المراد بالرأي لدى أهل الرأي عند سكوت النص، أو لأجل فهمه وتطبيقه) هو النظر في علة النص وغايته وسبب وروده لمعرفة المراد به من أجل حسن تطبيقه تطبيقاً صحيحاً. وفي حالة عدم النص فإنَّ المراد بالرأي الرجوع إلى مقاصد الشريعة وميزان المصالح والمفاسد بنظر الشارع (لا) بنظر الشخص وهواه كما تقدم بيانه .
يقول الأستاذ الجليل محمد أبو زهرة رحمه الله :
إنَّ المراجع لفتاوى الصحابة والتابعين .. يفهم من معنى الرأي ما يشمل اعتماد الفقيه في فتواه على ما عُرف من الدين بروحه العام، أو ما أحكامه في جملتها في نظر المفتي، أو ما يكون مشابهاً لأمر منصوص عليه فيها .. وعلى ذلك يكون الرأي شاملاً للقياس والاستحسان والمصالح المرسلة والعرف من كتاب: «مالك»، ص ١٤٩، باختصار).
يتفق مع
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. He erred, rather he approved each of them for what he did. 9/12 - The Second Example: The Issue of the Tied-Udder Sheep. And sheep. Abu Hurairah narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade the tying of the udders of camels and sheep, and said: 'Do not tie the udders of camels and sheep; whoever buys them after that has the choice after milking them: if he wishes, he may keep them, and if he wishes, he may return them along with a sa' of dates.' So the people of hadith say that the buyer returns them and a sa' of dates for the milk he milked from them, as stated in this prophetic hadith. The people of opinion say that the law of compensation for damages in Islamic law is to return the equivalent for fungible things or the value for non-fungible things, and this hadith in its apparent meaning makes the milk consumed by the buyer measured by something other than its equivalent or value. Their opinions differed on the outcome, and the best of their views is that he returns it with the value of the milk he milked from it, not with a sa' of dates, because this sa' of dates may be more valuable than the milk or less. They interpret the aforementioned hadith by saying that the Prophet (peace be upon him) estimated that the milk in that situation was equivalent to a sa' of dates, and dates were the readily available commodity among them at that time, so he ruled accordingly. But the legally obligatory principle for equivalence is value, so the hadith does not indicate that it is a general ruling; rather it is for a specific incident. As for the general ruling, reference is made to the principle. These are two typical examples of the difference between the school of hadith and the school of opinion. There are examples of extreme literalism from the people of hadith that the refined juristic sense aware of the purposes of sharia does not accept, just as the people of opinion were not free from excess in interpreting some hadith in a way that deviates from the usual understanding of discourse, showing artificiality or forced interpretation. The Meaning of 'Ra'y': 10/12 - Here it is important to note an important point: that what is meant by 'ra'y' when we say 'people of opinion and people of hadith' is not the determination of rulings by whim and desire or what the mufti and judge choose by their bare intellect without being bound by anything from the texts of sharia and its general purposes, which were previously explained in the first section of this research, as one who has no knowledge might imagine. Rather, what is meant by 'ra'y' among the people of opinion, when the text is silent, or for its understanding and application, is consideration of the rationale behind the text, its purpose, and the reason for its revelation to understand its intended meaning for the purpose of correct application. In the absence of a text, what is meant by 'ra'y' is recourse to the purposes of sharia and the scale of benefits and harms according to the perspective of the Lawgiver (not) according to the person's own view and whim, as previously explained. The esteemed Professor Muhammad Abu Zahrah (may Allah have mercy on him) says: 'One who reviews the fatwas of the Companions and Followers... understands from the meaning of ra'y that it includes the jurist's reliance in his fatwa on what is known of the religion by its general spirit, or what its rulings in their entirety seem to the mufti, or what is similar to a matter textually ruled upon... and thus ra'y includes qiyas, istihsan, masalih mursalah, and custom.' (From the book 'Malik', p. 149, abridged). It agrees with.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the historical methodological differences between Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y, presented as part of the development of Islamic fiqh. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, offers two concrete examples. The first is briefly referenced: a dispute over praying in the road, where the author notes that both sides were considered correct. The second example is detailed: the case of the musarrah (tied-udder) sheep. The hadith explicitly allows the buyer to return the sheep with a sa' of dates. Ahl al-Hadith take the hadith literally. Ahl al-Ra'y, however, argue that the general principle of compensation (damān) requires returning the equivalent or value, not a sa' of dates which may not be equivalent. They interpret the hadith as a specific ruling for that time when dates were common currency. The author endorses the view that the buyer returns the sheep with the value of the milk. He then notes excesses on both sides: some Ahl al-Hadith are too literal, some Ahl al-Ra'y over-interpret. Finally, he clarifies the meaning of 'ra'y': it is not arbitrary opinion but rigorous legal reasoning based on the texts, their purposes, and the maqasid of sharia. He quotes Muhammad Abu Zahrah to show that ra'y includes qiyas, istihsan, masalih mursalah, and custom. This passage is significant for understanding the Hanafi reliance on ra'y as a legitimate method within the boundaries of sharia.

Key Terms
المصراة (al-musarrah): An animal (camel or sheep) whose udder is tied to give the impression of abundant milk, a form of fraud.
الرأي (al-ra'y): Sound legal reasoning based on the rationale (illa) and purpose of texts, and on the general principles of sharia; not arbitrary opinion.
أهل الحديث (Ahl al-Hadith): Those who adhere strictly to the literal wording of hadith, often opposing analogical reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes general principles over isolated textual evidence when the text is context-specific, as demonstrated in the musarrah case.

The Meaning of Ra'y in Hanafi Usul
Clarifying the technical meaning of 'ra'y' in early Hanafi jurisprudence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ra'y as used in Hanafi literature and explain its scope as a disciplined method of legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Ra'y is not arbitrary opinion but rigorous reasoning based on the purposes of sharia and the spirit of the texts.

The term ra'y has often been misunderstood as mere personal opinion. This lesson clarifies its technical meaning in Hanafi usul, distinguishing it from whim and showing it as a legitimate methodology rooted in the sources.

Guided Path
1
State the misconception about ra'y
Identify the common false understanding: that ra'y means rulings by whim and personal desire without textual basis.
This misconception is refuted in the passage.
2
Define ra'y as used by Ahl al-Ra'y
Ra'y is consideration of the rationale (illa), purpose (ghayah), and reason for revelation (sabab al-wurud) to understand and apply texts correctly. When no text exists, it is recourse to the maqasid of sharia and the scale of benefits and harms according to the Lawgiver.
This definition is from the passage and aligns with Hanafi usul.
3
Identify the tools of ra'y
According to Muhammad Abu Zahrah, ra'y includes qiyas, istihsan, masalih mursalah, and custom. List these and give a brief example of each.
The passage quotes Abu Zahrah to this effect.
4
Explain the boundaries of ra'y
Ra'y is bound by the nusus (texts) and the general purposes of sharia. It is not unconstrained; it works within the framework of the law.
The passage stresses that ra'y is not from personal view but from the perspective of the Lawgiver.
5
Note the historical context
Understand that the term was used by early Hanafi jurists to describe their methodology, and it gained a distinct technical meaning over time.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
أخطأ، بل أقرّ كلاً منهما على ما فعل(١). ٩/١٢ - المثال الثاني : مسألة الشاة المصراة:
والغنم
روى أبو هريرة رضي الله عنه أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن تصرية الإبل

لا تُصَروا الإبل والغنم فمن ابتاعها بعد فهو بخير النظرين بعد أن يحلبها : إن شاء ،أمسك، وإن شاء رَدَّها وصاعاً من تمر» (٣). فأهل الحديث يقولون يردها المشتري، وصاعاً من تمر
أن
اللبن الذي احتلبه منها، كما جاء في هذا الحديث النبوي. وأهل الرأي يقولون : إنَّ قانون ضمان المتلفات في الشريعة الإسلامية إنما هو يرد المثل في إتلاف المثليات أو القيمة في القيميات وهذا الحديث بظاهره يجعل اللبن الذي استهلكه المشتري مقدراً بغير مثله ولا قيمته. واختلف رأيهم في النتيجة وأحسن آرائهم أنه يردها مع قيمة اللبن الذي احتلبه منها وليس مع صاع من تمر لأنَّ هذا التمر قد يكون أكثر قيمة من اللبن المحلوب أو أقل.
ويؤولون الحديث المذكور بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قدر أن اللبن المحلوب في تلك الحال يكافؤه صاع من تمر والتمر هو المال الميسور لديهم جميعاً إذ ذاك فقضى به. ولكن الأصل الواجب شرعاً في التعادل هو القيمة، فلا يفيد
الحديث الوارد في الموضوع أنه حكم عام، وإنما هو لحادثة بعينها. أما الحكم العام فيرجع فيه إلى الأصل.
هذان مثلان نموذجيان لاختلاف أهل مدرسة الحديث ومدرسة الرأي، وهناك أمثلة فيها غلو صارخ من أهل الحديث لا يقبله الذوق الفقهي البصير بمقاصد الشريعة، كما أنه لم يخل أهل الرأي في بعض مسائلهم من غلو في تأويل بعض الأحاديث بما يخرج عن المعتاد في فهم الخطاب، ويظهر فيه التكلف أو التمحل.
معنى «الرأي» :
١٠/١٢ ـ وهنا يجدر التنبيه إلى ناحية مهمة، وهي أن المقصود بالرأي عندما نقول: (أهل الرأي وأهل الحديث ليس هو تقرير الأحكام بالتشهي والهوى أو ما يختاره المفتي والقاضي بعقله المجرد دون تقيد بشيء من نصوص الشريعة ومقاصدها العامة التي سبق بيانها في القسم الأول من هذا البحث كما قد يتوهمه من لا علم له. وإنما المراد بالرأي لدى أهل الرأي عند سكوت النص، أو لأجل فهمه وتطبيقه) هو النظر في علة النص وغايته وسبب وروده لمعرفة المراد به من أجل حسن تطبيقه تطبيقاً صحيحاً. وفي حالة عدم النص فإنَّ المراد بالرأي الرجوع إلى مقاصد الشريعة وميزان المصالح والمفاسد بنظر الشارع (لا) بنظر الشخص وهواه كما تقدم بيانه .
يقول الأستاذ الجليل محمد أبو زهرة رحمه الله :
إنَّ المراجع لفتاوى الصحابة والتابعين .. يفهم من معنى الرأي ما يشمل اعتماد الفقيه في فتواه على ما عُرف من الدين بروحه العام، أو ما أحكامه في جملتها في نظر المفتي، أو ما يكون مشابهاً لأمر منصوص عليه فيها .. وعلى ذلك يكون الرأي شاملاً للقياس والاستحسان والمصالح المرسلة والعرف من كتاب: «مالك»، ص ١٤٩، باختصار).

History and Development of Fiqh

The Fourth Fiqh Period: Rise of the Major Madhhabs
Historical overview of the fourth fiqh period and madhhab emergence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the emergence of the Hanafi and Maliki schools within the context of the Ahl al-Ra'y and Ahl al-Hadith methodological divide, and identify the contributions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school emerged as the leading rationalist madhhab, balancing textual sources with legal objectives through istihsan.

The fourth period (early 2nd to mid 4th century AH) saw the flourishing of independent madhhabs. This lesson explores the methodological split between Ahl al-Ra'y and Ahl al-Hadith, which shaped the Hanafi and Maliki schools. We will also introduce the founding figures and their key contributions, with special attention to the Hanafi school and its codification by al-Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical context
Identify the time frame of the fourth fiqh period (early 2nd to mid 4th century AH) and recognize that this era saw the expansion and systematization of fiqh, with the emergence of the four major madhhabs among many others.
The passage mentions 'numerous madhhabs and juristic interpretations' beyond the four.
2
Distinguish the two methodological approaches
Contrast Ahl al-Hadith (strict adherence to the apparent meaning of texts) and Ahl al-Ra'y (greater weight to general objectives and wisdom of the text). Understand that this dichotomy is described as a universal feature of legal systems.
The author presents this as a natural divergence in any civilized human environment.
3
Locate the Hanafi school within the dichotomy
The Hanafi school is the culmination of Ahl al-Ra'y, with Abu Hanifa establishing the method of istihsan. Note that the Hanafi school is not purely rational; it relies on hadith but balances it with ra'y.
The passage calls Abu Hanifa the 'imam of the Iraqis' and emphasizes his strength of argument and deduction.
4
Identify Abu Hanifa's students and their role
Know the two most famous students: Abu Yusuf (chief judge under Harun al-Rashid, author of al-Kharaj) and Muhammad al-Shaybani (brilliant jurist and linguist). Together they are called al-Sahibayn and are credited with codifying and spreading Hanafi fiqh.
Their opinions sometimes differ from Abu Hanifa's, forming intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Compare the Hanafi and Maliki approaches
The Maliki school is described as moderate between the two extremes, relying heavily on hadith but also counting Malik among Ahl al-Ra'y. Its principles are closer to ra'y despite its Medinan origin.
This comparison helps situate the Hanafi school as more firmly rationalist.

Chapter Three: Stages of the Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics. It applies to one of the four madhhabs. The closest to which it applies is the Hanafi madhhab. But even this is contradicted by what we presented in clause (A) above. And it appears to us that the interpretation closest to reality and farthest from affectation is that the difference in methodology between the schools of Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y is a manifestation of two fundamental approaches in dealing with legislative texts generally, which are rarely absent from any civilized human environment: an approach that strictly adheres to the apparent meaning of the text, and an approach that gives greater weight to the general objectives upon which the text is based and the wisdom intended from it. Chapter Thirteen: The Fourth Fiqh Period: From the early second century to the mid-fourth century. 1/13 - In this vast span, fiqh expanded greatly and rose to its splendid revival after its previous formative period. In this vast period, numerous madhhabs and juristic interpretations emerged: among them the four madhhabs, and many others. (1) The four madhhabs are: the Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali. A - The Hanafi madhhab is attributed to its imam Abu Hanifa al-Nu'man ibn Thabit, who was of Persian origin and is called the Greatest Imam. He was born in the year 80 AH and died in the year 150 AH. He was the imam of the Iraqis. The leadership of Ahl al-Ra'y transferred to him and settled upon him, and he established the method of istihsan (juristic preference). He was renowned for strength of argument, speed of conclusive response, and genius of understanding and deduction. His most famous students are two: First: Abu Yusuf Ya'qub ibn Ibrahim, who assumed judicial authority in the Abbasid state during the reign of Harun al-Rashid, and authored for him the book 'al-Kharaj'. Second: Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, who was among the brilliant genius jurists and also among the masters of language. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad are called 'al-Sahibayn' (the two companions). To them primarily belongs the credit for codifying the fiqh of Abu Hanifa and spreading his madhhab. B - The Maliki madhhab is attributed to its imam Malik ibn Anas al-Asbahi, who was the scholar of Medina and the imam of the Hijazis. He was born in the year 93 AH and died in the year 179 AH. His madhhab is considered moderate between Ahl al-Ra'y and Ahl al-Hadith, due to its heavy reliance on hadith, as his narration spread, especially in the city of the Messenger (peace be upon him). Nevertheless, Malik is also counted among Ahl al-Ra'y, and his madhhab and principles are more closely related to ra'y.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview from al-Zarqa's work. It begins by noting that while the description of fiqh development applies generally to the four schools, the Hanafi school best fits the rationalist pattern. However, even that has nuances. The author then presents his interpretation: the methodological difference between Ahl al-Hadith (traditionists) and Ahl al-Ra'y (rationalists) is a universal dichotomy in legal systems. The fourth period (early 2nd to mid 4th century AH) saw the flourishing of independent madhhabs. The passage introduces the four schools and provides biographical details about Imam Abu Hanifa, his two main students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), and Imam Malik. The Hanafi school is characterized as the culmination of the rationalist approach, relying on istihsan, while the Maliki school is positioned as a middle ground. The author mentions the contributions of Abu Yusuf and al-Shaybani in codifying Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
أهل الرأي (Ahl al-Ra'y): The school of jurists who emphasize the use of personal reasoning (ra'y) and istihsan in deriving legal rulings, as opposed to strict adherence to hadith.
أهل الحديث (Ahl al-Hadith): The school of jurists who prioritize the apparent meaning of hadith and are more cautious in using personal opinion.
الصاحبين (al-Sahibayn): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who codified and spread his fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who are the two most famous students of Abu Hanifa mentioned in the passage?
A) Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani
B) Abu Hanifa and Malik
C) Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal
D) Malik and al-Shafi'i

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa and codified by al-Sahibayn, represents the rationalist approach (Ahl al-Ra'y) that balances textual sources with legal objectives through istihsan.

History and Development of Fiqh
beginner
8 min
Convergence of the Schools of Opinion and Hadith
Historical overview of school convergence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school (Madrasat al-Ra'y) and the Hadith school converged in the early Abbasid period, citing specific scholars and their interactions.

Core Idea
The schools of opinion and hadith converged as later scholars synthesized both methods.

This passage describes the initial tension between the Kufan school of opinion and the Medinan school of hadith, and how later generations bridged the gap. For a mufti, understanding this synthesis is crucial for appreciating the Hanafi reliance on both ra'y and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools
Distinguish Madrasat al-Ra'y (opinion school, Hanafi) from Madrasat al-Hadith (hadith school, Maliki/Hanbali). Note their regional and methodological differences.
The passage notes that the conflict intensified then settled.
2
Read the statement on opinion as valid method
The author says opinion became accepted as a valid juristic method with boundaries. This legitimizes the Hanafi use of ra'y.
3
Analyze the examples of convergence
Examine three examples: (1) Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani studied Malik's Muwatta; (2) al-Shafi'i learned from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan; (3) Abu Yusuf supported opinions with hadith. These show scholars crossing school lines.
4
Conclude the synthesis
The result is that later Hanafi books contain both ra'y and hadith, reflecting convergence.

Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics
And in the early part of this stage, the conflict between the school of opinion and the school of hadith intensified, then the consideration of opinion as a valid juristic method with its boundaries and legal principles settled – principles that its proponents clarified and with which they repelled doubts about this method, distancing it from the meaning of speaking according to whim and desire devoid of legal evidence.
=
Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra says:
But the difference did not last long between the people of opinion and hadith, for the generation...
And he benefited from his position in Medina through continuous contact with scholars from all regions during the Hajj season, and he met Abu Hanifa there and debated with him and said about him: 'He is a jurist' (see: book 'Malik' by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, p. 1). C - The Shafi'i school is attributed to its imam Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i al-Qurashi. He was born in 150 AH in Gaza and raised an orphan, and traveled to Iraq and Hijaz, taking from the companions of Abu Hanifa and from Malik. Then he settled in Egypt, abandoned his old opinions and founded his new school, and died in 204 AH in Fustat. He had abundant talents, and his school is considered closer to the people of hadith. He criticized his teacher Malik's method of istihsan.
.
D - The Hanbali school is attributed to its imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal al-Shaybani, a student of Imam al-Shafi'i. He was born in Baghdad in 164 AH and devoted himself to hadith, excelled in it and became famous, so his school was more colored by hadith. He died in 241 AH.
And besides these four, there were many great mujtahids, contemporaries and successors, who had significant fiqh and valuable opinions, some of which are still recorded in books that document the differences of jurists. But their schools did not achieve immortality for reasons including that they were not granted students with ability and activity to preserve and spread them. Among those mujtahids are the teachers of these four imams, i.e., their masters, such as Hammad ibn Abi Sulayman (teacher of Abu Hanifa), Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, al-Sha'bi (teachers of Hammad), Rabi'a al-Ra'y, Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri, Yahya ibn Sa'id (from the teachers of Malik). And among them also besides their teachers from their many contemporaries such as Ja'far al-Sadiq, Zayd ibn Ali Zayn al-Abidin, al-Awza'i, Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla, and Ibn Shubruma (contemporaries of Abu Hanifa), and al-Layth ibn Sa'd in Egypt (among the great mujtahids contemporaries of Abu Hanifa and Malik), and there were debates and legal correspondence between him and Malik that were preserved and published – see the two exchanged letters between Malik and al-Layth ibn Sa'd in the book (Malik) by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Zahra, Part One, p. 102, pp. 103-114).
Abu
200
Chapter (13) The Fourth Period: 101-350 AH
Those who came after the imams, the founders of the schools, and their students met, no matter how different their teachers were:
Imam Muhammad, from the companions of Abu Hanifa, travels to Hijaz and studies the book 'al-Muwatta' of Imam Malik.
Al-Shafi'i learns the fiqh of the people of opinion from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan(1).
And Abu Yusuf himself supports the opinions of many of the people of opinion with hadiths. Therefore, we find various fiqh books filled with both opinion and hadith, indicating their convergence, although jurists differed in how much they took from one or the other (see: introduction of 'al-Milkiyya wa Nazariyyat al-'Aqd' by the mentioned professor, p. 16, p. 37). And in this period, governments adopted a sectarian orientation in judiciary, hisba(2), taxation, and other affairs. For example, the fiqh of Abu Hanifa and his school was the prevailing school in the judiciary of the Abbasid state, and local customs and traditions became linked to fiqh, and rulings were built upon them.
It is
2/13 - And in the early part of this era, the codification of fiqh began in a scholarly, sectarian manner. Among the earliest books are those of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, student of Abu Hanifa, who compiled his school, and the book al-Muwatta by Malik ibn Anas, and the book al-Umm by al-Shafi'i. And in this period also began the codification of the science of usul al-fiqh, to regulate the principles of deriving rulings after the corruption of the Arabic instinct in tongues.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview from a Hanafi perspective, tracing the development of Islamic jurisprudence through its early stages. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins Chapter Three by noting that the initial intense conflict between the school of opinion (ra'y, primarily associated with the Hanafi school) and the school of hadith (associated with Malik and others) eventually settled, with opinion being accepted as a valid juristic method when bounded by legal principles. He quotes Muhammad Abu Zahra to show that the difference did not persist long, as later generations of scholars synthesized both approaches.

Al-Zarqa then briefly describes the four main Sunni schools: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali, noting key figures and characteristics. He emphasizes that many other mujtahids existed but their schools did not survive due to lack of capable students.

Moving to the fourth period (101-350 AH), the author illustrates how the earlier schools converged: Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (a Hanafi) studied Malik's Muwatta, al-Shafi'i learned from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (a Hanafi), and Abu Yusuf (a Hanafi) supported opinions with hadith. This led to books combining both opinion and hadith. Governments also started adopting specific madhhabs, with the Hanafi school becoming dominant in the Abbasid judiciary. Finally, the passage notes the beginning of systematic codification of fiqh by madhhab and the writing of usul al-fiqh to preserve the Arabic linguistic instinct and standardize derivation methods.

Key Terms
مدرسة الرأي (Madrasat al-Ra'y): The school of legal opinion, primarily associated with Abu Hanifa and his followers, who used reasoned opinion (ra'y) and qiyas within limits.
مدرسة الحديث (Madrasat al-Hadith): The school of hadith, associated with Malik and later Ahmad ibn Hanbal, prioritizing prophetic traditions over opinion.
الاستحسان (al-Istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of departing from a clear analogy in favor of a stronger evidence or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which scholar studied Malik's Muwatta, demonstrating the interaction between the schools of ra'y and hadith?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Al-Shafi'i

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school integrated both ra'y and hadith, as exemplified by its later scholars engaging with works of the hadith school.

History and Development of Fiqh
beginner
8 min
The Beginnings of Codification of Fiqh and Usul al-Fiqh
Historical overview of codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the significance of early codification by madhhab for the Hanafi school and identify the key works mentioned in the passage.

Core Idea
Codification by madhhab began with the works of Muhammad al-Shaybani and the writing of usul al-fiqh.

This passage marks the transition from oral transmission to written recording of fiqh by madhhab. For a mufti, knowing the foundational texts and the reason for codifying usul (the corruption of Arabic instinct) is essential to understand the authority of later compilations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time period
The passage places the beginning of codification in the early part of the Fourth Period (101-350 AH). Recognize this as after the main imams.
2
List the earliest books
The passage mentions three key works: the books of Muhammad al-Shaybani (the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah), Malik's al-Muwatta, and al-Shafi'i's al-Umm. Note that these are the foundational texts of the Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i schools respectively.
3
Understand the reason for codifying usul
The passage says usul al-fiqh was codified 'to regulate the principles of deriving rulings after the corruption of the Arabic instinct in tongues.' This indicates that as Arabic language deteriorated, a systematic methodology became necessary to preserve correct derivation.
4
Reflect on the impact for the Hanafi school
The Hanafi school was the first to systematically codify its fiqh through Muhammad al-Shaybani's works, which contributed to its preservation and spread, especially under Abbasid patronage.

And besides these four, there were many great mujtahids, contemporaries and successors, who had significant fiqh and valuable opinions, some of which are still recorded in books that document the differences of jurists. But their schools did not achieve immortality for reasons including that they were not granted students with ability and activity to preserve and spread them. Among those mujtahids are the teachers of these four imams, i.e., their masters, such as Hammad ibn Abi Sulayman (teacher of Abu Hanifa), Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, al-Sha'bi (teachers of Hammad), Rabi'a al-Ra'y, Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri, Yahya ibn Sa'id (from the teachers of Malik). And among them also besides their teachers from their many contemporaries such as Ja'far al-Sadiq, Zayd ibn Ali Zayn al-Abidin, al-Awza'i, Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla, and Ibn Shubruma (contemporaries of Abu Hanifa), and al-Layth ibn Sa'd in Egypt (among the great mujtahids contemporaries of Abu Hanifa and Malik), and there were debates and legal correspondence between him and Malik that were preserved and published – see the two exchanged letters between Malik and al-Layth ibn Sa'd in the book (Malik) by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Zahra, Part One, p. 102, pp. 103-114).

Chapter (13) The Fourth Period: 101-350 AH
Those who came after the imams, the founders of the schools, and their students met, no matter how different their teachers were:
Imam Muhammad, from the companions of Abu Hanifa, travels to Hijaz and studies the book 'al-Muwatta' of Imam Malik.
Al-Shafi'i learns the fiqh of the people of opinion from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan(1).
And Abu Yusuf himself supports the opinions of many of the people of opinion with hadiths. Therefore, we find various fiqh books filled with both opinion and hadith, indicating their convergence, although jurists differed in how much they took from one or the other (see: introduction of 'al-Milkiyya wa Nazariyyat al-'Aqd' by the mentioned professor, p. 16, p. 37). And in this period, governments adopted a sectarian orientation in judiciary, hisba(2), taxation, and other affairs. For example, the fiqh of Abu Hanifa and his school was the prevailing school in the judiciary of the Abbasid state, and local customs and traditions became linked to fiqh, and rulings were built upon them.
It is
2/13 - And in the early part of this era, the codification of fiqh began in a scholarly, sectarian manner. Among the earliest books are those of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, student of Abu Hanifa, who compiled his school, and the book al-Muwatta by Malik ibn Anas, and the book al-Umm by al-Shafi'i. And in this period also began the codification of the science of usul al-fiqh, to regulate the principles of deriving rulings after the corruption of the Arabic instinct in tongues.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview from a Hanafi perspective, tracing the development of Islamic jurisprudence through its early stages. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins Chapter Three by noting that the initial intense conflict between the school of opinion (ra'y, primarily associated with the Hanafi school) and the school of hadith (associated with Malik and others) eventually settled, with opinion being accepted as a valid juristic method when bounded by legal principles. He quotes Muhammad Abu Zahra to show that the difference did not persist long, as later generations of scholars synthesized both approaches.

Al-Zarqa then briefly describes the four main Sunni schools: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali, noting key figures and characteristics. He emphasizes that many other mujtahids existed but their schools did not survive due to lack of capable students.

Moving to the fourth period (101-350 AH), the author illustrates how the earlier schools converged: Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (a Hanafi) studied Malik's Muwatta, al-Shafi'i learned from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (a Hanafi), and Abu Yusuf (a Hanafi) supported opinions with hadith. This led to books combining both opinion and hadith. Governments also started adopting specific madhhabs, with the Hanafi school becoming dominant in the Abbasid judiciary. Finally, the passage notes the beginning of systematic codification of fiqh by madhhab and the writing of usul al-fiqh to preserve the Arabic linguistic instinct and standardize derivation methods.

Key Terms
مدرسة الرأي (Madrasat al-Ra'y): The school of legal opinion, primarily associated with Abu Hanifa and his followers, who used reasoned opinion (ra'y) and qiyas within limits.
مدرسة الحديث (Madrasat al-Hadith): The school of hadith, associated with Malik and later Ahmad ibn Hanbal, prioritizing prophetic traditions over opinion.
الاستحسان (al-Istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of departing from a clear analogy in favor of a stronger evidence or custom.
تدوين (tadwin): Codification; the act of writing down and systematically compiling legal rulings and principles.
أصول الفقه (usul al-fiqh): The principles of Islamic jurisprudence; the methodology for deriving legal rulings from primary sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The corruption of the Arabic instinct in tongues
C) The spread of incorrect opinions
D) The demand from the Abbasid government

Key Takeaway
Early codification by madhhab and the writing of usul al-fiqh preserved the Hanafi school's methodology for future generations.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Fourth Period (101-350 AH): Foundations of Hanafi Usul al-Ifta
Historical overview of the fourth period with emphasis on school interaction, codification, and legal maxims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key developments of the fourth period and explain their impact on Hanafi usul al-iftaa, particularly the formulation of universal legal maxims.

Core Idea
The fourth period saw the convergence of schools, codification of fiqh and usul, and the crystallisation of universal legal maxims that govern fatwa.

This lesson examines the fourth period of Islamic jurisprudence (101–350 AH), a transformative era when the major schools interacted, governments adopted specific madhhabs, and the science of usul al-fiqh was systematically compiled. This historical context is essential for understanding the legal maxims that form the backbone of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Guided Path
1
Contextualise the period
Locate the fourth period (101–350 AH) in the broader timeline of Islamic jurisprudence. Note that it follows the era of the great imams and their immediate students.
2
Observe cross-school interaction
Identify the examples given: Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani studies Malik's Muwatta, al-Shafi'i studies under Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, Abu Yusuf uses hadith to support opinions. Recognise that this exchange enriched all schools.
This interaction is crucial for understanding why Hanafi usul incorporated both ra'y and hadith.
3
Note governmental adoption
Recognise that the Abbasid state adopted the Hanafi school in its judiciary, hisba, and taxation. This institutionalisation affected the development and spread of Hanafi fiqh.
Local customs were also integrated, shaping rulings.
4
Identify the codification movement
Note that this period saw the systematic codification of fiqh by madhhab (e.g., works of al-Shaybani, Malik, al-Shafi'i) and the beginning of usul al-fiqh as a written discipline with al-Shafi'i's Risalah.
The passage emphasises that the principles of usul existed earlier; al-Shafi'i was the first to compile them.
5
Recognise the formulation of qawa'id kulliyya
The passage lists universal maxims like 'Certainty is not removed by doubt', 'Custom is authoritative', and 'Harm must be removed'. Understand that these maxims emerged from the interaction of textual evidence and juristic reasoning and became essential tools for fatwa.
These maxims are studied in detail in the third section of al-Madkhal.

Chapter (13) The Fourth Period: 101-350 AH. Those who came after the imams, the founders of schools and their students, met each other despite the differences of their teachers: Imam Muhammad, one of Abu Hanifa's companions, travels to Hijaz and studies the book 'al-Muwatta' of Imam Malik. Al-Shafi'i receives the fiqh of the people of opinion from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan. Abu Yusuf himself supports the opinions of many of the people of opinion with hadiths. Hence we find various fiqh books filled with both opinion and hadith, indicating their convergence, although jurists differ in how much they adopt one over the other. Governments in this period adopted a madhhab orientation in judiciary, hisbah, taxation and other works. For example, the fiqh of Abu Hanifa and his school was the prevalent madhhab in the judiciary of the Abbasid state, and local customs and traditions became linked to fiqh, and rulings were built upon them. 2/13 - In the early part of this era, the systematic codification of fiqh by madhhab began. Among its earliest books are those of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, student of Abu Hanifa, who compiled his madhhab, and the book al-Muwatta of Malik ibn Anas, and the book al-Umm of al-Shafi'i. Also in this period began the codification of the science of Usul al-Fiqh, regulating the principles of deriving rulings after the corruption of Arabic linguistic instinct in tongues. ... The third section: Stages of Development of Islamic Fiqh and its Characteristics. This noble science before that consisted of principles and rules not compiled, known to those firmly rooted in the science of Sharia, and their understandings in fiqh emanated from them. The first book written in Usul al-Fiqh was 'Risalah al-Usul' by Imam al-Shafi'i. He was not the originator of this science and its rules, for every mujtahid proceeds in his ijtihad upon principles he observes and argues with. Rather, al-Shafi'i was the first to compile and record the basic foundational issues in Usul al-Fiqh. In this period, many juristic terminologies appeared, which became a lasting treasure in the language of fiqh and law. These terminologies differed according to schools and their locations. In this stage, the theoretical nature intensified in fiqh, and there appeared the method of hypothesizing events before they occur and determining their rulings in advance, which had a great effect in expanding and broadening fiqh and sharpening ideas. However, later the depth in that had disadvantages: fiqh in many of its rulings became distant from practical need and temporal interests, and there appeared a set of issues whose occurrence was almost impossible and studying them a waste of time. Within the limits of this broad period, many universal principles and fiqh controls were formulated, derived from textual evidences, such as the principle 'Certainty is not removed by doubt', the principle 'The default is the innocence of the liability', the principle 'Custom is authoritative', the principle 'Harm must be removed', the principle 'Hardship brings ease', and many other principles that were present in the origins of the schools, then their meanings took fixed verbal formulations of wide comprehensiveness. Detail will come in the third section of this introduction, which is the section dedicated to research on universal principles and their explanation.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the fourth period of Islamic jurisprudence (101-350 AH) from 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm' by Mustafa al-Zarqa. The author describes the interaction between different schools: for example, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (a leading Hanafi) studied Malik's Muwatta, and al-Shafi'i studied under Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, showing cross-pollination. Abu Yusuf, another Hanafi authority, supported opinion-based rulings with hadith, blending the two approaches. This period saw the official adoption of madhhab-based judiciary by governments; for instance, Hanafi fiqh became dominant in Abbasid courts. Local customs were incorporated into fiqh rulings. The passage highlights the beginning of systematic codification of fiqh by madhhab, with early works by al-Shaybani, Malik, and al-Shafi'i. It also marks the start of codification of Usul al-Fiqh as a discipline; al-Shafi'i's Risalah is the first known work, though the principles existed before. The author notes the proliferation of juristic terminology, varying by school. The period's theoretical nature led to hypothetical fiqh (ifta' al-hawadith qabla wuqu'iha), which expanded fiqh but later resulted in impractical issues. Importantly, many universal legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyya) were formulated, such as 'Certainty is not removed by doubt', 'Default is innocence', 'Custom is authoritative', 'Harm must be removed', and 'Hardship brings ease'. These maxims became fixed phrases with wide application. This historical context is essential for understanding the development of Hanafi usul al-ifta and the reliance on these maxims in fatwa.

Key Terms
أصول الفقه (usul al-fiqh): The science of the principles of deriving Islamic legal rulings from their sources.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that apply across multiple chapters of fiqh.
الحسبة (al-hisba): An executive function in Islam to enforce the duty of enjoining good and forbidding evil, protecting public interests (today called 'public order').

Key Takeaway
The fourth period (101-350 AH) was crucial for the development of Hanafi fiqh and usul, as it saw the codification of legal principles and the emergence of universal maxims that guide fatwa.

The Codification of Usul al-Fiqh: Al-Shafi'i's Role
Historical account of early usul development

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the role of Imam al-Shafi'i in compiling usul al-fiqh and contrast it with the pre-existing state of the discipline.

Core Idea
Imam al-Shafi'i compiled usul al-fiqh but did not invent it – mujtahids always used principles.

This lesson explores the early history of usul al-fiqh. Before al-Shafi'i, the principles of ijtihad were known to deeply learned scholars but were not formally compiled. The passage clarifies that al-Shafi'i was the first to record these principles, not their originator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the state of usul before al-Shafi'i
Read the first sentence: principles existed but were 'not compiled' and known only to the 'rasikhun' (deeply learned).
This shows that fiqh was practiced with methodology, just not written.
2
Determine al-Shafi'i's exact contribution
The text says he wrote 'Risalah al-Usul' and was 'the first to compile and record the basic foundational issues'. Note: not the originator of the principles themselves.
Contrast with the misconception that he invented usul.
3
Recognize the emergence of terminologies
During this period, many fiqh terms developed, varying by madhhab and region.
This reflects the growth of a specialized legal language.
4
Understand the theoretical turn
Fiqh became more theoretical, with scholars hypothesizing events before they occurred, which expanded the corpus.
This had positive and negative effects later.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته

(ر: ف ١/٨٠ - ٥).
(۱) محكمة : بفتح الكاف المشددة، صيغة مفعول من التحكيم (ر: ف ٥/٦).
Third Chapter: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. Before that, this noble science existed as principles and rules not compiled, known to those deeply rooted in the science of Sharia, and their understandings in fiqh were derived from them. The first book written in the science of Usul al-Fiqh was 'Risalah al-Usul' by Imam al-Shafi'i. He was not the originator of this science and its rules, for every mujtahid proceeds in his ijtihad on principles he upholds and argues with. Rather, al-Shafi'i was the first to compile and record the basic foundational issues in Usul al-Fiqh. In this period, many fiqh terminologies emerged, which became an enduring wealth in the language of fiqh and law. These terminologies differed according to different madhhabs and their locations. In this stage, the theoretical character of fiqh intensified, and the method of hypothesizing events before they occur and determining their rulings in advance emerged, which had a great impact on the expansion and growth of fiqh and the sharpening of ideas. However, later, the negative aspect of this over-abstraction was that fiqh in many of its rulings became distant from practical needs and temporal interests, and a set of issues appeared whose occurrence was almost impossible, and studying them was a waste of time. Within the broad limits of this period, many universal rules and fiqh principles were established, derived from the evidences of texts, such as the rule 'Certainty is not removed by doubt', the rule 'The default is freedom from liability', the rule 'Custom is authoritative', the rule 'Harm must be removed', the rule 'Hardship brings ease', and many other rules that were circulating in the principles of the madhhabs, so their meanings took fixed verbal forms with broad implications. Details of this will come in the third section of this introduction, which is the section dedicated to the study of universal rules and their explanation. (See: vol. 1, pp. 80-5). (1) 'Authoritative': with a shaddah on the kaaf, a passive form from 'tahkim' (arbitration).
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the development of Islamic jurisprudence and its characteristics. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, describes the state of usul al-fiqh before it was formally compiled: it existed as unwritten principles known to the deeply learned scholars, which guided their ijtihad. Imam al-Shafi'i is credited with writing the first work on usul al-fiqh, but he is not the originator of the discipline; rather, he was the first to compile and record the basic issues. During this period, many technical fiqh terms emerged, which varied across different madhhabs. The theoretical nature of fiqh intensified, with scholars beginning to hypothesize events and determine their rulings in advance, greatly expanding the body of fiqh. However, this also led to impractical hypothetical cases. Importantly, the major legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyyah) were codified during this period, derived directly from textual evidence. The author lists several foundational maxims, such as 'Certainty is not removed by doubt', 'The default is freedom from liability', 'Custom is authoritative', 'Harm must be removed', and 'Hardship brings ease'. These maxims became fixed principles applied across fiqh. The passage concludes by referring to the detailed discussion in a later section of the book. The Emergence of the Major Legal Maxims (Qawa'id Kulliyyah)
Codification of universal legal maxims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major legal maxims listed in the passage and explain their derivation from textual evidences.

Core Idea
Major legal maxims like 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' were codified from textual proofs during the classical period.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage, which describes the codification of universal legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyyah). The author lists several foundational maxims that became fixed principles in the madhhabs. Understanding these maxims is essential for applying fiqh across different chapters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxims listed
Read the list: 'the rule 'Certainty is not removed by doubt', 'The default is freedom from liability', 'Custom is authoritative', 'Harm must be removed', 'Hardship brings ease'.
These are among the five major maxims (al-qawa'id al-kubra) in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand their derivation
The text says they are 'derived from the evidences of texts' – meaning from Quran, Sunnah, or general principles.
For example, 'Harm must be removed' is based on the hadith 'la darar wa la dirar'.
3
Recognize their fixed verbal forms
The maxims took 'fixed verbal forms with broad implications', meaning they are standardized across schools.
These forms are used as standard premises in ijtihad.
4
Place them in historical context
This codification occurred during the period of theoretical expansion, when fiqh was being systematized.
The passage situates the maxims as part of the overall development of fiqh methodology.

Third Chapter: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. Before that, this noble science existed as principles and rules not compiled, known to those deeply rooted in the science of Sharia, and their understandings in fiqh were derived from them. The first book written in the science of Usul al-Fiqh was 'Risalah al-Usul' by Imam al-Shafi'i. He was not the originator of this science and its rules, for every mujtahid proceeds in his ijtihad on principles he upholds and argues with. Rather, al-Shafi'i was the first to compile and record the basic foundational issues in Usul al-Fiqh. In this period, many fiqh terminologies emerged, which became an enduring wealth in the language of fiqh and law. These terminologies differed according to different madhhabs and their locations. In this stage, the theoretical character of fiqh intensified, and the method of hypothesizing events before they occur and determining their rulings in advance emerged, which had a great impact on the expansion and growth of fiqh and the sharpening of ideas. However, later, the negative aspect of this over-abstraction was that fiqh in many of its rulings became distant from practical needs and temporal interests, and a set of issues appeared whose occurrence was almost impossible, and studying them was a waste of time. Within the broad limits of this period, many universal rules and fiqh principles were established, derived from the evidences of texts, such as the rule 'Certainty is not removed by doubt', the rule 'The default is freedom from liability', the rule 'Custom is authoritative', the rule 'Harm must be removed', the rule 'Hardship brings ease', and many other rules that were circulating in the principles of the madhhabs, so their meanings took fixed verbal forms with broad implications. Details of this will come in the third section of this introduction, which is the section dedicated to the study of universal rules and their explanation. (See: vol. 1, pp. 80-5). (1) 'Authoritative': with a shaddah on the kaaf, a passive form from 'tahkim' (arbitration).
