Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the development of Islamic jurisprudence and its characteristics. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, describes the state of usul al-fiqh before it was formally compiled: it existed as unwritten principles known to the deeply learned scholars, which guided their ijtihad. Imam al-Shafi'i is credited with writing the first work on usul al-fiqh, but he is not the originator of the discipline; rather, he was the first to compile and record the basic issues. During this period, many technical fiqh terms emerged, which varied across different madhhabs. The theoretical nature of fiqh intensified, with scholars beginning to hypothesize events and determine their rulings in advance, greatly expanding the body of fiqh. However, this also led to impractical hypothetical cases. Importantly, the major legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyyah) were codified during this period, derived directly from textual evidence. The author lists several foundational maxims, such as 'Certainty is not removed by doubt', 'The default is freedom from liability', 'Custom is authoritative', 'Harm must be removed', and 'Hardship brings ease'. These maxims became fixed principles applied across fiqh. The passage concludes by referring to the detailed discussion in a later section of the book. For students, it is crucial to understand that these maxims are not arbitrary but are extracted from the primary sources. The footnote clarifies the pronunciation of 'muhakkamah'.

Key Terms
قواعد كلية (qawa'id kulliyyah): Universal legal maxims derived from textual evidences that apply across multiple chapters of fiqh.
أصول الفقه (usul al-fiqh): The discipline of the principles of jurisprudence, dealing with the sources and methodology of deriving rulings.
اليقين لا يزول بالشك (al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk): Certainty is not removed by doubt: a maxim that preserves the status quo ante when doubt arises.

The Fifth Juristic Period: Stagnation and Closing of Ijtihad
Description of the era when ijtihad declined and madhhab adherence intensified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the door of ijtihad was closed and identify the role of takhrij and tarjih in later Hanafi fatwa practice.

Core Idea
The closure of ijtihad was a protective measure, not a prohibition, leading to high specialization in takhrij and tarjih within the madhhabs.

This lesson explores the transition from independent ijtihad to madhhab-based legal reasoning during the fifth juristic period. You will understand why scholars issued fatwas to close the door of ijtihad and how this shaped the work of later Hanafi jurists, who focused on deriving rulings from established principles (takhrij) and preferring stronger opinions (tarjih) within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical period
Note the timeframe (mid-4th to mid-7th century AH) and the key characteristic: stagnation of independent ijtihad and rise of strict madhhab adherence.
2
Understand the rationale for closing ijtihad
The scholars feared that unqualified individuals would corrupt the established fiqh. The closing was a practical measure to protect the religion, not a permanent ban.
3
Recognize the shift to takhrij and tarjih
With ijtihad closed, jurists turned to takhrij (deriving rulings from madhhab principles) and tarjih (prefering one opinion over another within the madhhab). These became the main activities.
4
Reflect on the impact for Hanafi fatwa practice
Later Hanafi muftis rely on the mufta bihi (relied-upon) positions determined by tarjih, rather than deriving directly from primary sources. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for issuing correct fatwas today.

Chapter Fourteen: The Fifth Juristic Period: From the mid-4th century to the mid-7th century. 1/14 - In this period, the movement of ijtihad stagnated, and those interested in fiqh turned to adhering to the madhhabs of those earlier mujtahids, especially the four. Each of these four madhhabs became centered in one or more regions. In each madhhab, great jurists emerged who engaged in codification, refinement, and organization of the madhhab. 2/14 - Closing of the door of ijtihad and the activity of al-takhrij and al-tarjih within the madhhabs: In the first half of this period, the scholars of the four madhhabs gave fatwas to close the door of ijtihad, due to the decline of ambitions to acquire the set of qualities and linguistic and religious sciences to the extent that qualifies one for ijtihad in the rulings of Sharia. The public's ability to distinguish between those worthy of being followed and those not worthy weakened, and the scholars feared that the ignorant and reckless floundering in whims would destroy what those great imams had built of the fiqh of Sharia as a correct scientific construction. The venerable professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra (1) sees that among the actual factors that directed the thoughts of the men of the madhhabs to issue fatwas closing the door of ijtihad are the following reasons: (1) (See: 'Property and Contract Theory in Islamic Law', pp. 38-39).
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage describes the fifth juristic period (c. 350-650 AH), characterized by a stagnation of independent ijtihad and a shift towards strict adherence to the established four madhhabs. Jurists in this era focused on compiling, refining, and organizing the rulings of their respective madhhabs. Importantly, the scholars of the four madhhabs issued fatwas to close the door of ijtihad. The reason was not that ijtihad became forbidden in principle, but that the qualifications required for ijtihad had declined; people no longer possessed the necessary linguistic and religious knowledge. There was a fear that if unqualified individuals attempted ijtihad, they would cause corruption and undermine the solid legal structure built by the earlier imams. Consequently, the activities of 'takhrij' (deriving rulings from established principles) and 'tarjih' (preferring one opinion over another within the madhhab) became the main form of juristic work. The author references Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, who provides additional sociological reasons for this development, though the list is not fully provided here. For students of Hanafi fiqh, this period is crucial: it explains why later Hanafi fatwa manuals often rely on the preferred views within the school rather than direct ijtihad from sources.

Key Terms
التخريج (al-takhrij): Deriving new rulings from established principles of the madhhab, not directly from primary sources.
الترجيح (al-tarjih): Prefering one opinion or transmission over another within a madhhab based on strength of evidence or authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the scholars of the four madhhabs issue fatwas to close the door of ijtihad?
A) They believed ijtihad was permanently forbidden.
B) They feared that unqualified people would corrupt the religion.
C) They wanted to eliminate all differences of opinion.
D) They considered the primary sources to be insufficient.

Key Takeaway
The closure of ijtihad in the fifth period shifted juristic work to takhrij and tarjih within the madhhab, establishing a hierarchy of rulings that muftis must follow.

Closure of Ijtihad: Historical Analysis and Causes
Historical analysis of the closure of ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical context of the closure of ijtihad in the fifth Islamic period and identify the two main causes cited by al-Zarqa and Abu Zahra.

Core Idea
The closure of ijtihad was not a theological prohibition but a practical response to declining qualifications and institutional pressures.

In this passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am, we explore the fifth period of Islamic jurisprudence (4th-7th centuries AH). The author describes how the vibrant ijtihad of earlier centuries gradually gave way to taqlid and school consolidation. A key event was the fatwa issued by scholars of the four madhhabs declaring the door of ijtihad closed. Al-Zarqa, drawing on Muhammad Abu Zahra, explains that this closure was driven by two factors: madhhab fanaticism and the nature of judicial appointments. Understanding this historical shift is crucial for comprehending the subsequent development of Hanafi legal theory and practice.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the period
Identify the time frame (mid-4th to mid-7th centuries AH) and main characteristics: stagnation of ijtihad, consolidation around the four madhhabs, and emergence of great jurists who codified and refined each school.
This period marks a shift from independent ijtihad to adherence to established madhhabs.
2
Define the closure event
What was the fatwa that declared ijtihad closed? Scholars of the four schools issued it due to the decline in qualifications and fear of deviation. State the reasons given for the closure.
The fatwa was not a single event but a general sentiment among later scholars.
3
Identify the causes
List the two main causes presented by Abu Zahra as quoted by al-Zarqa: 1) Madhhab fanaticism, 2) Judicial authority. Provide a brief description of each.
These causes are from the source's citation of Shaykh Muhammad Abu Zahra.
4
Analyze each cause
Explain how madhhab fanaticism led to stagnation (students focused on transmission rather than methodology) and how judicial appointments restricted ijtihad (caliphs chose muqallid judges bound to a single school).
Consider the interplay between scholarly tendencies and state policy.
5
Evaluate the footnote
Al-Zarqa adds a footnote on codification: it is beneficial to codify law but selection should be from multiple madhhabs based on evidence and public interest, not a single school. This suggests an ongoing need for juristic assessment.
This footnote provides a normative perspective on how fiqh should be applied in official courts.

Chapter Fourteen: The Fifth Fiqh Period: From the Middle of the Fourth Century to the Middle of the Seventh Century. 1/14 - In this period, the movement of ijtihad stagnated, and those interested in fiqh inclined to adhere to the schools of those earlier mujtahids, especially the four. Each of these four schools became centered in one or more regions. Great jurists emerged in each school who engaged in codification, refinement, and organization of the school. 2/14 - Closure of the door of ijtihad and the activity of takhrij and tarjih within madhhabs: In the first half of this period, the scholars of the four schools issued fatwas closing the door of ijtihad, due to the decline of aspirations to acquire the set of linguistic and religious qualifications to the degree that qualifies for ijtihad in Sharia rulings. The public's ability to distinguish between those worthy of imitation and others weakened, and scholars feared that ignorant and reckless deviation in scientific whims would destroy what those great imams had built of fiqh as a sound scientific structure. The venerable professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra (1) sees that among the actual factors that directed the minds of madhhab scholars to issue fatwas closing the door of ijtihad are the following reasons: (1) (Ref: Ownership and Contract Theory in Islamic Law, pp. 38-39). (Page 203) Book Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. 1 - Madhhab Fanaticism: Students became fanatically attached to the works of their teachers, the mujtahid imams who illuminated the previous era and uncovered the darkness of issues with the bright light of their intellects. It is no secret that fanaticism for an idea drives a person to stagnate on it, cling to its fringes, and call people to it exclusively. That is what those who came after the earlier imams did: they focused on studying their schools and spreading them instead of following their methodology and exercising ijtihad as their founders did, so people trusted the predecessors and doubted themselves. 2 - Judicial Authority: Initially, caliphs appointed judges from among the mujtahids, not their followers, but later they preferred to appoint from among the muqallids to restrict them to a particular school, and to specify for them the basis on which they would judge, such that they were isolated from any judgment that opposed that school, and because some mujtahid judges were subjected to criticism by madhhab jurists, making their rulings a source of public criticism rather than reassurance. (1) There is no doubt that it is in the public interest to restrict the judiciary to specific codified rulings so that people know in advance the rules to which their transactions are subject, enabling them to be aware of their validity or invalidity. But that does not require that the codified rulings for the judiciary be selected from a single school; rather, what is sounder in evidence, wiser in wisdom, and more congruent with public interest should be chosen from each school, forming a comprehensive fiqh collection as a law to which the judiciary adheres. Then the selection from the schools should be renewed by substitution and amendment whenever need arises and the public interest requires according to changing times. This is what Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' suggested to the Abbasid Caliph Abu Ja'far al-Mansur: to unify what is adjudicated among people in a law that he would write and select from the opinions of mujtahid judges and jurists of that era after scrutiny and weighing. We will elaborate on this topic in Appendix 2 to Chapter 22 under the title 'Codification of Fiqh.'
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative from Mustafa al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am, describing the fifth period of Islamic jurisprudence (roughly 4th-7th centuries AH). The author explains that this period witnessed a stagnation of independent ijtihad and a consolidation around the four established madhhabs. Scholars of the time issued fatwas declaring the closure of the door of ijtihad, citing the lack of qualified persons and the fear of chaotic deviation. Al-Zarqa then presents, drawing on Muhammad Abu Zahra, two main causes: first, fanatical attachment to the schools, which led followers to merely transmit and defend rather than engage in ijtihad; second, the judicial system, where caliphs began appointing muqallid judges restricted to a single madhhab, to ensure consistency and avoid criticism. The footnote emphasizes that codification of law is beneficial but should not be limited to one madhhab; rather, it should draw from all schools based on strength of evidence and public interest, a point earlier suggested by Ibn al-Muqaffa'. This passage is foundational for understanding the shift from ijtihad to taqlid in Islamic legal history.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Independent reasoning to derive legal rulings from primary sources; in this passage, its practice declined and its door was declared closed due to lack of qualified scholars.
التعصب المذهبي (al-ta'assub al-madhhabi): Fanatical adherence to a particular madhhab, leading to stagnation and rejection of other views; here identified as a cause for the closure of ijtihad.
التقليد (al-taqlid): Following a mujtahid without knowing his evidence; in the judicial context, judges were appointed as muqallids restricted to a specific madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what were two main causes for the closure of the door of ijtihad?
A) Lack of texts and political instability
B) Madhhab fanaticism and the nature of judicial appointments
C) The death of the four imams and the decline of Arabic
D) Opposition from caliphs and the rise of non-Islamic laws

Key Takeaway
The closure of ijtihad was a historical phenomenon driven by educational decline, madhhab fanaticism, and state control of the judiciary, not an absolute prohibition.

Three Historical Causes of Widespread Taqlid in Islamic Jurisprudence
Historical analysis of three causes of taqlid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three historical factors that led to the widespread practice of taqlid, and distinguish between legitimate taqlid and blind fanaticism.

Core Idea
Taqlid spread due to fanaticism, judicial policy, and codification – not from a ban on ijtihad.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa analyzes the historical forces that shaped the madhhab system. He identifies three key factors that moved the Muslim community from the dynamic ijtihad of the early Imams to the widespread taqlid of later centuries. Understanding these causes helps us appreciate the structure of Hanafi authority and the role of the mujtahid fi al-madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three causes
Read the passage and extract the three headings the author uses: التعصب المذهبي, ولاية القضاء, تدوين المذاهب. Write them down in order.
These are listed with numbers in the Arabic text.
2
Explain each cause in your own words
For each cause, summarize how the author says it contributed to the spread of taqlid. For example, for التعصب المذهبي, explain how students' fanaticism for their teachers' works led to stagnation.
Use the commentary as a guide.
3
Analyze the relationship between the causes
Consider how the judiciary cause and the codification cause reinforced each other. Why did the caliphs' policy of appointing muqallid judges make codification more influential?
Think about cause and effect.
4
Define the role of the mujtahid fi al-madhhab
The author describes a 'مُجْتَهِدٌ فِي الْمَذْهَب' as the only remaining intellectual activity. Use the legal_definition_dissector block to analyze its components.
This term appears at the end of the passage.
5
Apply to Hanafi tradition
Consider how the Hanafi school specifically exemplifies these causes. For instance, the codification was done by Imam Muhammad, and the judiciary in many Islamic states adopted the Hanafi madhhab.
Use your knowledge of Hanafi history.

۱ - التعصب المذهبي :
فقد تعصب التلاميذ لآثار أساتذتهم من الأئمة المجتهدين الذين أناروا العصر السابق، وكشفوا ظلمات المسائل بنور عقولهم الساطع.

فإنَّ تدوينها قد سَهَّل على الناس تناولها والناس دائماً يطلبون السهل اليسير دون الصعب العسير.

Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics
1 - Sectarian Fanaticism:
The students became fanatic for the works of their teachers from the mujtahid imams who illuminated the previous era and uncovered the darkness of issues with the light of their brilliant intellects. It is not hidden that fanaticism for an idea causes a person to stagnate upon it, cling to its fringes, and call people to it exclusively. Thus did those who came after the previous imams: they focused on studying their madhhabs and spreading them instead of following their methodology and exercising ijtihad as their founders did, so people trusted the predecessors and doubted themselves.
2 - The Judiciary:
The caliphs initially chose judges from the mujtahids, not their imitators, but later they preferred to choose them from the imitators to restrict them to a specific madhhab, and to specify for them what they should judge based on, such that they would be removed from any judgment that opposed that madhhab. And because some mujtahid judges would be criticized by sectarian jurists, their rulings became a source of people's criticism, not a cause for their reassurance.
(There is no doubt that it is in the interest to restrict the judiciary to specific codified rulings so that people know in advance what rules their transactions are subject to, so they are aware of their validity or invalidity. But that does not require that the codified rulings for the judiciary be chosen from a single madhhab; rather, from each madhhab that which is stronger in evidence, more eloquent in wisdom, and more in line with public interest should be chosen, so it becomes a jurisprudential code like a law that the judiciary follows, then the selection from the madhhabs is renewed by replacement and amendment whenever need arises and interest requires according to the change of time. This is what Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' suggested to the Abbasid caliph Abu Ja'far al-Mansur: to unify what is judged among people in a law that he codifies and chooses from the opinions of mujtahid judges and jurists of that era after examining them and weighing between them.
We will detail this topic in the second appendix of Chapter 22 under the title 'Codification of Fiqh...'
Chapter (14) The Fifth Era: 351-650 AH
Thus, the restriction of the judge to a madhhab approved by the caliph was a cause for most people to be content with it and turn towards it.
- Codification of Madhhabs:
Indeed, their codification made it easy for people to access them, and people always seek the easy and simple over the difficult and hard.
In previous eras, a compelling necessity drove people to ijtihad to learn the rulings of new incidents and matters whose legal ruling they did not know. When the mujtahids of previous eras came and codified the rulings of incidents that occurred and those likely to occur, whenever a question arose for people, they found that the predecessors had addressed it, so they were satisfied with their statement concerning it, and their need was fulfilled by what they found, so there was no incentive to research anew.
This was aided by the great scientific status of the predecessors, worthy of appreciation, and the veneration that their superiority over time earned them, and the care of nations to honor their righteous predecessors to link their present to their past with a strong bond. For all this, people turned to taqlid, except in knowing the reasons for madhhab rulings or giving preference to some opinions within the madhhab itself over others.
He who is given the scientific ability for that is called a 'mujtahid within the madhhab,' meaning he is not an absolute mujtahid with an independent madhhab, but rather a follower of a mujtahid imam, yet he has a considered opinion within his imam's madhhab and in building upon its principles. It is worth noting that most of the imams are reported to have forbidden taqlid of themselves without conviction in their evidence. (Most people and their common folk must follow a reliable imam.)
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a historical analysis of the factors that led to the widespread adoption of taqlid (imitation) in Islamic jurisprudence after the era of the great mujtahid Imams. The author identifies three main causes.

First: Sectarian Fanaticism (التعصب المذهبي). The students of the Imams became fanatically devoted to their teachers' works, focusing on studying and spreading the specific madhhab rather than continuing the methodology of independent reasoning (ijtihad). This led people to trust the earlier scholars and doubt their own ability to derive rulings.

Second: The Judiciary (ولاية القضاء). Initially, caliphs appointed mujtahid judges, but later they preferred to appoint muqallids (imitators) bound to a specific madhhab. This was partly because mujtahid judges were often criticized by sectarian jurists, causing public uncertainty. The author notes in a footnote that it is beneficial to codify laws for the judiciary, but that codification should draw from multiple madhhabs based on strongest evidence and public interest, as suggested by Ibn al-Muqaffa' to Caliph al-Mansur. However, in practice, binding judges to one madhhab led people to adopt that madhhab.

Third: Codification of Madhhabs (تدوين المذاهب). Writing down the rulings of each madhhab made them easily accessible. People naturally prefer ease, and since the earlier mujtahids had already addressed most issues, there was little incentive for new ijtihad. The prestige of the early scholars further reinforced reliance on their work.

The author then explains that the only remaining intellectual activity was 'mujtahid within the madhhab' (مجتهد في المذهب)—one who can understand the reasons behind rulings and weigh opinions within a single madhhab, but who does not create an independent school. Finally, the author notes that most Imams forbade taqlid of themselves without conviction in their evidence, implying that those who are qualified should examine the proofs, while the common people must follow a reliable imam.

Key Terms
التعصب المذهبي (al-ta'assub al-madhhabi): Sectarian fanaticism that led students to blindly adhere to their imam's school instead of continuing ijtihad.
ولاية القضاء (wilayat al-qada'): The judiciary; here referring to the caliphs' practice of appointing judges bound to a specific madhhab.
تدوين المذاهب (tadwin al-madhahib): Codification of the legal opinions of each madhhab into written works, which made taqlid easier.
مجتهد في المذهب (mujtahid fi al-madhhab): A jurist within a madhhab who can derive rulings based on the principles of his imam but does not create an independent school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Judges became more independent and innovative in their rulings.
B) The common people began to doubt the madhhab system and turned to other sources.
C) Most people became content with that madhhab and turned towards it.
D) Judges were allowed to freely choose from any madhhab.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid in Islamic jurisprudence was driven not by a ban on ijtihad but by historical factors: fanaticism, judicial policy, and codification, while the concept of mujtahid fi al-madhhab preserved a limited form of ijtihad within each school.

Collective Ijtihad: A New Method for Modern Fiqh Challenges
Proposes collective ijtihad by scholarly councils as the needed method for contemporary fiqh issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for collective ijtihad and identify its basis in the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

Core Idea
Collective ijtihad through consultative councils is essential to address unprecedented modern challenges while preserving the inexhaustible wellspring of Islamic jurisprudence.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa argues that the closing of individual ijtihad doors was justified in the past, but today's revolutionary scientific and social changes require a new approach: collective ijtihad. He calls for fiqh councils comprising scholars from diverse schools to meet contemporary needs.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the historical context
Identify why the door of individual ijtihad was closed: accumulated ijtihadi rulings formed a sufficient fiqh wealth in the past, making further individual ijtihad unnecessary or even harmful.
This is a historical claim; later scholars debated its accuracy.
2
Identify modern challenges
List the unprecedented developments mentioned: flight, submarine travel, nuclear energy, global communication, space exploration, and economic interdependence.
These are examples; the overarching point is the complexity and novelty of contemporary issues.
3
Understand the proposed solution
Al-Zarqa proposes 'ijtihad al-jama'ah'—collective ijtihad through 'majami' fiqhiyyah' (fiqh councils) composed of leading scholars from various schools and regions, using scientific consultation (shura).
This is a shift from individual to institutional ijtihad.
4
Connect to prophetic practice
The method mirrors the shura of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, who consulted scholars in complex matters. This provides legitimacy and historical precedent.
Al-Zarqa is not creating something new but reviving a neglected method.
5
Evaluate the necessity
Assess why collective ijtihad is essential: individual scholars cannot master all specializations needed for modern issues; collective expertise ensures comprehensive and balanced rulings.
This is a maqasid-based argument: the interest (maslahah) of the Ummah requires collaboration.

٣/١٤ - اجتهاد الجماعة :
هذا، وإذا كانت صيانة الأحكام الاجتهادية التي وجد منها ثروة فقهية كافية في الماضي قد اقتضت سد باب الاجتهاد الفردي، ففي هذا الزمن تغير وجه المصلحة بما جدَّ من عجائب الحضارة فقد سخر الإنسان فيه ما من قوى الطبيعة الكونية حتى طار في الهواء وغاص مسافراً ومحارباً تحت الماء، ثم حطم الآن الذرة واستخدم من قوتها ما حير العقول. وأصبح بفلكة زر من جهاز كصندوق صغير يرى فيه ويسمع من بلاد نائية وغزا الفَضَاء فأطلق الأقمار الاصطناعية تدور حول الأرض. ثم حقق حلمه بالرحلة إلى القمر، وأصبحت المواصلات بين مشارق الأرض ومغاربها تتم في سرعة المح البصر بقوة الكهرباء، وترابطت علائق الأمم الاقتصادية بعضها ببعض، وتلاقت قوانينها وجهاً لوجه. لذلك أصبح من مصلحة الفقه الإسلامي نفسه أن يقوم فيه اجتهاد من نوع آخر، هو اجتهاد الجماعة على طريقة الشورى العلمية في مجامع فقهية تضم فحول العلماء من مختلف المذاهب والأقطار، ليفوا حاجة العصر من هذا الفقه الإسلامي الفياض الذي لا ينْضَب معينه .
وهذه الشورى هي الطريقة التي كان يلجأ إليها الخلفاء الراشدون في المشكلات العلمية والسياسية كلما حزبهم أمر .
3/14 - Collective Ijtihad: This being so, if safeguarding the ijtihadi rulings—of which a sufficient fiqh wealth was found in the past—required closing the door of individual ijtihad, then in this time the aspect of benefit has changed due to the wonders of civilization that have emerged. Man has harnessed cosmic natural forces, flying in the air and diving as traveler and warrior underwater, then split the atom and used its power to bewilder minds. With the push of a button on a device like a small box, he sees and hears from distant lands. He conquered space, launching artificial satellites orbiting Earth. Then he achieved his dream of traveling to the moon. Communications between East and West now occur at the speed of lightning with electricity. Economic relations of nations are intertwined, and their laws face each other. Therefore, it has become in the interest of Islamic jurisprudence itself that a new type of ijtihad emerge: collective ijtihad on the method of scientific consultation in fiqh councils that include leading scholars from various schools and regions, to meet the needs of the age from this overflowing Islamic jurisprudence whose source never runs dry. This consultation was the method the Rightly Guided Caliphs resorted to in scientific and political problems whenever something befell them.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage argues that the circumstances of the modern era necessitate a new form of ijtihad. In the past, the abundance of recorded rulings justified closing individual ijtihad, but today's unprecedented advancements—flight, submarine travel, nuclear energy, global communication, space exploration, and economic integration—present novel issues that existing rulings do not directly address. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, proposes 'ijtihad al-jama'ah' (collective ijtihad) through consultative fiqh councils (majami' fiqhiyyah) composed of leading scholars from different schools and regions. This approach mirrors the shura (consultation) practiced by the Rightly Guided Caliphs. The goal is to derive rulings that meet contemporary needs from the rich Islamic legal tradition, which is inexhaustible. This passage highlights that the method of ijtihad must evolve while remaining rooted in the principles of the Shari'ah. It is a call for institutionalized, collaborative ijtihad rather than relying solely on individual scholars.

Key Terms
اجتهاد الجماعة (ijtihad al-jama'ah): Collective ijtihad performed by a group of qualified scholars through consultation (shura) in formal fiqh councils.

Key Takeaway
Collective ijtihad through shura-based councils is a legitimate and needed evolution of legal method to address modern complexities while staying rooted in the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

History and Development of Fiqh

Restricted Ijtihad: The Framework of Hanafi Scholarship (351-650 AH)
Lists features of the fifth era: restricted ijtihad, prolific authorship, and madhhab organization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of restricted ijtihad (ijtihad muqayyad) and identify its role in the production of Hanafi fiqh works and fatwa methodology.

Core Idea
Restricted ijtihad replaced absolute ijtihad — jurists worked within madhhab principles, not independently.

You have likely heard that ijtihad 'stopped' after the early centuries. This lesson shows that what actually changed was the type of ijtihad. From the 4th/10th century onward, Hanafi jurists practiced ijtihad muqayyad — reasoning within the school's principles. This allowed them to produce the enormous classical fiqh literature while remaining faithful to Abu Hanifa's legacy. Understanding this shift is essential for correctly assessing the authority of any Hanafi ruling.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish absolute vs restricted ijtihad
Absolute ijtihad (ijtihad mutlaq) derives rulings directly from Quran, Sunnah, ijma, and qiyas without being bound by a specific madhhab's framework. Restricted ijtihad (ijtihad muqayyad) operates within the usul and furu' of an established school. The passage states that restricted ijtihad replaced absolute ijtihad in this era.
Hanafi scholars of the fifth era still exercised reasoning, but their starting point was the madhhab's principles, not primary sources.
2
Understand how restricted ijtihad allows divergence from the imam
The passage notes that jurists could 'contradict their school's imam in some rulings' by using takhrij (extrapolation) from the same usul. This means a later Hanafi jurist could reach a different ruling from Abu Hanifa while still using Abu Hanifa's principles.
This is why we find multiple opinions within the Hanafi school — they are often the result of restricted ijtihad, not free ijtihad.
3
Recognize the scholarly output of the era
Despite the cessation of absolute ijtihad, the fifth era saw massive literary production: organizing madhahib, compiling scattered rulings, explaining the 'illah (reasoning) behind rulings, and deriving new cases (takhrij al-hawadith). This created the corpus we study today.
For Hanafis, most of our relied-upon texts (al-Hidayah, al-Mabsut, etc.) are products of this restricted-ijtihad phase.
4
Apply to understanding the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy
Restricted ijtihad forms the middle of the Hanafi hierarchy. Above it is absolute ijtihad (the founders: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). Below it is simple taqlid (following without reasoning). A mufti must be capable of restricted ijtihad to know which opinions are mufta bihi (operative) and how to apply them to new cases.
This hierarchy is crucial for fatwa: a mufti cannot issue a fatwa based on absolute ijtihad if a madhhab ruling exists; they must work within the school's framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤/١٤ - مزايا هذا الدور :
كان لكثير من فقهاء المذاهب في مرحلة هذا الدور اجتهاد مقيَّد محدود أي آراء فقهية قائمة على أصول المذهب الذي ينتمون إليه، قد يخالفون فيها إمام مذهبهم في بعض الأحكام مخالفة مستندة إلى التخريج على أصوله نفسها بنظر آخر. (ر: ٢/١٤).
- وقد حلَّ هذا الاجتهاد المقيد محل الاجتهاد المطلق الذي كان في طبقة أئمتهم.
·
وقد بلغ النتاج الفقهي في مدى هذا الدور وسابقه على أيدي كبار المؤلفين المذهبيين مبلغاً يجل عن التحديد والتصور.
-
وكانت في هذا الدور، رغم توقف الاجتهاد جهود كبرى في تنظيم تلك المذاهب وجمع شتاتها، ثم في تعليل مسائلها وتخريج الحوادث
(۱) انظر في هذا الموضوع المحاضرة التي كتبها ونشرها الأستاذ الكاتب المؤرخ الكبير رفيق بك العظم رحمه الله بعنوان «قضاء الجماعة وقضاء الفرد».

:
4/14 - Advantages of this Era: Many of the jurists of the schools in this stage had restricted, limited ijtihad, i.e., juristic opinions based on the principles of the school to which they belonged. They might contradict their school's imam in some rulings, a contradiction based on derivation from his own principles from another perspective. (Refer: 2/14). - And this restricted ijtihad replaced the absolute ijtihad that was in the layer of their imams. - And the juristic output in the scope of this era and the preceding one at the hands of great madhhab authors reached a level beyond description and imagination. - And in this era, despite the cessation of [absolute] ijtihad, there were great efforts in organizing those madhahib, gathering their scattered parts, then explaining the reasoning of their issues and deriving rulings for new events. (1) See on this topic the lecture written and published by the great historian writer Rafiq Bey al-Azm, may Allah have mercy on him, entitled 'Judgment of the Group and Judgment of the Individual'. 208:
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage describes the fifth era of Islamic fiqh development (351-650 AH). The key feature is the prevalence of restricted ijtihad (ijtihad muqayyad), where jurists worked within the principles of their established madhhab. This restricted ijtihad allowed them to differ from their imam's opinion in some cases by extrapolating new rulings from the same foundational principles. The passage notes that this restricted form replaced the absolute ijtihad of earlier imams. Despite the cessation of independent interpretive reasoning at the highest level, the era saw immense scholarly output—voluminous works compiling and organizing the legal schools, explaining their rulings, and applying their methods to new cases. The footnote references a lecture on 'collective judgment' (qada' al-jama'ah). For the Hanafi student, this explains the framework within which most classical Hanafi works were produced: they are exercises in restricted ijtihad, not necessarily independent reasoning from primary sources.

Key Terms
اجتهاد مقيَّد (ijtihād muqayyad): Reasoning within the boundaries of a particular madhhab's principles, as opposed to absolute ijtihad which derives directly from Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.
تخريج (takhrij): Extrapolating a ruling from the madhhab's principles for a new case, often resulting in an opinion that may differ from the founder's explicit view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What replaced absolute ijtihad during the fifth era (351-650 AH) according to the passage?
A) Direct citation of Quran and Sunnah
B) Restricted ijtihad based on madhhab principles
C) Taklid (blind following)
D) Individual reasoning without any method

Key Takeaway
Restricted ijtihad (ijtihad muqayyad) is the engine of Hanafi fiqh after the early centuries, enabling scholars to produce vast literature and adapt rulings while remaining faithful to the school's usul.

Fifth Era of Fiqh: Intra-Madhhab Expansion and the Science of Tarjih
Historical analysis of intra-madhhab fiqh expansion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the fifth era required tarjih and identify the roles of mukharrijun and muftis in intra-madhhab development.

Core Idea
Fiqh developed not only between madhhabs but within them, as conflicting riwayat forced scholars to weigh and prefer.

The fifth era (350-650 AH) marks a critical shift: after madhhabs were established, the focus turned inward. Scholars now had to resolve internal conflicts—multiple opinions from the same imam, and new incidents not directly covered. This lesson explores how tarjih and the work of mukharrijun shaped fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources of intra-madhhab conflict
From the passage, note that conflicts arose from differing transmissions (riwayat) from the imam of the school and differing opinions among the mukharrijun when they derived rulings for new incidents.
This is the root cause of the need for tarjih.
2
Define tarjih and its purpose
Tarjih is the process of preferring one opinion or transmission over another based on strength of evidence or authority. Its purpose, as stated in the passage, is to enable muftis and judges to rely on the strongest rulings.
Tarjih is not arbitrary; it follows established usul principles.
3
Recognize the role of mukharrijun
Mukharrijun are scholars who extrapolate rulings for new incidents from the principles of the madhhab. They contributed to the ramification of opinions within the school.
They are distinguished from the founders; they apply the madhhab's usul.
4
Connect to the emergence of fatwa books
The passage notes that fatwa books appeared in this era. These books compiled the preferred (mufta bihi) opinions after tarjih, providing practical guidance for muftis.
Fatwa books are a direct outcome of intra-madhhab development.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (١٤) الدور الخامس : ٣٥١ - ٦٥٠هـ الجديدة على أصولها وفي ترجيح الآراء والأقوال التي يختلف فيها ضمن المذاهب . أن - واتسع بذلك أيضاً علم أصول الفقه على أيدي كبار رجال تلك المذاهب. وكان اتساعه نتيجة لازمة لتلك التوسعات المذهبية التي يجد تستند إليه . - ومما يمتاز به هذا الدور تشعب الآراء الفقهية في كل مذهب بسبب اتساع دائرته واختلاف آراء المخرجين فيه لأحكام الحوادث، مضافاً ذلك إلى اختلاف الروايات في كثير من الموضوعات عن إمام المذهب نفسه؛ إذ قد يكون له في الموضوع الواحد عدة آراء منقولة مختلفة وهذا ما أدى إلى استمرار حركة الترجيح والتصحيح لبعض الآراء والروايات والتضعيف ،لبعضها، ليعتمد المفتون والقضاة على الأقوى منها. وقد ظهرت في هذا الدور كتب الفتاوى التي سنبحث عنها في الدور السادس.
Chapter (14) The Fifth Era: 351-650 AH. New [rulings] based on their principles, and in preferring opinions and sayings that differ within the schools. That - The science of usul al-fiqh also expanded at the hands of the great men of those schools. Its expansion was a necessary result of those school expansions that depend on it. - What distinguishes this era is the ramification of juristic opinions in each school due to the expansion of its scope and the differing opinions of the extrapolators [mukharrijun] regarding rulings of new incidents, in addition to the differing transmissions on many topics from the imam of the school itself; for he may have several transmitted different opinions on one subject. This led to the continuation of the movement of preference (tarjih) and correction of some opinions and transmissions and weakening of others, so that muftis and judges rely on the strongest among them. In this era, books of fatwas appeared, which we will discuss in the sixth era.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage describes the fifth era (350-650 AH) of Islamic jurisprudence development. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, highlights how this period witnessed the expansion of fiqh within each established madhhab. Scholars engaged in tarjih (preference) among conflicting opinions and riwayat, even those attributed to the same imam. This necessitated a systematic methodology for determining the strongest ruling, leading to the emergence of specialized fatwa books. The passage also notes the parallel expansion of usul al-fiqh as a discipline to support these madhhab developments. The tone is analytical, focusing on the internal dynamics of the madhhabs rather than inter-madhhab competition.

Key Terms
ترجيح (tarjih): The process of preferring one legal opinion or transmission over another based on strength of evidence or authority.
مخرجون (mukharrijun): Scholars who extrapolate rulings for new incidents from the principles of the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What led to the need for tarjih within each madhhab during the fifth era?
A) New Quranic revelations and prophetic traditions.
B) Conflicting transmissions from the imam of the school and differing opinions of the mukharrijun.
C) Political rulers imposing new laws.
D) The decline of usul al-fiqh as a discipline.

Key Takeaway
The fifth era transformed fiqh from following a single imam to a dynamic system of weighing and preferring within the madhhab, laying the foundation for fatwa literature.

Inter-Madhhab Debates and the Call for Mutual Respect
Critique of fanatical partisanship and call for tolerance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the error in the later scholars' condition for taqlid and articulate the Hanafi position on mutual respect among madhhabs.

Core Idea
True taqlid does not require believing others are wrong; ikhtilaf is a mercy, not a defect.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm critiques the later fanatical inter-madhhab debates and a shocking condition some later scholars placed on taqlid. We'll explore why that condition contradicts the spirit of the founding imams and what the proper attitude should be for a mufti today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Read the passage carefully and find the extreme condition that some later scholars imposed on taqlid. Write it down.
The condition is stated explicitly: the muqallid must believe his imam's madhhab is entirely correct and others are wrong.
2
Understand the root
According to al-Zarqa, what factors fueled this asabiyya madhhabiyya? Consider the temporal factors he mentions.
He attributes it to temporal factors that directed it opposite to the spirit of tolerance.
3
Contrast with the imams
How did the early imams (e.g., Abu Hanifa, Malik) view each other? The passage says they were known for mutual scientific appreciation.
This contrast highlights the deviation of later followers.
4
Recognize the value of ikhtilaf
Why does al-Zarqa consider ikhtilaf a 'great wealth' in fiqh? Relate it to the Shariah's capacity for various theories.
Ikhtilaf shows the flexibility and richness of Islamic jurisprudence.
5
Apply the lesson
As a mufti following the Hanafi school, how should you approach rulings from other madhhabs? Al-Zarqa calls for mutual appreciation.
Sincerity to the Shariah requires respecting valid differences.

٥/١٤ - المناظرات المذهبية : وكانت في هذا الدور منافسات ومناقشات ومناظرات واسعة بين رجال المذاهب استمرت إلى ما بعد ذلك، تسودها صبغة العصبية المذهبية أكثر من الغرض العلمي. وقد وصلت تلك المناظرات في العصور التالية لدى المتأخرين من رجال المذاهب إلى تشاحن بل تطاحن كريه وإن كانت قد خلفت لنا كثيراً من الطرائف الفقهية واللطائف العلمية الدقيقة التوليد. وكان المالكية أبعد من غيرهم عن معترك هذا الجدل المذهبي. وإن هذه العصبية المذهبية في فقه الأحكام لدى المتأخرين من أتباع المذاهب الأربعة كانت تغذيها عوامل زمنية قد وجهتها اتجاهاً معاكساً لروح التسامح والتقدير العلمي المتبادل المشهور بين أئمة تلك المذاهب أنفسهم. لقد وصل بعض رجال المذاهب فيما بعد إلى القول بأنَّ من شرائط التقليد المذهبي أن يعتقد المقلد بأن مذهب إمامه كله صواب يحتمل الخطأ، ومذهب غيره خطأ يحتمل الصواب!! مع أنّ تلك المذاهب الفقهية كلها تستمد استمداداً معتبراً من منابع الشريعة بأساليب صحيحة مختلفة مقبولة في فهم نصوصها والتخريج على قواعدها ويعد هذا الاختلاف ثروة عظيمة في الفقه الإسلامي ونظرياته كما سنرى في الفصل (۱۹) الآتي. وإنَّ الإخلاص للشريعة التي يظهر هذا الاختلاف المذهبي ما فيها من قابلية للخلود واتساع لشتى النظريات الفقهية يوجب على رجال كل مذهب أن يعتزوا بوجود المذهب المخالف، ويتبادلوا التقدير كما كان يفعل أئمة مذاهبهم من قبل. ٢١٠
5/14 - Inter-madhhab Debates: In this era, there were competitions, discussions, and extensive debates between the men of the schools, which continued afterwards, dominated by the character of madhhab partisanship more than scholarly purpose. These debates in later times among the later followers of the schools reached quarreling, even bitter fighting, although they left us many juristic anecdotes and precise scholarly subtleties of generation. The Malikis were farthest from the fray of this madhhab debate. This madhhab partisanship in fiqh rulings among the later followers of the four schools was fueled by temporal factors that directed it opposite to the spirit of tolerance and mutual scientific appreciation famous among the imams of those schools themselves. Some later madhhab figures even came to say that among the conditions of madhhab taqlid is that the muqallid believe that his imam's madhhab is entirely correct, subject to error, and the other madhhab is incorrect, subject to correctness!! Whereas all these fiqh schools derive in a valid manner from the sources of Shariah through different sound methods accepted in understanding its texts and extrapolation upon its rules. This difference is considered a great wealth in Islamic jurisprudence and its theories, as we will see in Chapter (19) below. Sincerity to the Shariah, which shows this madhhab difference as a quality of immortality and capacity for various juristic theories, requires the men of each school to take pride in the existence of the opposing school and exchange appreciation as the imams of their schools did before. 210.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage turns to inter-madhhab debates. The author notes that while earlier imams like Abu Hanifa and his contemporaries showed mutual respect, later followers engaged in bitter partisanship. He highlights the irony that some later scholars considered it a condition of taqlid to believe one's own madhhab is wholly correct and others are wrong, which contradicts the founders' attitudes. Al-Zarqa emphasizes that all four madhhabs derive from valid methods and that ikhtilaf is a wealth, not a weakness. He calls for mutual appreciation, tying it to sincerity to the Shariah. This is a strong critique of blind partisanship and a call for scholarly tolerance, very relevant for iftaa usul.

Key Terms
عصبية مذهبية (asabiyya madhhabiyya): Blind partisanship to one's madhhab, often leading to intolerance and conflict.
تقليد مذهبي (taqlid madhhabi): Following a particular madhhab in legal rulings, which the author distinguishes from fanatical conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) That the muqallid must believe his imam's madhhab is entirely correct and others are wrong
C) That taqlid is only for laypersons
D) That one must follow the majority opinion

Key Takeaway
Proper taqlid does not require believing others are invalid; ikhtilaf is a wealth to be respected, not a source of conflict.

Historical Development and Madhhab Diversity: Lessons from the Sixth Fiqh Period
Critiques fanatical taqlid and chronicles the sixth period's decline.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the correct Hanafi stance on madhhab diversity and identify the causes of fiqh decline in the sixth period.

Core Idea
Respect for other madhahib is a duty; blind taqlid and neglect of maqasid led to fiqh stagnation.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa presents two intertwined themes: first, the proper Islamic attitude toward madhhab difference as modeled by the early imams; second, the historical decline of fiqh when that attitude was lost. This lesson trains you to recognize the symptoms of intellectual stagnation and to uphold the spirit of ijtihad within taqlid.

Guided Path
1
Articulate the correct view of taqlid
What did the early imams practice? What did later extremists wrongly add? Identify the two contrasting views.
The passage refutes the claim that taqlid requires believing one's own madhhab is always right.
2
Explain the value of ikhtilaf
Why does the author consider madhhab differences a 'great wealth' for fiqh?
All schools derive from valid methods, and their differences expand legal theory.
3
Identify the time frame of the sixth period
When did the sixth fiqh period begin and end?
Mid-7th century to 1286 AH (appearance of the Majalla).
4
List the characteristics of decline
What specific changes marked the sixth period? Consider stagnation, rigidity, loss of maqasid, reliance on commentaries.
The shift from studying Quran/Sunnah to memorizing school books is a key indicator.
5
Draw a parallel to contemporary iftaa
How can a mufti today avoid the pitfalls described? Relate the lesson to modern fatwa practice.
A mufti should maintain awareness of maqasid and respect valid ikhtilaf.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث : أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته حتى التسامح والتقدير العلمي المتبادل المشهور بين أئمة تلك المذاهب أنفسهم. لقد وصل بعض رجال المذاهب فيما بعد إلى القول بأنَّ من شرائط التقليد المذهبي أن يعتقد المقلد بأن مذهب إمامه كله صواب يحتمل الخطأ، ومذهب غيره خطأ يحتمل الصواب!! مع أنّ تلك المذاهب الفقهية كلها تستمد استمداداً معتبراً من منابع الشريعة بأساليب صحيحة مختلفة مقبولة في فهم نصوصها والتخريج على قواعدها ويعد هذا الاختلاف ثروة عظيمة في الفقه الإسلامي ونظرياته كما سنرى في الفصل (۱۹) الآتي. وإنَّ الإخلاص للشريعة التي يظهر هذا الاختلاف المذهبي ما فيها من قابلية للخلود واتساع لشتى النظريات الفقهية يوجب على رجال كل مذهب أن يعتزوا بوجود المذهب المخالف، ويتبادلوا التقدير كما كان يفعل أئمة مذاهبهم من قبل. من الفصل الخامس عشر الدور الفقهي السادس منتصف القرن السابع إلى ظهور المجلة سنة ١٢٨٦ هـ ١/١٥ - في هذا الدور أخذ الفقه بالانحطاط. فقد بدأ في أوائله بالركود وانتهى في أواخره إلى الجمود، وإن كان في بحر هذا الدور الواسع قد بَرَز بعض الفحول اللامعين من الفقهاء والأصوليين. ففي هذا العصر سادَ الفكر التقليدي المغلق، وانصرفت الأفكار عن تلمس العلل والمقاصد الشرعية في فقه الأحكام إلى الحفظ الجاف، والاكتفاء بتقبل كل ما في الكتب المذهبية دون مناقشة. وطفق يتضاءل ويغيب ذلك النشاط الذي كان لحركة التخريج والترجيح والتنظيم في فقه المذاهب وأصبح مريد الفقه يدرس كتاب فقيه معين من رجال مذهبه فلا ينظر إلى الشريعة وفقهها إلا من خلال سطوره، بعد أن كان مريد الفقه قبلاً يدرس القرآن والسنة، وأصول الشرع ومقاصده، وأدلة الأحكام. وقد أصبحت المؤلفات الفقهية - إلا القليل - أواخر هذا العصر اختصاراً لما وجد من المؤلفات السابقة أو شرحاً له فانحصر العمل الفقهي في ترديد ما سبق، ودراسة ألفاظ وحفظها. وفي أواخر هذا الدور حلَّ الفكر العامي محل الفكر العلمي لدى كثير من متأخري رجال المذاهب الفقهية .
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics, including the tolerance and mutual scientific appreciation known among the imams of those schools themselves. Some later followers of the schools came to say that among the conditions of madhhab taqlid is that the muqallid believes that his imam's madhhab is entirely correct, possibly containing error, and that the madhhab of others is erroneous, possibly correct!! Even though all those fiqh schools derive their authority in a credible manner from the sources of Sharia through different correct methods accepted in understanding its texts and deriving rulings from its principles, and this difference is considered a great wealth in Islamic fiqh and its theories, as we will see in Chapter 19. And indeed, devotion to the Sharia, which shows this madhhab diversity as embodying a capacity for permanence and breadth for various fiqh theories, requires that the followers of each school take pride in the existence of the opposing school and exchange appreciation as the imams of their schools used to do. From Chapter Fifteen: The Sixth Fiqh Period: From the mid-seventh century to the appearance of the Majalla in 1286 AH. 1/15 - In this period, fiqh began to decline. It started at its beginning with stagnation and ended with rigidity, although in the vast sea of this period some brilliant scholars of fiqh and usul emerged. In this era, closed traditional thought prevailed, and minds turned away from seeking the causes and Sharia objectives in fiqh rulings to dry memorization and accepting everything in the school books without discussion. And that activity of derivation, preference, and organization in the fiqh of schools began to diminish and disappear. The student of fiqh would study the book of a particular jurist from his school and would not look at the Sharia and its fiqh except through its lines, whereas previously the student of fiqh would study the Quran and Sunnah, the principles of Sharia and its objectives, and the evidences of rulings. And fiqh writings - except for a few - became in this late period summaries of what existed in previous writings or commentaries on them, so that fiqh work was confined to repeating the past and studying words and memorizing them. And in the late part of this period, lay thought replaced scientific thought among many later followers of the fiqh schools.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm presents a critical historical analysis of the development of Islamic fiqh. It opens with a discussion of the third chapter (likely introductory) emphasizing that the early imams of the madhahib respected each other and recognized the validity of different methodological approaches. The author refutes the later extremist view that taqlid requires believing one's own madhhab is entirely correct and others are wrong. He affirms that all schools derive from the Sharia through accepted methods, and their differences represent a wealth of legal theory. The second part of the passage describes the sixth period (mid-7th to late 13th century AH) as a period of decline, where ijtihad gave way to rigid taqlid, memorization replaced understanding of 'illal and maqasid, and fiqh study became confined to summarizing and commenting on earlier works. This is a legal philosophy passage that warns against the dangers of blind adherence and emphasizes the importance of preserving the dynamic spirit of fiqh.

Key Terms
التقليد المذهبي (al-taqlīd al-madhhabī): The practice of following a particular madhhab without independent ijtihad; the author criticizes the notion that it requires believing one's own madhhab is entirely correct.
التخريج (al-takhrīj): The derivation of new rulings from existing principles within a madhhab; a scholarly activity that declined in the sixth period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was a characteristic of the sixth fiqh period (mid-7th to 13th century AH)?
A) Ijtihad and derivation of new rulings reached their peak.
B) Fiqh study focused on understanding Quran, Sunnah, and maqasid.
C) Fiqh writings were mostly summaries and commentaries on earlier works.
D) Scholars completely abandoned the study of fiqh.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi tradition teaches respect for madhhab diversity and warns against the stagnation that results from abandoning ijtihad and maqasid.

The Sixth Fiqh Period: Decline and the Rise of the Matn Method
Describes the decline of fiqh and emergence of the matn method in the sixth period.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key characteristics of the sixth fiqh period and explain how the matn method shaped later Hanafi scholarship.

Core Idea
The shift from direct engagement with primary sources to reliance on condensed matns marked a period of decline in fiqh methodology.

The history of fiqh did not end with the great imams; it entered a long period where the method of study itself changed. In the sixth period, from the mid-seventh century to the Ottoman Majalla, the dominant approach became the matn method—a style of authorship that prioritized brevity over clarity. In this lesson, we will explore how this shift affected the study and practice of Hanafi fiqh, recognizing both its losses and its lingering benefits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the decline indicators
From the text, list the signs of decline: stagnation, rigidity, closed traditional thinking, dry memorization, and reliance on secondary works.
Al-Zarqa uses words like 'rukuud', 'jumud', and 'taqlidi mughlaq' to describe the period.
2
Trace the cause of decline
The text states that students shifted from studying Quran and Sunnah to studying a single jurist's book. Identify how this move away from primary sources led to intellectual closure.
The method of takhrij and tarjih gave way to passive acceptance.
3
Describe the matn method
Define 'matn' based on the passage: a highly condensed abridgment that attempts to cover all topics in narrow phrases, often becoming obscure.
Al-Zarqa's metaphor: 'like one trying to contain a camel in a bottle.'
4
Analyze the layers of commentary
Following the matn, scholars added a sharh, then hawashi, then taqrirat. Understand how each layer built on the previous one and what it aimed to achieve.
The sharh clarifies, the hashiyah analyzes, the taqrirat annotates. But the core topic may get lost in verbal details.
5
Evaluate the consequences
Weigh the positive (analysis, verification) against the negative (verbalism, loss of maqasid). Conclude whether the matn method served or harmed fiqh.
Al-Zarqa acknowledges that the later works contain 'much analysis and verification', yet mixed 'the core with the husk'.

من الفصل الخامس عشر: الدور الفقهي السادس: منتصف القرن السابع إلى ظهور المجلة سنة ١٢٨٦ هـ ١/١٥ - في هذا الدور أخذ الفقه بالانحطاط. فقد بدأ في أوائله بالركود وانتهى في أواخره إلى الجمود، وإن كان في بحر هذا الدور الواسع قد بَرَز بعض الفحول اللامعين من الفقهاء والأصوليين. ففي هذا العصر سادَ الفكر التقليدي المغلق، وانصرفت الأفكار عن تلمس العلل والمقاصد الشرعية في فقه الأحكام إلى الحفظ الجاف، والاكتفاء بتقبل كل ما في الكتب المذهبية دون مناقشة. وطفق يتضاءل ويغيب ذلك النشاط الذي كان لحركة التخريج والترجيح والتنظيم في فقه المذاهب وأصبح مريد الفقه يدرس كتاب فقيه معين من رجال مذهبه فلا ينظر إلى الشريعة وفقهها إلا من خلال سطوره، بعد أن كان مريد الفقه قبلاً يدرس القرآن والسنة، وأصول الشرع ومقاصده، وأدلة الأحكام. وقد أصبحت المؤلفات الفقهية - إلا القليل - أواخر هذا العصر اختصاراً لما وجد من المؤلفات السابقة أو شرحاً له فانحصر العمل الفقهي في ترديد ما سبق، ودراسة ألفاظ وحفظها. وفي أواخر هذا الدور حلَّ الفكر العامي محل الفكر العلمي لدى كثير من متأخري رجال المذاهب الفقهية. ٢/١٥ - طريقة المتون: وقد شاعت نتيجة لذلك طريقة المتون في التأليف الفقهية وأصبحت هي الطريقة السائدة العامة، وحلّت كتب المتأخرين فيها محل كتب المتقدمين القيمة في الدراسة الفقهية. وطريقة المتون هذه يعمد فيها المتأخرون إلى وضع مختصرات يجمعون فيها أبواب العلم كلها في ألفاظ ضيقة يتبارون فيها بالإيجاز، حتى تصل إلى درجة المسح أو الألغاز، وتكاد كل كلمة أو جملة تشير إلى بحث واسع أو مسألة تفصيلية، كمن يحاول حصر الجمل في قارورة. ثم يعمد مؤلف المتن نفسه، أو سواه إلى وضع «شرح» على المتن لإيضاح عباراته، وبسط تفاصيل مسائله، والزيادة عليها. ثم توضع من قبل آخرين تعليقات على تلك الشروح تسمى «الحواشي». ثم توضع على تلك الحواشي ملاحظات تسمى «تقريرات». وتتضمن تلك الشروح والحواشي والتقريرات كثيراً من اللفظية في حل العبارات والألفاظ دون المقاصد الجوهرية في العلم وقد يضيع الموضوع الواحد أو يتشتت ما بين المتون والشروح والحواشي والتقريرات. ولا نعني بهذا خلو الحواشي من الفوائد العلمية، بل هي مشحونة بكثير من التحليل والتحقيق والتمحيص والمباحث ذات الشأن، ولكنها قد مزج فيها اللباب بالقشور، وعانى الفقه فيها سقم الطريقة.
From Chapter Fifteen: The Sixth Fiqh Period: Mid-Seventh Century to the Appearance of the Majalla in 1286 AH. 1/15 - In this period, fiqh began to decline. It started with stagnation at its beginning and ended with rigidity at its end, although in the vast sea of this period some brilliant scholars of fiqh and usul emerged. In this era, closed traditional thinking prevailed, and minds turned away from seeking legal causes (ilal) and shari'i objectives in the fiqh of rulings toward dry memorization and accepting everything in the madhhab books without discussion. The activity of takhrij, tarjih, and organization in the fiqh of the schools gradually diminished and disappeared. The seeker of fiqh began to study the book of a specific jurist from his madhhab and would not look at the Shari'ah and its fiqh except through its lines, whereas previously the seeker of fiqh used to study the Qur'an and Sunnah, the principles of the Shari'ah and its objectives, and the evidences of rulings. In the latter part of this period, fiqh works - except for a few - became summaries of or commentaries on previous works. Thus fiqh work was confined to repeating what came before, studying words and memorizing them. At the end of this period, lay thinking replaced scientific thinking among many of the later followers of the fiqh schools. 2/15 - The Matn Method: As a result, the matn method became widespread in fiqh authorship and became the prevailing general method. The books of the later scholars replaced the valuable books of the earlier ones in fiqh study. In this matn method, later scholars would produce abridgments in which they gather all the chapters of a science in narrow phrases, competing in brevity to the point of becoming puzzles or riddles, where almost every word or sentence points to a broad discussion or a detailed issue, like one trying to contain a camel in a bottle. Then the author of the matn himself or someone else would write a 'sharh' (commentary) on the matn to clarify its expressions, expand its detailed issues, and add to them. Then others would write glosses on these commentaries called 'hawashi'. Then on these glosses, notes called 'taqrirat' would be written. These commentaries, glosses, and taqrirat contain much verbalism in solving expressions and words without the substantive objectives of the science, and a single topic may get lost or scattered among the matn, commentaries, glosses, and taqrirat. We do not mean by this that the glosses are devoid of scholarly benefits; rather, they are filled with a lot of analysis, verification, scrutiny, and significant discussions, but in them the core is mixed with the husk, and fiqh suffered from the sickness of method.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage describes the sixth period of Islamic jurisprudence (mid-7th century AH to 1286 AH, when the Ottoman Majalla was promulgated). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, characterizes this era as one of decline (inhitat): it began with stagnation (rukuud) and ended with rigidity (jumud). The active juristic spirit of earlier times—where scholars engaged in takhrij (deriving rulings from principles), tarjih (preferring one opinion over another), and organizing fiqh—faded. Instead, the dominant intellectual approach became closed traditionalism: students merely memorized and accepted whatever was in the madhhab books without critical discussion. Fiqh study shifted from engaging directly with the Qur'an, Sunnah, and legal principles to relying exclusively on the works of a single later scholar. Most new books were either abridgments (mukhtasarat) or commentaries on earlier works. The author identifies the 'matn method' as the culmination of this trend. A 'matn' is a highly compact abridgment of all the topics of a science, often so concise that it becomes obscure. Then, to explain the matn, a 'sharh' is written; then glosses (hawashi) on the sharh; then taqrirat on the hawashi. This multiplication of layers often led to a focus on verbal elaboration rather than substantive understanding, causing topics to become fragmented. However, al-Zarqa notes that these later works were not without value—they contained much analysis and verification—but overall, the methodology suffered: 'the core was mixed with the husk.'

Key Terms
متن (matn): A highly abbreviated summary of a fiqh work that aims to pack all topics concisely, often at the expense of clarity.
شرح (sharh): A commentary on a matn, intended to unpack its condensed phrases, elaborate on its rulings, and add details.
حاشية (hashiyah): A gloss or marginal commentary on a sharh, often providing further clarifications, cross-references, and analyses.
تقريرات (taqrirat): Notes or annotations on hawashi, often by later scholars to explain or correct the glosses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What replaced direct study of the primary sources (Qur'an and Sunnah) in the sixth fiqh period?
A) Study of a specific jurist's book from the madhhab
B) Memorization of hadith collections
C) Focus on usul al-fiqh only
D) Comparative fiqh across schools

Key Takeaway
The sixth fiqh period saw a shift from engaging with the primary sources to relying on condensed matns and their commentaries, which led to a decline in dynamic juristic thought but also produced rich, albeit layered, scholarship.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
The Matn Method in Hanafi Fiqh: From Pedagogical Tool to Complex Tradition
Historical and methodological analysis of matn authorship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical evolution of the matn method in Hanafi fiqh and evaluate its impact on fiqh authorship.

Core Idea
The matn method began as a simple pedagogical tool for beginners but later became a complex, often cryptic tradition that stifled innovation.

This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa traces the origins and transformation of the matn (concise text) method in Hanafi fiqh authorship. It highlights how later scholars turned simple summaries into a dominant but problematic approach, mixing valuable analysis with verbosity and obscuring the essence of fiqh. Understanding this evolution helps a mufti appreciate the historical context of the texts he uses and the importance of returning to original sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original purpose of the matn method
Look for the passage that describes the initial pedagogical goal: to provide simple, easy texts for beginners, similar to al-Ajrumiyyah in grammar.
The author explicitly states that the early matn were meant to collect basic topics in easy wording for beginners (shada).
2
Describe the later development and criticism
Note how the method became widespread among later scholars (mutakkhirin), with extremely concise, puzzle-like texts that required extensive commentaries (shuruh), glosses (hawashi), and superglosses (taqrirat).
The author criticizes that these later texts often lost the essential purposes and scattered topics across multiple layers of commentary.
3
Examine the author's critique of the method
Understand that while commentaries contain valuable analysis, they mix core with chaff, and the focus on verbal analysis obscures substantive fiqh.
The author uses the phrase 'mixed the core with the chaff' to describe the later works.
4
Identify the positive example of innovation
The author cites Imam al-Kasani's Bada'i' al-Sana'i' as a reformer who reorganized fiqh thematically, breaking away from the matn tradition.
Al-Kasani is praised for his creative style and thematic arrangement without following the matn model.

٢/١٥ - طريقة المتون :

وطريقة المتون هذه يعمد فيها المتأخرون إلى وضع مختصرات يجمعون فيها أبواب العلم كلها في ألفاظ ضيقة يتبارون فيها بالإيجاز، حتى تصل إلى درجة المسح أو الألغاز، وتكاد كل كلمة أو جملة تشير إلى بحث واسع أو مسألة تفصيلية، كمن يحاول حصر الجمل في قارورة هذا المختصر متنا» (۱). ويسمى

المناقشات
ولا نعني بهذا خلو الحواشي من الفوائد العلمية، بل هي مشحونة بكثير من التحليل والتحقيق والتمحيص والمباحث ذات الشأن، ولكنها قد مزج فيها اللباب بالقشور، وعانى الفقه فيها سقم الطريقة.
(۱) المتن (بفتح فسكون أصل معناه في اللغة ما يكون في جانب صلب الحيوان من لحم وعصب، فللظهر متنان عن جانبي العمود الفقري. وقد يطلق المتن في الاستعمال على الظهر كله. (ر: المصباح المنير). وقد سموا به في الاصطلاح هذه المختصرات العلمية لأنها تتضمن المسائل الأساسية التي يحمل عليها ،غيرها، كما أن الظهر أساس للركوب والحمل.

الفصل (١٥) الدور السادس: ٦٥١ - ١٢٨٦هـ
٣/١٥ - وتاريخ المتون في الحقيقة يرجع إلى أقدم من هذا العصر. ولكنها عندما ظهرت قديماً كان الغرض منها حكيماً: وهو جمع المسائل الأولية البسيطة في متون صغيرة بعبارة سهلة لتكون مبادىء لشداة (۱) الفقه على نحو الآجرومية» (۲) في علم النحو. وهذا الغرض يقضي بأن يبقى المتن مختصراً بسيطاً سهلاً للمسائل الأولية مقصوراً عليها للغاية التعليمية، فلا توضع عليه الشروح الواسعة ولا الحواشي المعقدة
ولكن المتون انقلبت في العصر المبحوث عنه إلى طريقة عامة تعقيدية في تأليف الفقه؛ حتى أن [؟] أن يترك له أثراً وذكراً علمياً لا يفكر أن من يريد [؟] يخدم العلم بمؤلف مستقل يعمد به إلى التجديد في أسلوب الفقه ولغته، وفي تنقيحه وتقسيمه، وترتيبه وتبويبه والرجوع بمسائله المشتتة في غير أبوابها إلى أبوابها ومناسباتها فيضيف إلى جهود المتقدمين الحميدة جهوداً (۳) ؛ بل كان كل مؤلف متأخر يحصر جهده في وضع حاشية على شرح، أو شرح على متن معقد، أو يضع متناً على نسق سائر المتون الاختزالية اللغزية التي تقدمته .
جديدة
ومن هنا نرى أنَّ أن طريقة المتون بدأت قديماً معقولة الفكرة والغرض لوضع مبادىء العلم البسيطة تسهيلاً على المبتدئين، ثم أصبحت بالعوامل التقليدية على أيدي المتأخرين أشواكاً وتعاريج توعر بها طريق الفقه،
(۱) الشادي: المبتدىء الذي يأخذ طَرَفاً من العلم والأدب جمعه شداة، كقاض وقضاة، وأصله في اللغة: من يقتطع قطعة من الإبل ويسوقها (ر: المصباح والقاموس). (۲) الآجرومية مقدّمة في النحو، لمؤلفها محمد المشهور بابن آجروم أي الفقير الصوفي بلسان البربر (ر: فتح رب الأرباب» للشيخ عباس رضوان). (۳) تذكر بهذه المناسبة أنَّ من مفاخر المؤلفين في الفقه الحنفي الإمام علاء الدين الكاساني المتوفى سنة ٥٨٧ هـ. الذي وضع كتابه بدائع الصنائع في ترتيب الشرائع توسيعاً لكتاب تحفة الفقهاء» الشيخه علاء الدين السمرقندي، فقد نثر الكاساني في (بدائعه) فقه المذهب من الكتب ثم صاغه صياغة جديدة في سبعة مجلدات لا على مثال سبق، بأسلوب وترتيب موضوعي معجب فكان من المجددين حقاً في تدوين الفقه
۲۱۳
Chapter Three: Stages of the Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics
2/15 - The Method of Matn:
As a result, the method of matn (concise texts) in jurisprudential authorship became widespread and became the general dominant method, and the books of the later scholars replaced the valuable books of the earlier ones in jurisprudential study.
In this method of matn, the later scholars undertake to produce summaries in which they gather all the chapters of the science in narrow expressions, competing in conciseness, until it reaches the level of obliteration or puzzles, and almost every word or phrase refers to a broad discussion or a detailed issue, like one who tries to confine sentences in a bottle. This summary is called a 'matn' (1).
Then the author of the matn himself, or someone else, undertakes to write a 'sharh' (commentary) on the matn to clarify its expressions, elaborate the details of its issues, and add to them. Then others place comments on those sharhs called 'hawashi' (glosses). Then notes are placed on those glosses called 'taqrirat' (superglosses). These sharhs, hawashi, and taqrirat contain a lot of verbosity in analyzing expressions and words without the essential purposes of the science, and the same topic may be lost or scattered among the matn, sharhs, hawashi, and taqrirat.
Discussions
We do not mean by this that the hawashi are devoid of scholarly benefits; rather, they are filled with much analysis, verification, scrutiny, and important discussions, but in them the core is mixed with the chaff, and fiqh suffered from the sickness of the method.
(1) Al-matn (with a fatha then sukun): its original meaning in language is what is in the solid side of an animal of flesh and sinew, so the back has two matns on the sides of the spine. It may also be used metaphorically to refer to the entire back. (See: al-Misbah al-Munir). Terminologically, they named these scholarly summaries matn because they contain the basic issues upon which others are carried, just as the back is the basis for riding and carrying.
:
212

Chapter (15) The Sixth Period: 651-1286 AH
3/15 - The history of matn in fact goes back earlier than this era. But when they appeared in the past, their purpose was wise: to collect simple, basic topics in small texts with easy wording to serve as principles for beginners (shada) (1) of fiqh, similar to al-Ajrumiyyah (2) in grammar. This purpose requires that the matn remain concise, simple, easy for basic topics, confined to them for educational purposes, so that extensive commentaries and complex glosses are not placed upon it.
But the matn turned in the period under discussion into a generally complicated method in the authorship of fiqh, to the extent that [؟] one who wants to leave an impact and scholarly remembrance does not think about serving the science through an independent work [؟] that brings innovation in the style and language of fiqh, and in its revision, division, arrangement, chaptering, and returning its scattered issues from outside their chapters to their proper chapters and contexts, thus adding new efforts to the praiseworthy efforts of the predecessors (3). Rather, every later author confined his effort to writing a gloss on a commentary, or a commentary on a complex matn, or producing a matn on the pattern of the preceding reductionist and cryptic matns.
new
Hence we see that the matn method began in the past with a reasonable idea and purpose for placing simple principles of the science to facilitate beginners, then it became, through traditional factors at the hands of later scholars, thorns and twists that made the path of fiqh rugged.
(1) Al-shadi: the beginner who takes a part of knowledge and literature; its plural is shada, like qadi and qudat. Its origin in language: one who cuts off a piece of camels and drives them (see: al-Misbah and al-Qamoos). (2) Al-Ajrumiyyah: an introduction to grammar, by its author Muhammad known as Ibn Ajurrum, meaning the poor Sufi in the Berber language (see: Fath Rabb al-Arbab by Shaykh Abbas Radwan). (3) On this occasion it is mentioned that among the distinguished authors in Hanafi fiqh is Imam Ala al-Din al-Kasani (d. 587 AH), who wrote his book Bada'i' al-Sana'i' fi Tartib al-Shara'i' as an expansion of the book Tuhfat al-Fuqaha' by his shaykh Ala al-Din al-Samarqandi. Al-Kasani disseminated in his Bada'i' the fiqh of the madhhab from the books and then formulated it anew in seven volumes without any previous model, with an admirable style and thematic arrangement, and he was truly among the renewers in the codification of fiqh.
213
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a historical and methodological analysis of the 'matn' (concise text) method in Hanafi fiqh authorship. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, describes how this method became dominant among later scholars (mutakkhirin). He explains that these scholars produced extremely concise summaries that were often cryptic, requiring commentaries (shuruh), glosses (hawashi), and superglosses (taqrirat). While these later texts contained valuable analysis, they also mixed important content with trivial details, and the focus on verbal analysis often obscured the essence of fiqh. The author then traces the origin of the matn method: initially, it was a wise pedagogical tool to provide simple introductory texts for beginners, like al-Ajrumiyyah in grammar. However, in the later period, it became a complex and rigid tradition that discouraged innovation. As a positive example, he praises Imam al-Kasani's 'Bada'i' al-Sana'i'' for its creative reorganization of Hanafi fiqh. The passage underscores the tension between pedagogical utility and scholarly tradition, and critiques the later trend of producing derivative works rather than original, systematic compositions.

Key Terms
المتن (al-matn): A concise summary of fiqh topics, often with extremely terse wording, used as a base text for commentaries and glosses.
الشرح (al-sharh): A commentary on a matn, intended to clarify its expressions and expand on its issues.
الحاشية (al-hashiyah): A gloss or marginal annotation on a sharh, providing additional analysis and verification.
التقرير (al-taqrir): A supergloss or notes on a hashiah, often representing the final layer of commentary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the original purpose of the matn method?
A) To provide comprehensive legal rulings for advanced scholars
B) To create introductory texts for beginners with easy wording
C) To replace all earlier books with concise summaries
D) To produce complex glosses that require further commentary

Key Takeaway
The matn method evolved from a simple educational tool to a complex tradition that mixed valuable analysis with verbosity, highlighting the need for critical engagement with later texts.
